Sustainable Particleboards: Renewable Building Materials from Agricultural and Forestry By-products
Andrea Wechsler Pizarro
A thesis in fulfilment of the requirements for the degree of Doctor of Philosophy
Faculty of Built Environment October 2013

ABSTRACT
The aim and significance of this research is to develop more sustainable options to standard particleboards by applying a life cycle approach. This research brings together and applies principles and frameworks from industrial ecology. The focus of the research is on the development of sustainable composites based on Australian agricultural and forestry by-products, particularly macadamia shells, pine cones and Eucalyptus capsules, bonded with non-toxic, renewable or recycled environmentally friendly resins. These resins were castor oil-based polyurethane and recycled polypropylene.
The first part of the research consisted of a study of appropriate fillers and resins for alternative sustainable particleboards and an assessment of the life cycle environmental impacts of standard particleboards. The second stage explored the feasibility of using the proposed fillers combined with the selected resins. These composites were compared with reference panels based on radiata pine wood. The studied composites were assessed by testing their physical and mechanical properties. Microstructural analysis was also performed. The particleboards with castor oil-based polyurethane were further analysed for their level of formaldehyde emissions.
All proposed composites demonstrated exceptionally good physical properties, with results up to 92% lower thickness swelling for the new proposed composites compared with standard particleboards. Composite materials based in castor oil polyurethane recorded up to 360% greater internal bond strength than traditional particleboards, and formaldehyde emissions were 99% lower than those of standard particleboards. The flexural properties of the new proposed composites were, however, 36% - 68% lower than the current practice but still acceptable for use in non-structural applications.
The results of the analysis of the samples filled with macadamia shells, pine cones and Eucalyptus capsules bonded with recycled polypropylene and castor oil polyurethane showed that the alternative fibres and resins considered in this study have acceptable properties for use in composites. These new materials were able to ameliorate the identified environmental impacts of standard particleboards and resulted in valuable new panel applications in the built environment, particularly in high moisture environments.
vii

CHAPTER 1: INTRODUCTION
The aim of this research is to develop more sustainable options to standard particleboards existing in the market by applying a life cycle approach. It specifically investigates the applicability of Australian agricultural and forestry by-products, particularly macadamia shells, pine cones and Eucalyptus capsules, bonded with different non-toxic, renewable or recycled resins. This study explores potential solutions for the problems presented by standard particleboards in high moisture environments. The research is significant because it brings together principles and frameworks for the sustainable production and consumption of building materials, and applies it to develop more sustainable alternatives to standard particleboards. This chapter introduces the theories in which this PhD research is based, followed by a discussion of the main environmental problems faced by standard particleboards, the scope of the study, research questions, objectives, and the structure of the thesis.
1.1. SUSTAINABILITY IN THE BUILT ENVIRONMENT
Ecosystems are in decline due to the impacts of economic development based on overconsumption of natural resources and the use of natural systems as a sink for wastes (UNEP, 2012). This overconsumption of natural resources and the use of natural systems as a sink for wastes greatly affects the viability of both renewable and non- renewable resources, by the risk of depletion of these common resources (Hardin, 1968).
Despite the fact that forests and crude oil are two scarce resources that are particularly critical to preserve, the way they are used in the built environment contributes to negative environmental impacts rather than beneficial ones. Due to increasing urbanization, there is an unprecedented demand for building products and an urgent need to develop alternatives to currently unsustainable practices.
Alternative approaches to the production and consumption of materials have been proposed to address this problem. For example, the United Nations Environmental Program (UNEP) developed a general policy framework called ‘Sustainable
Consumption and Production’ (SCP) (2012). This framework has been informed by approaches to industrial production and consumption such as industrial ecology Industrial ecology has been defined as ‘the science of sustainable development’ (Graedel and Allenby, 1995). Although industrial ecology is documented as originating in the early seventies in Japan (Erkman, 1997; Watanabe, 1972; Billen et al., 1983; Winter et al., 1988), it was brought to the occidental world by Frosch and Gallopoulos (1989) in the late 80s. Industrial ecology is a multidisciplinary field of research that combines aspects of engineering, economics, sociology, toxicology and the natural sciences. It studies material and energy flows through industrial systems through the whole life cycle of a product with particular concern about the impacts that industrial activities have on the environment. This concept, sometimes referred to as ‘industrial metabolism’, can be utilized to follow material and energy flows, transformations, and dissipation in the industrial system as well as into natural systems (Garner and Keoleian, 1995).
Inspired by the first Rio Earth Summit in 1992, the building sector began to address unsustainable production and construction issues. The first International conference on Sustainable Construction held in Tampa Florida (Kibert, 1994) discussed how the building industry should respond to the challenge of sustainable development. At the same time the life-cycle approach to development was gaining legitimacy (ISO, 2007) and a number of principles for making sustainable material choices were subsequently developed. Examples of these are the United States Green Building Council’s LEED system (USGBC LEED) (2005), the environmental performance rating schemes for completed buildings under the UK Building Research Establishment’s BREAM programme (Baldwin, 1996) and the supporting life cycle assessment of the construction sector by the ATHENA group (2013). In Australia, Good environmental choice Australia (GECA) ecolabels have been specifying environmental performance requirements for building materials and manufactured goods, framed in the Australian Ecolabel Program (2012).
Influenced by concepts of industrial ecology, improved holistic frameworks for material selection and building design were proposed in such works as ‘Construction Ecology’ (Kibert et al., 2002) and ‘Building Ecology’ (Graham, 2003). Both authors apply
analogies from behaviour found in ecosystems to industrial systems. In natural systems, materials function in a closed loop where no resource is wasted. These analogies integrate industrial and natural systems to enhance the use of resources in the built environment (Kibert et al., 2002; Ayres, 1989; Kneese et al., 1970). Both publications promote the practice of life-cycle thinking in the built environment which includes consideration of the whole system and effects over time.
Some of the strategies used to meet the environmental requirements of industrial ecology are to advocate for more durable products and materials, to encourage appropriate material selection, to reduce material intensity and adopt an efficient process management (Garner and Keoleian, 1995). In order to promote material life extension, industrial ecology recommends specifying recycled materials and as a last resort, to use recyclable materials if new items do have to be used. The principle involved is effectively turning waste into the food for other processes (McDonough and Braungart, 2002). An appropriate material selection suggests substituting the prime sources comprising the materials for non-toxic and biodegradable ones. Strategies to reduce material intensity is to encourage the use of renewable resources while process management suggests increasing energy efficiency (Keoleian et al., 1995), promoting locality in solutions in which short distances between industries is an advantage (Grann, 1997; Garner and Keoleian, 1995).
According to Odum & Odum (1976; 1996), all natural systems build order, develop feedbacks of energy and recycle materials. These reinforcing systems are called ‘surviving designs’. They can sustain themselves using the outputs of consumption as nourishment for the production of the resources they need to survive, while minimising waste (Odum, 1996). In contrast a ‘non-surviving system design’ is linear and does not reinforce production with the outputs of consumption. A non-surviving system is an example of the consumption pattern of our contemporary society. Frameworks such as industrial ecology introduce a surviving system pattern to production and consumption (Figure 1.1).
While there has been much work done to develop and refine more sustainable principles and frameworks for the sustainable production and consumption of building materials
there have been far fewer attempts to actually apply such principles. This research contributes to taking practical steps and applies principles from SCP and industrial ecology to the development of sustainable particleboards. Industrial ecology theory is used in this research as a foundation for the use of by-products resulting from production in the agricultural and forestry industries. This research considers their reutilization in a new production system of improved particleboards from a life cycle perspective.
 Figure 1.1: a. Non-surviving system design b. Surviving system design (Odum, 1996)
Therefore, the performance criteria drawn from the industrial ecology framework to meet the requirements of environmental sustainability are to use local materials, to use wastes as resources, to maintain biodiversity, to eliminate toxic materials and by- products, to increase energy efficiency, to extend the useful life of products, and to use recyclable or biodegradable materials (Frosch and Gallopoulos, 1989; McDonough and Braungart, 2002; Garner and Keoleian, 1995; Kibert et al., 2002; Graham, 2003; Ayres, 1989; Kneese et al., 1970; Keoleian et al., 1995; Grann, 1997; Nattrass and Brian, 1999; Robèrt, 2002).
An overview of the whole thesis is presented in Figure 1.2 and further explained in the following sections. The research diagram of the thesis displays the research questions on the left side, with the key findings of each research sub-question presented in the green, red, yellow and blue rectangles, in the centre of the diagram. The chapters in which these finding are presented can be seen on the right side of the Figure. The middle part of the figure is divided into three colours: the green squares are related with the fibres, the blue squares relate to the adhesives and the red squares relate to the manufacturing processes. Further on, the elements framed in a yellow box are related to the castor oil-based polyurethane, and the elements framed inside the grey box are related to the recycled polypropylene matrix.

1.2. WOOD-BASED COMPOSITE MATERIALS
A wood-based composite material is defined as any lignocellulosic material bonded together with a resin. The most common wood-based composite material used in interior applications are particleboards, which are glued with up to 10%-15% urea formaldehyde adhesive (UF) (Stark et al., 2010). Other types of wood-based composites are wood and vegetable fibre composites, which contain up to 60% wood elements contained in a thermoplastic, thermoset or elastomeric matrix. One example of these composites is a wood-plastic composite (Stark et al., 2010; Maloney, 1986).
Based in the previous definitions, this research uses the term ‘particleboard’ for any lignocellulosic-based composites bonded with 20% or less adhesive. The term ‘adhesive’ refers to an thermoset resin used to glue the particles together. The term ‘composites’ is used in this research for all other lignocellulosic-based composites bonded with 40% matrix. The matrix used to bond the ‘composites’ refers to an thermoplastic or elastomeric resin. Therefore, in this research, the difference in the term is given by the amount and type of resin used to bond the lignocellulosic particles.
Standard particleboard is a widely used building material. They are intended for interior applications in flat furniture or in panelling applications such as partition walls (Stark et al., 2010). Worldwide particleboard production for 2009 was 92,010,711 m3 (FAO, 2012), whilst the average annual production in Australia during the years 2003-09 was around 980,000 m3 (Pink, 2012; Pink, 2010). The main particleboards producers in Australia are Laminex with the product Particleboard Standard (STD) (Chemwatch, 2011; Ecospecifier global, 2013) and Carter Holt Harvey, with the product Pyneboard (CarterHoltHarvey, 2012).
Standard particleboards’ negative environmental effects along its life-cycle are due to two main factors: first, their prime source relies on pine wood fibres from monoculture plantations (Magin, 2002) and second, carcinogenic oil-based UF adhesives are used (Marutzky, 1989; EPA, 2011) which release emissions during the manufacturing process (Rivela et al., 2006; Wilson, 2010). The main environmental burdens were shown in Figure 1.2 and are discussed next.
Most of the wood used in particleboard production derives from monocrop plantations. This type of plantation carries a range of environmental risks, such as reduced soil fertility, increased erosion and compaction of soil and reduced groundwater and stream flow which consequently increases fire risks. Monocrop plantations can create an environment that is hostile to many species, particularly due to the high utilization of long lasting herbicides, affecting all the flora and fauna around them (Graham, 2003; Achard, 2009; Calkins, 2009; Plantations 2020, 2003; Magin, 2002). In addition, sub- optimal use of tree is another important issue, with only 50% of a tree used as timber or recovered (Magin, 2002; Leckoundzou et al., 2010). More efficient utilization would result in fewer trees being required for building products.
The second issue is related to the oil based UF adhesive. Petroleum-based polymers have been used for commodities since the Second World War due to their durability and low cost. In the last 25 years there have been significant rises in the price of oil (UNEP, 2012) due to the extraction of the most accessible and profitable sources several decades ago. It is now necessary to drill deeper and further into the earth’s crust to extract the oil, thus raising costs (Kibert et al., 2002; Odum and Odum, 1976). This situation has affected the price of oil derivatives including constituents of formaldehyde based resins (Van Langenberg et al., 2010). In addition, the carbon dioxide released in the processes of particleboard manufacture contributes to global warming and poor air quality (Wescott and Frihart, 2004). Furthermore, UF adhesives break down when exposed to humidity. As a consequence of the hygroscopic nature of radiata pine wood, and the non-resistance of UF against moisture, the physical properties of particleboards are quite deficient in high moisture environments, reducing their life span considerably (Chemwatch, 2011; Dunky, 2003; Ebewele et al., 1993; Frihart, 2005; Pizzi, 2003; Marutzky, 1989). Finally, significant environmental impact from the use of UF adhesives relates to the CO2 and formaldehyde emissions released during the particleboard manufacturing processes involving wood drying and hot plate pressing (Rivela et al., 2006; Wilson, 2010).
More sustainable alternatives must be developed to address these problems, through low temperature curing and the utilization of moisture resistant, non-toxic and non-oil based resins, in addition to exploring the potential of underused local fibres.
1.3. AGRICULTURAL AND FORESTRY BY-PRODUCTS IN AUSTRALIA
Australia has a vast production of macadamias (Macadamia integrifolia and macadamia tetraphylla), radiata pine (Pinus radiata D. Don) and a large variety of Eucalyptus wood. The Australian macadamia nut industry generates as much as 28,000 tons of macadamia shells annually (Australian Macadamia Society, 2010). Vast quantities of cones and capsules are likewise generated as by-products of the seed production industries, with one pine seed company in Australia producing as many as 100 to 150 m3 of cones annually (Cameron, 2009). Currently, these by-products tend to be used for low-value applications such as garden mulch, Christmas decorations or as boiler fuel.
While these low value uses mean that direct disposal in a landfill is avoided, which is financially and practically prohibitive due to the sheer volume, it is nonetheless a poor utilization of a potentially valuable resource. These available local by-products from the forest grower regions of Australia are dry after the primary process and highly resistant to the local weather. Consequently, they present an opportunity as prime materials for the manufacture of particleboards, specifically for applications where moisture is present since it implies problems for standard particleboards, as presented earlier in Figure 1.2.
1.4. SCOPE OF THE STUDY
This research is based on a “surviving s stem design”, as explained above in Odum’s (1996) concepts and the ideas embedded in industrial ecology and illustrated in Figure 1.1. It targets the wastes of other processes to feed the production of particleboards. Figure 1.3 below displays the proposed system for the manufacture of particleboards based on agricultural and forestry by-products in Australia. The figures in this diagram, as explained in the legend, represent the relationships between the production, consumption and by-products of the industrial process involving macadamia nuts, pine and Eucalyptus seed, and their reutilization in a new production process. Figure 1.3 does not illustrate the EMERGY flows and transformities as used by Odum in his studies (1996).
After being cultivated, collected and dried, the cones and capsules are heated to release their seeds while the macadamia shells are cracked in order to remove the kernel from the shell. The macadamia kernels are processed into different products and delivered to the consumers. The pods of the seeds are considered waste in a traditional non- surviving system, to be burnt or disposed of in a landfill. The new system, based on the surviving-system design, proposes using the by-products to feed a new cycle, and use the shells, cones and capsules to manufacture particleboards instead of using wood particles originating from plantations. The new process would involve: the transportation of the pods from the production sites to the particleboard manufacturer, followed by grinding and mixing the by-products with a renewable or recycled resin and then pressing, cooling and trimming the final product as the new proposed panels.
 Figure 1.3 Schematic diagram of macadamia shells, Pine cones and Eucalyptus capsules production and to its use for particle board production. Author.
1.5. PROBLEM STATEMENT
The analysis of the problems and issues raised above allows the following statement to be made:
Current wood-based composite materials used in the built environment carry important environmental side effects, caused by the linear nature of industrial systems were raw materials are used and wastes are generated. These main environmental impacts are associated with the origin of the fibres, with the toxicity of the adhesive, with the emissions released during the manufacturing process, and with poor performance in high moisture environments. The cause of the impacts needs to be further analysed and more sustainable alternatives proposed, based on a circular system design.
1.6. AIM AND OBJECTIVES
The overall aim of this research is to apply a life cycle approach to develop more sustainable alternative wood-based composite materials than the current standard particleboard used in interior panelling applications. To achieve this main aim, the following secondary objectives are stated:
· To outline the most relevant environmental impacts of standard particleboards.
· To identify the most appropriate agricultural and forestry by-products in Australia
which can be used as a substitute for pine wood in particleboards.
· To identify the most appropriate recycled or renewable binders to be used as a substitute for UF in particleboards.
· To manufacture the composites and particleboards with the proposed alternative constituents.
· To evaluate if the physical and mechanical performance of the new proposed composites and particleboards are comparable with current practice.
· To assess if the proposed composites are more sustainable than the current practice.
1.7. RESEARCH QUESTION
The main research question rose from the environmental burdens of standard particleboards and the quest for sustainability framework. The main research question is:
Is it possible to develop more sustainable particleboards, targeting the weakest point of current practice in terms of performance and sustainability?
The main research question was explored through consecutive research sub-questions. The sub-questions have been schematically presented previously in Figure 1.2, and are as follows:
· What are the life-cycle environmental impacts of particleboards that undermine their sustainability?
This question aims to explore the major environmental impacts of standard particleboards in order to propose alternative solutions. The exploration relied on a review of the relevant literature and is discussed in detail in chapter 3.
· Given that the identified life-cycle environmental impacts are related to the origin and the processing of the fibres, and with the origin and toxic properties of the adhesives, are there alternative constituents or processes in Australia that have the potential to reduce or eliminate these impacts and contribute positively to sustainability?
This research sub-question is also explored by means of the literature review. Alternative constituents were identified in order to decrease the identified environmental impacts of standard particleboards. The most appropriate fillers, resins and processes were selected in order to ameliorate or eliminate the utilization of
monocrops, formaldehyde, VOC and CO2 emissions, and improve the performance of the composites in high moisture environments.
· What are the criteria for selecting these alternative constituents?
Similar to the previous research sub-question, this research sub-question was developed through the literature review. The main criteria for selecting the new proposed fillers stems from the industrial ecology framework.
· Can the by-product fibres from macadamia nuts and pine and Eucalyptus seed production be used to manufacture panel materials?
This research sub-question was developed from a practical perspective. The aim was to scientifically demonstrate the performance selection criteria drawn from the industrial ecology framework. Composites were manufactured with the selected fibres and resins, and further evaluated against international standards in order to compare their performance.
· If the by-product fibres from the macadamia nuts and pine and Eucalyptus seed production can be used to create panel products, would the identified impacts from traditional particleboards be ameliorated and the new products be therefore more sustainable?
The aim of this research sub-question is to close the original research question. This research sub-question is related to the experimental part of the study. The analyses of the proposed composites were compared with reference samples and with the standard particleboards and all were assessed with the criteria acquired from industrial ecology.
1.8. RESEARCH METHODS
The research methods used involved two stages. As seen in Figure 1.2, the first stage of the research was initially based on a literature review. The literature review consisted of three parts: a study of the most appropriate fillers and alternative sustainable
particleboards and resins; and a review of the life cycle environmental impacts of standard particleboards. The second stage explored the feasibility of using the proposed fibres combined with different and more sustainable matrixes. These new proposed composites were compared with reference panels manufactured with radiata pine wood and conventional resins. The study considered their chemical content, the density of each material, and grain size. The samples manufactured with recycled polypropylene and castor oil-based adhesive as matrixes, were assessed by testing their physical properties (density, water absorption, degree of swelling), mechanical properties (modulus of elasticity, modulus of rupture, and impact resistance), and microscopy analysis. The particleboards with castor oil-based polyurethane were further analysed for their level of formaldehyde emissions. The physical and mechanical results were statistically analysed and the performance, chemical contents, and the microscopy analysis considered and discussed.
1.9. THESIS STRUCTURE
This thesis expounds on the research process in 6 chapters. Chapter 1 discusses the problem context and theoretical framework rationale, defining the knowledge gap. It explains the structure of the thesis, and summarizes the research methods. Chapter 2 and 3 are the literature review which focus on the first three research sub-questions regarding the life-cycle environmental impacts of standard particleboards. These consider the most suitable alternative constituents and the criteria for selecting them. The first section of chapter 2 is a literature review of the most common agricultural by- products used in plant based composite materials, followed by an exploration of the main food industry and forestry by-products in Australia. The second section gives an introduction to wood fibres based composites and resins, including bio-resins, followed by studies on the development of non-conventional wood composites. Chapter 3 reports the main environmental problems faced by particleboards.
Chapter 4 explains the research methods used to define the suitability of the fibres and binders selected, from the preliminary combinations to the final samples. It describes the testing and analysis conducted in the development of a functional and efficient sustainable particleboard.
Chapters 5 and 6 focuses on the third and fourth research sub-questions, exploring the possibility of using the proposed alternative constituents to manufacture composite panel materials and diminishing or eliminating the environmental impacts of standard particleboards. The first section of chapter 5 reports the results of the chemical content and density of the new proposed fibres compared with softwood. This section also presents a visual evaluation of the manufactured composites based in guayule rubber, recycled PP, and castor oil-based adhesive. The second section of this chapter discusses the results of the composites manufactured with a recycled PP matrix. The third section of chapter 5 presents the results of the samples with castor oil-based polyurethane adhesive. Chapter 6 presents the discussions of the results. And conclusions and recommendations for future research are given in chapter 7.
CHAPTER 2: LITERATURE REVIEW PART 1
This chapter has two objectives. The first objective is to identify the most appropriate agriculture and forestry by-products in Australia to be used as a substitute to pine wood in particleboards. The second objective is to identify the most appropriate recycled or renewable binders to be used as a substitute to UF in particleboards. It begins reviewing non-wood fibres suitable to be used in the production of particleboards, not only in Australia, but worldwide. Then it presents the most appropriate forestry and agricultural by-products in Australia to be used as substituents of wood fibres, and finally it presents a review of the resins used in the manufacture of wood based composites and particleboards, concluding with the selection of three more sustainable alternatives.
The different viewpoints to define sustainable composite materials mainly depend on the final application or treatment that the material will have. For example, a wood composite can be considered sustainable if it is biodegradable, if it requires less energy to be produced, or if it is recyclable. Baillie (2004) suggested that ecological responsibility is to evaluate what is local, and to understand the web of relational properties that must inform responsible design, manufacturing and consumption. Others consider sustainable as being bio-based, consisting on bio-fibre and resins from renewable sources and thus expected to be biodegradable (Mohanty et al., 2002).
In this research, a wood composite is considered sustainable if it fulfils the criteria drawn from industrial ecology framework, indicated in chapter 1. These criteria emphasises the locality and recyclability of materials, conservation of biodiversity, use of renewable, biodegradable, energy efficient, and non-toxic resources, and the extended useful life.
2.1. WORLDWIDE AGRICULTURAL BY-PRODUCTS SUITABLE FOR PARTICLEBOARD PRODUCTION.
The increasing demand for wood to be used in wood composites has led researchers to investigate the utilization of non-wood lignocellulosic materials instead of wood (Stark et al., 2010; Philippou et al., 2001). This section explores the most relevant possible
non-wood fillers that are by-products from the agriculture industry and reports the applications related with them.
Natural fibre fillers can be defined as cellulosic materials. They are the most abundant form of biomass and the form most likely to be used as reinforcement in wood composites, not only for ecological and economic reasons, but also because of their high mechanical and thermal performance, low cost and availability (Baillie, 2004).
Çöpür et al. (2007) mention in their study that the utilization of agricultural and forestry by-products as a raw material in wood composite materials has been occurring since the 1900s. Nowadays it is possible to find more than 30 industries that use renewable biomass in production of particleboards around the world (Bektas et al., 2005), and it seems as a promising future for the production on alternative particleboards. Nonetheless, the high cost of collecting, transporting and storing the by-products is one of the problems detected with the utilization of these fibres. Problem that could be solved by having local particleboard manufacturing industries close to the rural production areas (Çöpür et al., 2007).
The unique properties of composites and particleboards manufactured with agriculture and forestry by-products depend on a number of variables, some of them are the locality of the plant, the section of the plant they have been harvested from, the maturity of the plant, and how the fibres are harvested and preconditioned. Natural fibres are not uniform in their properties, but the advantage of these fibres is that any local fibre can be utilized to make low cost composites (Wool and Sun, 2005).
The division and subdivision of natural fibres are shown in Figures 2.1 and 2.2. They are divided into four groups: animal fibres, wood fibres, vegetable fibres, and mineral fibres. This research focused only in vegetable fibres. Vegetable fibres, as shown in Figure 2.1 and 2.2, is subdivided in hairs, bast, hard fibres, stem, fruits, straw, and others.
Many studies have been carried out on the use of alternative fibres to wood in composite materials, most of them consider the utilization of the bast, hard fibres and
stem; and the less of them have consider the utilization of the fruits and seed shells of the plant (Youngquist et al., 1994). This section explores and reviews studies and development of these less explored types of fibres, and a brief review of some applications with the stems, used as a reference. These are marked inside of the red frame in the following diagrams.
Figure 2.1 Diagram of natural fibres divisions, based on Wood and Sun analysis (2005).
 Figure 2.2 Diagram of vegetable fibres subdivisions based on Wood and Sun analysis (2005).
2.1.1. Stem
The stem of the plant is one of the parts most used, as it has higher strength and stiffness and is rich in cellulose. The stem fibres are fibrous bundles in the inner bark of the plant stem and run the length of the stem (Baillie, 2004). In addition to the straw comprises the skeletal system of the plant, supporting the leaves. It is the part of the plant that absorbs the nutrients (Wool and Sun, 2005). Some plants in which the stem is successfully used in research and/or commercial applications of composite materials are wood, bamboo, jute, kenaf, flax, hemp, ramie, agave bagasse, sugar cane bagasse and sorghum (Baillie, 2004).
2.1.1.1. Sorghum (Sorghum saccharatum) stalks
Numerous Sorghum species (Sorghum bicolorare, sorghum saccharatum) are used for food, as grain and in syrup or molasses; and as fodder, in the production of alcoholic beverages and as biofuels (Akbulut and Özcan, 2008). Most species are drought and heat tolerant and are especially important in arid regions. They form an important component of pastures in many tropical regions. Sorghum species are an important food crop in Africa, Central America, and South Asia and is the fifth most important cereal crop grown in the world (FAO, 2012). The main producer of sorghum in the world in 2010 was United States with 8,779,280 tonnes, followed by Mexico with 6,940,230 tonnes produced in 2010; and India with a production of 6,700,000 tonnes in 2010. Australia is not a large producer of sorghum with an annual production of 1,598,000 tonnes in 2010 (FAO, 2012).
The utilization of sorghum by-products in composite materials has been studied extensively by researchers since the 1960s, some of them were Nemrhal and Kuniak (1961), whom used sorghum and a thermosetting resin for the manufacture of hardboards, resulting in equal or improved mechanical properties when compared standard hardboards made from spruce wood. Girarcl and Rose (1965), used low-grade brans from sorghum plant to increase the internal bond strength (IB) in the interior of insulating boards. Shen (1989), manufactured three-layer mat panels with sorghum without using adhesives, by hot pressing at 235 °C and 2.8 MPa pressure, with good
strength properties and dimensional stability. Smimizu et al. (1990), developed boards with sorghum stalks; the process consisted in using opened flattened stalks, arranging them to form a sheet, and bonding the several similar sheets with adhesive under pressure and heat. The resulting material had equal to or higher flexural strength than conventional plywood and excellent sound-absorbing and heat-insulating properties in comparison with that of conventional particleboard and fibreboard. The reclaimed stalks of the sorghum plant are used in US to make a decorative millwork material commercialised as Kirei board. Kirei board, presented in Figure 2.3, has been in use for wall coverings, cabinetry, furniture, flooring and other decorative and finished products. This panel board, even if it is manufactured with agricultural by-products and claims to have no urea formaldehyde (kirei, 2011), uses polymer isocyanate as a binder (API), based in ethylene co-vinyl acetate polymer (EVA), and in polymeric methylene diphenyl Diisocyanate (pMDI) (Hori et al., 2008). Both pMDI and EVA, are skin, eyes and respiratory sensibilisers (DuPont, 2008; DOW, 2009). In addition vinyl acetate has been recognized to be a carcinogen and a workplace hazardous substance (DuPont, 2008).
Sorghum was not further studied in this thesis as it is not highly available in Australia.
Figure 2.3 Sorghum Plants and panel applications (kirei, 2011)
2.1.1.2. Bagasse
Bagasse is the fibrous material that remains after the stalks of a plant, usually sugar cane, or blue agave is processed.
Hesch (1967) has done quite some research in the manufacture of particleboards out of different types of bagasse, from introducing a review of the morphological and chemical characteristics of bagasse used in particleboard manufacture, to manufactured particleboards from bagasse showing that this agricultural by-product is a suitable raw material for particleboard (Youngquist et al., 1994).
Sugarcane (Saccharum officinarum) bagasse
Sugarcane (Saccharum officinarum) bagasse is grown in over 110 countries with an estimated total production of 1,591 million metric tonnes in 2007; an image of sugarcane bagasse is presented in Figure 2.4. In 2010, the world's largest producer of sugar cane was Brazil with a production of 717,462,000 tonnes, followed by India with a production of 292,300,000 tonnes, and China with an annual production in 2010 of 111,454,359 tonnes. Compared with Brazil, China and India, Australia is a smaller producer of sugarcane (FAO, 2012), with an annual production in 2010 of 31,457,000 tonnes (FAO, 2012). In Australia, the bagasse that remains from sugar is used as biofuel to provide electricity and heat requirements for the same sugar cane industry. The most relevant example is the largest sugar producing and refiner in Australia, Sucrogen, in Queensland. Sucrogen generates electricity out of the residues of the sugar production process. The plant has a generation capacity of 197 megawatt, and feeds eight sugar mills and also exports the surplus to the local power grid (Clean Energy Council, 2012). It may also, because of its high cellulose content, serve as raw material for paper and cardboard (Sun et al., 2004). The utilization of sugar cane bagasse has been widely researched worldwide since at least the 1960s (Youngquist et al., 1994). Youngquist (1994), in his literature review on use of non-wood plant fibres for building materials and panels, reported a detailed list of some of these studies. Most of the identified early researches in the 1960s included the manufacture of panels with sugar cane bagasse bonded with UF resin, pressing with temperatures from 130 °C to 150 °C, at pressures
up to 6.9 MPa for 5-30 minutes. The thicknesses of these panels were from 8–35 mm, and densities from 0.30 to 0.75 g/cm3. The strength properties were comparable to those of similar boards made of jute sticks, hemp shives, flax shives, and various wood species, with values from 16.7 to 25.5 MPa bending strength, and modulus of elasticity (MOE) of 1.4 to 2 GPa. These studies proposed that bagasse particleboards could be used as flooring, wall partitions, ceilings, roofing, and furniture (Youngquist et al., 1994; Hesch, 1967; Anonymous, 1968). In the 1970s further research included treatments of the fibres in order to improve the physical properties; Sefain et al. (1978) worked on the effect of thermal treatment on the properties of sugar cane bagasse hardboard. This treatment reduced the strength, but improved water resistance. Later, Hamid et al. (1983), manufactured particleboards with sugarcane bagasse and different types of resins. Some of these composites were bagasse-phenolic, bagasse-rubber, bagasse-thermoplastic, and bagasse in-situ.
University of Guadalajara in Mexico has worked extensively on research projects to develop and manufacture composite boards using sugar cane bagasse particles and residual high density polyethylene (HDPE) from milk bottles (Fuentes T. et al., 2007; Fuentes Tavalera et al., 2005), however the research was interrupted due to a lack of funding, interest from the industry and the closing of the sugar cane industry in the area. Some commercial composite materials have also been developed with sugarcane bagasse. The manufacturer Tablopan in Venezuela, (Figure 2.4) has been making particleboards from sugarcane bagasse and UF resin for over 40 years. However, some four years ago, it had to shut down since bagasse became unavailable (El Palmar, 2009). While in South Africa, the manufacturer Xanita uses recycled cardboard boxes and sugar cane bagasse with VOC free organic starch-based adhesives to manufacture a honeycomb panel with two external particleboard layers. This board has high-strength, non-toxic and low density. Xanita has multiple uses, such as exhibition and set-building, displays, furniture, coffins and more. Xanita boards can be completely repulped back into kraft paper for re-use. The material is extremely lightweight and despite the core being shipped from South Africa it consumes less energy overall in transport than comparable products due to this weight advantage (Xanita, 2009). Figure 2.4 shows sugar cane bagasse and some of its uses in composite materials.
     Figure 2.4 Sugar cane plants and panel products applications (El Palmar; Xanita, 2009)
Since sugarcane bagasse is already used in Australia for the generation of electricity, it does not represent an environmental burden. Therefore, the utilization of sugarcane bagasse was not further studied in this research, and it was only used as a reference.
Blue agave (Agave tequilana)
Blue agave is mainly used for the production of tequila in Mexico, one of their residuals is agave bagasse, which implies up to 40% of the total weight of the milled agave, and it is mainly used for fuel. Bagasse is available all year in the main regions of the tequila producing areas in Mexico (Iñiguez-Covarrubias et al., 2001; Valenzuela, 2011) and blue agave bagasse is not available in Australia (FAO, 2012). University of Guadalajara in Mexico is the main research centre on the utilization of the agave bagasse or the residues from the production of tequila for the production of fibreboard (Ramirez- Calderon et al., 2005). Iñíguez-Covarrubias et al. (2001), studied the utilization of agave bagasse in long and short fibres particleboards in medium and high specific gravities. The fibreboards manufactured with agave bagasse with medium specific gravity had moisture and mechanical properties comparable to medium specific gravity fibreboards made using aspen fibre. All agave bagasse fibreboards with high specific gravity were stiffer in bending than the ANSI standard for hardboards (1982). Images of agave bagasse particleboards are displayed in Figure 2.5.
Ramírez-Calderon et al. (2005), studied the development of composites with agave fibre and polypropylene by thermo kinetic mixing and extrusion, the extrusion considered the utilization of a twin screw extruder, and the addition of maleated polypropylene (MAPP) to improve adhesion. The addition of MAPP coupling agent improved the tensile, flexural, and unnotched Izod impact strength. However, MAPP decreased the moisture absorption of the composites. In composites without MAPP, just the tensile and flexural moduli increased while the content of agave fibre increased since the inherently greater stiffness of the agave fibre compared to PP. Because agave tequilana is a plant of great value in Mexico, its use in composites with PP is recommended with the addition of MAPP coupling agent.
Figure 2.5 Manufacture of agave particleboards. Courtesy of Dr. Iñíguez- Covarrubias (2009).
2.1.2. Fruit
The utilization of the by-products of fruit production is the less explored area of research and development in composite materials manufacture. In this section a literature review of composite materials utilizing the protective layers of some fruits and the pits are presented. These are coconut tow, peanut, hazelnut, almond, coffee and sunflower shells, in addition to stone fruit pits. In this thesis, the words shells, hulls and husks are used as synonyms.
2.1.2.1. Coconut (Cocos nucifera)
Coconut is mainly produced by Indonesia, with an annual production in 2010 of 18,000,000 tonnes, followed by Philippines with an annual production in 2010 of 15,540,000 tonnes and India, producing 10,840,000 tonnes in 2010. Australia is a minor producer producing less than 26,000 tonnes annually (FAO, 2012).Various groups are working on the utilization of coconut fruit fibre or coir on various applications since the 1950s, some examples are:
Hadinoto (1957) raised a case study of a pilot plant with an annual production capacity of 3,500 tonnes of board to produce coconut husks based boards in Indonesia, in 1951. George (1964; 1962), in his studies, developed particleboards manufactured from coconut husk with pith waste and coir shearing bonded with a tannin-formaldehyde or phenol formaldehyde-resin in one layer and three layers medium density particleboard. The three layer particleboard had wood particles on both faces. The panels were pressed at 145 °C and 4.9 MPa for 15 min for a 6 mm board. Strength and water resistance were within the usual range for medium-density particleboard. The boards were found to be quite smooth on their surfaces, fire-resistant and tough.
Still if coir has been found to be very tough, nonetheless this fibre has a quite viscoelastic behaviour (Ash et al., 2006).
More recently, in Mexico at University del Norte, the fibre of the coconut tow obtained as residual was mixed with recycled plastic. Their physical, chemical and mechanical
properties were evaluated, as well as physical and mechanical properties of mortars reinforced with fibre of coconut tow (Quintero and Gonzalez, 2006). At University of Colima and Guadalajara, Navarro (2005) has been developed a coconut fibre and high density recycled plastic panel for housing; and in University of Guadalajara, Fuentes et al. (1997) have evaluated the potential of coconut stem wood and coir as raw materials for particleboard production with acceptable physical and mechanical properties compared with wood fibre panels.
In Brazil, the organization Poematec and, the Poverty and Environment in Amazonia (POEMA), have developed products from waste coconut fibres for use in agriculture, as the seat bases for the Mercedes Benz Class A cars (Poematec), and garden and housing accessories (Coquim, 2009), these appear in Figure 2.6.
Figure 2.6 a. Mercedes Benz Class A seat bases manufactured with coconut fibres (Poematec, 2009); b. Flower pot manufactured with coconut fibres (Coquim, 2009)
2.1.2.2. Peanut (Arachis hypogaea)
The main producer of peanut worldwide is India, with an annual production in 2012 of 400,000 tonnes, followed by Argentinean production of 300,000 tonnes; Australia’s production is up to 40,000 tonnes annually (FAO, 2012). Hulls consist of the 30% of the total peanut (Conicet, 2007). The elimination of such a volume of by-products has high environmental impacts, which implies higher transportation, incineration and stock piling spends. Peanut hulls are difficult to degrade due to the large volumes, high lignin and low nitrogen contents (Conicet, 2007).
  a.
b.
The utilization of peanut shells in composite materials is not new, and has been taking place since the 1970s. Pablo et al. (1975), developed a particleboard on a pilot-plant at a semi-commercial scale using mixtures of peanut shells and wood particles. While in France, simultaneously, the development of single layer panel materials based in peanut shells bonded with UF resin has been identified. The panels were proposed to be used in ceiling and wall panelling (Youngquist et al., 1994; Anonymous, 1975).
Güler et al. (2008), manufactured 3 layer particleboards using peanut hulls mixed with European black pine wood chips bonded with UF resin. He concluded that an increase of peanut hull in the mixture resulted in a decrease in mechanical and physical properties of produced panels. Only panels with 25% shell in the mixture solely met the standard required by TS-EN 312 standard (Güler et al., 2008). The modulus of rupture (MOR) of 100% peanut shells bonded with UF resin was 9.9 Pa and the MOE was 1,276 Pa. In case of IB, the lowest IB value of 0.32 Pa was obtained for the particleboards consisting solely peanut hull (Güler et al., 2008), meeting the minimum IB of 0.24 Pa required by the standard TS-EN 312 for general purpose end-use (Güler et al., 2008; Bektas et al., 2005; Kozlowski and Piotrowski, 1987).
The Experimental Centre for Affordable Housing (CONICET) (2007) in Argentina is developing new panel materials, exploring the compounding of peanut shells with polyester, post-consumer Polyethylene terephthalate (PET), and cement for ceiling and wall panel applications. These panels are low weight, good acoustic insulation and low cost. These characteristics make the panels appropriate for conditioning social housing (Beretta and Gatani, 2007). Figure 2.7 presents some of the applications developed by CEVE.
 a.
b.
Figure 2.7 a. Peanuts inside their shell; b. Logotype of The Experimental Centre for Affordable Housing (CEVE); c. Composite panel manufactured with peanut shells with polyester resin; d. Peanut shells with recycled PET composite; e. Peanut shells with concrete composite; f. Process of mixing the peanut shells with the polyester resin; g. Process of forming the mix of polyester and peanut shells into the panels; h. Ceiling panels manufactured with peanut shells and polyester resin (Conicet, 2007).
2.1.2.3. Hazelnut (Corylus avellana L.)
Hazelnut is mainly produced in Turkey, producing up to 73% worldwide production in 2010 (600,000 ton), followed by Italy (90,270 ton), and United States (25,401 ton) (FAO, 2012). Australia is a minor producer.
Copur et al (2007), studied the chemical properties and the feasibility of manufacturing particleboards from hazelnut husks. They used three different types of adhesives and two different densities. The water absorption (WA) and thickness swelling (TS) had a poor performance (20-29%) when compared with the TS-EN 312-2-Standard for general purpose particleboards (DIN, 1996). They concluded that the adhesive type and density of the particleboards had an effect over the water absorption and thickness swelling, but still in average with other particleboards manufactured with agricultural by-products, such as flax (20%) (Kozlowski and Piotrowski, 1987), tobacco straw (22%) (Kalaycoglu, 1992), cotton stalks (24%) (Güler and Ozen, 2004), hemp (25%) 29
  c. d. e.
f. g. h.
   
(Kozlowski and Piotrowski, 1987), sunflower stalks (25%) (Bektas et al., 2005), cotton carpel (26%) (Alma et al., 2005) and tea plant waste (27%) (Kalaycoglu, 1992). The MOR was between 11.9 – 7.7 Pa; however, these results did not meet the minimum bending test results required by the TS-EN 312-2 standard for general purpose particleboards (DIN, 1996), of 11.5 N/mm2. The IB of the particleboards varied from 0.3- 0.5 Pa, complying with the requirements of the EN 312-2, 1996-Standards for general purpose for interior fitments, which is 0.35 N/mm2. In this case, the adhesive type had no significant influence in the performance. However, the density did affect the results, the higher the density, the higher IB.
2.1.2.4. Almonds (Prunus amygdala L.)
The main producer of almonds worldwide is United States, with 1,413,800 tonnes in 2010, followed by Spain, with a production of 221,000 tonnes in 2010, and Iran, with a production on 158,050 tonnes in 2010. Australia had a quite smaller production of 30,000 tonnes in 2010 (FAO, 2012). Almond shell accounts for 35–75% of the total fruit weight (Ebringerová et al., 2008).
Pirayesh and Khazaeian (2012) studied the feasibility of using almond shell as a bio- waste resource in 3 layer particleboards bonded with UF resin, concluding that the almond shell is a viable filler to be used in particleboards if it is mixed up to 30% with wood particles. The addition of almond shells greatly improved the water resistance of the panels when compared with wood panels. The water absorption and thickness swelling after 24 hours was 33% and 10% respectively, 50% lower than a particleboard manufactured with wood and UF resin in both cases. These results can be caused by the lower content of cellulose and holocellulose when compared to wood and the higher amounts of extractives. Both cellulose and hemicellulose contain high amounts of hydroxyl groups and have hydrophilic characteristics. Several researchers have reported a positive role of extractives on the resistance to water (Nemli and Colakoglu, 2005; Büyüksarı et al., 2010). However, flexural properties (MOR and MOE) and IB strength were up to 50% lower in the particleboards with almond shells compared with wood particleboards. The particleboards manufactured with almond shells did not meet the minimum requirements for mechanical properties according to the DIN standard EN
312 (1996) for interior fitments including furniture manufacture. The results could be explained from a chemical perspective, since lignin, cellulose and hemicellulose are critical in the mechanical properties of wood composites, and almond shells have lower cellulose and holocellulose content than wood. Cellulose and holocellulose contain high polar hydroxyl groups, which are responsible for the hydrogen bonds with UF adhesive. These bonding interactions have important effects on the surface wettability and adhesion. Lower compactness can also affect. Water soluble extractives, quite high in almond shells, could have affected the gelling time of UF (Güler et al., 2008; Nemli and Colakoglu, 2005; Büyüksarı et al., 2010). Lower mechanical properties have been reported for particleboards made using agricultural crops by-products (Güler et al., 2008; Bektas et al., 2005; Kozlowski and Piotrowski, 1987; Güler and Ozen, 2004; Çöpür et al., 2007; Nemli and Colakoglu, 2005).
In Spain, the panel manufacturer Duralmond (2008) produces a product based in 30% - 40% almond shells mixed with natural, biodegradable and synthetic resins. Their products range from moulded decorative panels to soffits, and can be used in interior and in exterior applications. These are shown in Figure 2.8. Duralmond claims that their products do not produce any wastes during manufacture, are non-toxic, biodegradable, water repellent, and insect resistant, have good acoustic properties, and are fire resistant. In addition, these composites have low densities, which vary from 175 g/cm3 - 700 g/cm3.
Figure 2.8 a. Almonds inside their shell; b. Duralmond octagonal decorative panel; c. Duralmond soffit (Duralmond, 2008).
    b. c. a. b. c.
2.1.2.5. Coffee (Coffea robusta)
Some composite panel materials have been made from coffee (Coffea robusta) crops, 57% of whose world production comes mainly from Brazil and Colombia. Brazil produced 2,906,320 tonnes during 2010, followed by Vietnam with 1,105,700 tonnes produced in 2010, and Indonesia with 684,076 tonnes produced in 2010. Australia is not a big robusta coffee producer (FAO, 2012), the only coffee grown in Australia is Coffea arabica.
Research on coffee shells has been taking place since the 1960s. The Tropical Products Institute (1963) manufactured particleboards of 1.27 cm thick from a mixture of coffee husks and cotton seeds, and from a mixture of coffee husks and groundnut shells, bonded with UF resin. The material was pressed at 140 °C for 10 min at around 2.1 MPa. Increased resin content and increased density resulted in improved board strength and water resistance. Boards containing 15% resin with a density of 1.1 g/cm3, exceeded the minimum strength requirements of the British Standard. Coffee husks mixed with other waste materials gave boards of lower strengths than coffee husks by itself.
Runton (1972) patented a panel material manufactured with a mixture of ground-coffee bean hulls and rice hulls bonded with phenol-formaldehyde resin and pressed at 143.3 °C to 204.4 °C under a pressure of 15.7 kg/mm2 to obtain a product useful for furniture parts.
One commercial example of boards manufactured with coffee hulls is the Raw studio in the UK, Nick Rawcliffe (2012) has developed and manufactured coffee tables based in coffee hulls and plastics; these are shown in Figure 2.9.
a.
b. c.
   Figure 2.9 a. Coffee table made from coffee hulls, designed by Raw Studio in the UK; b. Detail of the texture of the coffee table manufactured with coffee beans and recycled plastic; c. Roasted coffee bean (Rawcliffe, 2012).
2.1.2.6. Sunflower (Helianthus annuus)
Ukraine is the biggest producer of sunflower, with a production of 6,771,500 tonnes in 2010, followed by the Russian federation, with a production of 5,344,820 tonnes in 2010, and in third place it is Argentina with a production of 2,232,030 tonnes in 2010 (FAO, 2012). Australia is a much smaller producer, with an annual production of 40,000 tonnes in 2010. Seed husks are an abundant agricultural fibre by-product.
The company EnvironBiocomposites (2009) has developed a particleboard out of sunflower seeds hulls known as Dakota burl composite. No VOCs are emitted into the atmosphere during manufacturing. The material can be cut, sanded and routed with standard woodworking tool. It is suitable for applications such as horizontal and vertical surfaces such as cabinetry, furniture and architectural applications. They are intended for commercial and residential interior applications only, and it is not recommended for use in kitchen or bathroom countertop spaces. This is still a commercially available product, as seen in Figure 2.10.

       d.

   Figure 2.10 a. Sunflowers; b. Detail of the surface of EnvironBiocomposites; c. EnvironBiocomposites particleboard; d. Woman removing sunflower seeds from sunflower; e. Sunflower seeds; f. EnvironBiocomposites particleboard and pen (EnvironBiocomposites, 2009).
2.1.2.7. Stone fruits (Prunus spp.) pits
Stone fruits are a major crop group in Latin America and also widely popular in Australia. Australia produces 160,000 tonnes of stone fruit annually. Chile is the main worldwide producer of stone fruits with an annual production close to 400,000 tonnes (Hennicke, 2009).
Stone fruit kernels are a major disposal problem, SPC Ardmona in Australia generates from 4 to 7 tonnes annually and is starting to use them as filler for stock feed and as fuel (Holtham, 2009). Some of the most common popular stone fruits are peach (Prunus persica), plum (Prunus domestica), cherry (Prunus avium) and apricot (Prunus armeniaca). The pits from manufacturers of fruit juices and jams, canned or bottled fruit slices and dices, and pitted fruit snacks such as prunes, peaches, nectarines, plums, apricots and cherries, can be a good source of wood-like material. The company Stone fruits floors (2008), in South Africa are producing wood flooring derived from peach pits. The floors consist of peach pits that have been laid by hand and sealed with glue,
resin and urethane. The sharp edges of the pits are ground off to expose the red hue of the pits and to make the floors comfortable to walk on with bare feet. Floors can either be fully installed on site or shipped as pre-manufactured tiles which reduce on-site labour requirements. Images of flooring applications with stone fruits are given in Figure 2.11. No further studies have been found in applications of stone fruit pits in particleboards.
  1a.
b.
  Peach pits
 Figure 2.11 a. Stone fruits pits flooring; b. Stone fruits pits (Stonefruitfloors, 2008)
2.1.3. Summary of main bagasse and fruit fibres used in particleboards and composites materials
High amounts of lignin and cellulosic by-products are generated every year as a result of the agricultural processing industry. These by-products are considered a disposal problem, and are usually burned. Many researches and development have studied the possibility of using them in composite products, some of them have been successful and some others have not.
Table 2.1 presents a comparison of the main chemical components of the most relevant by-products found in literature, and Table 2.2 shows a resume of the main properties of the panel materials found in literature. Some of the fibres previously presented are not reported in the following Tables, since the correspondent data was not available in literature. The elastic properties of particleboard manufactured with bagasse were better than the elastic properties of the particleboards manufactured with fruits (hazelnut husks, almond shells and peanut husks) and wood. However, agave bagasse
particleboards reported poorer physical properties when compared with the wood, shells and husks. This performance can be explained from a chemical and a mechanical perspective. Cellulose and holocellulose contains high polar hydroxyl groups, which are responsible for the hydrogen bonds with UF adhesive. These bonding interactions have important effects on the surface wettability and adhesion (Güler et al., 2008; Nemli and Colakoglu, 2005; Büyüksarı et al., 2010). Lower mechanical properties have been reported for particleboards made using agricultural crops by-products since most of them have lower amount of cellulose and hemicellulose than softwood (Güler et al., 2008; Bektas et al., 2005; Kozlowski and Piotrowski, 1987; Güler and Ozen, 2004; Çöpür et al., 2007; Nemli and Colakoglu, 2005).

2.2. FOOD INDUSTRY AND FORESTRY BY-PRODUCTS IN AUSTRALIA SUITABLE FOR PARTICLEBOARDS AND NON-WOOD COMPOSITES
The previous section reviewed the most relevant worldwide agriculture by-products used in the manufacture of particleboards or non-wood based composites, comparing the main chemical properties of the fibres and the physical and mechanical properties of the composites. This section reports the most suitable by-products from the food industry and forestry industry in Australia to be used as fillers in composites materials and particleboards, in order to develop more sustainable alternatives to standard particleboards.
Drawn from the industrial ecology framework (Frosch and Gallopoulos, 1989; McDonough and Braungart, 2002; Garner and Keoleian, 1995; Kibert et al., 2002; Graham, 2003; Ayres, 1989; Kneese et al., 1970; Keoleian et al., 1995; Grann, 1997; Nattrass and Brian, 1999; Robèrt, 2002), and shown in Table 2.4, the criteria for selecting the most appropriate alternative fillers are:
· To use local materials: this criterion is applied towards avoiding travelling long distances from the plantation sources to the manufacturing plants and to the consumers.
· To use wastes as resources and to maintain biodiversity: the aim of these two criteria is to discourage the plantation of more monocultives to produce wood based materials, but instead promote the optimization of the use of trees, using by- products from other industrial processes.
· To eliminate toxic materials and by-products: this criterion aims to eliminate by- products or wastes from agricultural and forestry industrial streams, using them in new processes.
· To increase energy efficiency: This criterion aims to use materials which will require as less energy as possible to produce the composites and particleboards
· To extend the useful life of products: this criteria aims to find fillers that will have a good mechanical performance and a good resistance to moisture.
Drawn from the above selection criteria, the agricultural and forestry by-products selected for fillers are macadamia shells, pine cones and Eucalyptus capsules. In addition to the criteria presented above, these by-products were locally available, underused, and presented a disposal problem for the industries. In addition, there were few to no studies related with the utilization of these by-products in the development of new materials. Therefore macadamia shells, Eucalyptus capsules, and pine cones presented a promising research opportunity.
2.2.1. Macadamia (Macadamia spp) shells
Macadamia nuts come from plants belonging to the family of Proteaceae and are native to Australia. Macadamia nut trees grow best in sub-tropical climates where rainfall is gentle, but plentiful, during the spring flowering season and early autumn, just prior to harvest (Cochrane, 2007; Fraser, 2007). Currently, Australia is the main commercial producer of macadamia nuts, with an output of around 40,000 tonnes of nuts a year (FAO, 2012). Macadamia nuts are also produced commercially in Brazil, Costa Rica, Bolivia, Hawaii, New Zealand and Africa. The total global production is 100,000 tonnes per year (FAO, 2012; Australian Macadamia Society, 2010).
The shells and other waste comprise almost 70% w/w of the macadamia nuts. They are burned as a wood substitute in coffee roasting, ground to produce organic waste for
gardening, used for mulch, or used for chicken litter that, after use, is used as fertilizer. They are also used as a source of biomass fuel, having a calorific value of 5,500 kcal/kg (Anonymus, 2012). It has also been reported that macadamia shell powder can be used as a filler in the plastics industry (Woodroof, 1967).
Like other plant matter, macadamia nut shells are comprised of cellulosic fibres (Toles et al., 1998). Macadamia shells have a thickness of 1.5 mm - 5 mm, and are composed of two main layers, an internal softwood-type layer, and an external hardwood-type layer. The internal layer is soft, easy to break, flexible, and absorbs moisture, in order to give the appropriate nutrients and shelter to the nut. This also allows the nut to crack from the inside to assist germination due to expansion on soaking. The external layer is hard, brittle and has a hydrophobic nature. It contains more fissures than the internal softer wood, reflecting its brittle nature (Kaupp and Kaupp, 2007).
The modulus of elasticity of macadamia shells is within the range 4.2 to 5.2 GPa (Wang et al., 1995), which is significantly lower than that of timbers such as radiata pine (8.1 GPa) (Kretschmann, 2010). The tensile strength of macadamia shells is 55 MPa (Wang et al., 1995; Wang and Mai, 1995; Jennings and Macmillan, 1986). They have a bulk density of 680 kg/m3, a total density of 1,127 kg/m3, and a moisture content of around 10% (Baumann et al., 2000).
Very limited work has been carried out on the use of macadamia shells in composite materials. One brand, Husque, which is made from pulverized macadamia shells bonded with petroleum based thermoset polymer, is available as tableware products, bowls, vases and platters, polished with polyester resin and macadamia oil. Images of macadamias, macadamia shells and Husque bowls are shown in Figure 2.12.
       Figure 2.12 Macadamia tree, shells and Husque bowls (Egloff, 2011; Cochrane, 2007; Fraser, 2007).
Dong and Davies (2012), studied the flexural properties of a composite manufactured with polyester filled with macadamia shell particles at ratios from 10% - 40%. The macadamia shells/polyester composites were manually manufactured and the flexural properties were obtained in accordance with the standard ASTM D790 (2003). In addition, the study considered the evaluation of the influence of the voids over the flexural properties of the composites. They concluded that adding macadamia shell particles to the polyester, does not improve the flexural strength (MOR) of polyester, however it does increase the flexural modulus (MOE) of the polyester, while it decreases when increasing void content.
No further studies were found with macadamia shells particleboards.
2.2.2. Eucalyptus capsules
Eucalyptus trees, native from Australia, are the most planted hardwood in the world (Eldridge et al., 1993). All the eucalypts important from the forestry standpoint occur naturally only in Australia, which goes up to 127,024,000 hectares (Pink, 2008). Nonetheless, Eucalyptus plantations covering more than one million hectares have been 40

created in various countries lying in world climatic zones of subtropical or Mediterranean type. Eucalyptus have been introduced to many parts of the world, notably Brazil (3,751,857 hectares planted) (Stape, 2008), Ecuador, Colombia, and Chile (160,000 hectares planted) (Soto A et al., 2011). On rotations of frequently only 9 to 10 years, they form the basis of very considerable industrial concerns, such as sawmilling, pulp, charcoal and others.
The most commonly planted species of Eucalyptus in Australia is Eucalyptus globulus, comprising around 65% of all plantation hardwood with approximately 177,213 hectares planted in Australia in 2005, followed by Eucalyptus nitens, with 143,259 hectares planted in 2005 (Bureau of Rural Sciences, 2011).
The capsules of the Eucalyptus are the fruit containing the seeds of the tree, and the capsules of the Eucalyptus globulus are amongst the largest of all Eucalyptus species (Potts et al.; Suitor, 2008), with 15 mm long and 2 cm diameter (Skolmen and Ledig, 1990). In order to obtain the seeds, the capsules are collected form the trees, and dried. This procedure triggers the capsules to open and release the seeds (Cochrane, 2008). These empty capsules are produced in large quantities in silviculture businesses and in seed production industries. Once the seeds are collected, Eucalyptus capsules can be used as mulch and fuel, due to their high content of lignin and cellulose. However, these capsules are commonly destroyed since their storage are considered a hassle by tree growers (Cameron, 2009).
Not many applications have been developed out of Eucalyptus capsules, one example is the Australian-crafted bowl of capsules contained in resin, as seen in Figure 2.13 (Stemp, 2009).
    Figure 2.13 Eucalyptus capsules and application (Eucalyptus capsules encapsulated in resin crafted bowl) (Stemp, 2009).
2.2.3. Pine cones
Other forestry by-product that is underused is radiata pine cones. Pinus radiata (Pinus radiata D. Don) was first inserted into Southern Hemisphere countries from its very limited area of natural occurrence on the Monterrey peninsula of California (Tulstrup, 1959). Australia has 741,598 hectares planted (Bureau of Rural Sciences, 2011) and in the south-central part of Chile this species has been successfully introduced, with plantations over well above 1,478,000 hectares (Soto A et al., 2011). Radiata pine constitutes one of the most important potential raw material resources for the production of pulp and paper (Tulstrup, 1959).
Pine cones, a renewable resource, are not used to their potential. Large quantities of cones are produced annually throughout the world, especially in pine plantations grown for the pulp and paper industry. The pine cones contain large quantities of glucose derived from cellulose and smaller quantities of mannose, galactose, and xylose derived from hemicelluloses (Pettersen, 1984). They also contain significant amounts of phenol extractives, phenol, and condensed tannins (Gonultas, 2008). They are collected, dried
to facilitate seed release, and generally discarded, burned in the stove in winter, or sold to make decorations. SeedEnergy Australia has tried to recycle and compost them, but the process is slow and the results are in an inferior quality than using bark (Cameron, 2009). In Australia, just one industry produces 100 to 150 m3 of empty cones per year after seed extraction (Cameron, 2009).
Up to date, there is no information on using radiata pine cones as an alternative to wood material in manufacture of commercial particleboards. New uses for pine cones can provide additional income for forest landowners. Since the pine cones are collected and dried, they do not require any additional cost for collecting and drying.
Some studies have been carried out by researchers Gonultas, Büyüksarı, and Ayrilmis (2010; 2008; 2009; 2010; 2010; 2011), manufacturing panel boards out of stone pine (Pinus pinea) cones and phenol formaldehyde adhesive. These studies concluded that the addition of cone particle improved water resistance of the panels, and that the pine cones can be considered as an alternative to wood material in the manufacture of particleboard used in indoor environments due to lower thickness swelling, water absorption and formaldehyde emission. The mechanical properties were however poorer when adding pine cones to the composites and particleboards. In addition, pine cones can be used as renewable biological formaldehyde absorbent. The condensed tannin, due to its phenol nature, can react with formaldehyde in the UF resin and can reduce formaldehyde emissions from wood based composites. Figure 2.14 shows pine cones.
Figure 2.14 Pine cones. Author.
