All three macadamia shells, Eucalyptus capsules and pine cones are produced in large quantities by the food and silviculture industry, and so far, they have not been utilized to their full potential. These three by-products are widely available in Australia (Cameron, 2009; Lee, 2010), and have properties that will make them excellent filler for panel materials at a low cost. Some of these properties are their high lignin and cellulose content, in addition to the protective function of their shells, adapted to the local weather. The material obtained after the seeds extraction is dry and clean, which seems as an opportunity to reduce processing emissions and costs. It is possible to produce a durable product suitable for interior panel applications.
Macadamia shells, pine cones and Eucalyptus capsules were identified as the most appropriate agriculture and forestry by-products in Australia to be used as a substitute to pine wood in particleboards.
2.3. TRADITIONAL AND ALTERNATIVE RESINS SUITABLE FOR MORE SUSTAINABLE PARTICLEBOARDS AND COMPOSITE MATERIALS
The aim of this section is to identify the most appropriate recycled or renewable binders to be used as a substitute to UF in particleboards. This section introduces to traditionally used resins and bio-resins, and a review of studies and developments of alternative more sustainable wood based composites.
2.3.1. Types of resins used in wood based composites
Adhesives in wood based composites have been an issue since beginning of times. The first adhesives used by man were fully bio-based, such as hide glue, gum or starch glues and casein. Hide glue, for example is made from animal hide, hooves, bones, and fish. This adhesive is not suitable for exteriors or humid environments since it is not very resistant to high temperatures, water, bacteria and fungi. Along with hide glue, gum or starch glues were made by boiling flour in water, but they were not as effective as the first one. Casein, which is the whey in milk, was used in aircraft and yacht work. Casein joints cure in a day or two and are relatively waterproof, but if exposed to prolonged moist conditions it decays (Gordon, 1979). These original glues were renewable, and bio-based, however, there was a need for a more efficient and durable adhesion system.
The mechanisms involved in the adhesion process are three, as explained by Frihart (2005): the mechanical, the diffusion of polymers, and chemical adhesion. The adhesions in wood can take place through a combination of these mechanisms or by their individual action.
The mechanical theory of adhesion relates to the adhesive flowing and penetrating the porous substrate of wood. Once the adhesive hardens, it forms hooks between the wood
and adhesive substrates, anchoring in a similar manner to the operation of a Velcro. This theory is directly linked to the porosity of the wood. If the wood has a lower density, therefore a higher porosity, the adhesive penetrates with no trouble. However, it might penetrate too much, resulting in a gluing line that might be too thin. Another important factor is the viscosity of the adhesive. A suitable viscosity will allow the adhesive to penetrate and fill appropriately the porous of the wood substrate (Frihart, 2005).
The theory of diffusion of polymers states that the adhesion occurs through the diffusion of segments of polymer chains at a molecular level when the molecules of both substrates are mobile and soluble in each other. Therefore, one end of a molecule diffuses into the other substrate. This theory attributes the adhesion system to the infiltration of chains of compatible and miscible substrates at the interface. However, both substrates should be polymers (Frihart, 2005; Butt et al., 2010).
The chemical theory of adhesion of polymers explains the adhesion from the perspective of the compatibility of two polymers. If these polymers are compatible, the chains of polymers within combine through primary linkage across the interphase (hydrogen, ionic or covalent bonds), forming a perfect bonding (Butt et al., 2010; Frihart, 2005).
Different adhesives act at different level or in a combination of the three of them. Other factor to consider is the pH of wood and resin. The pH of the resin should be between 1.5 and 11, other way it might degrade the fibres (Frihart, 2005; Butt et al., 2010).
Figure 2.15 presents a diagram of the three theories of adhesion previously presented.
 Figure 2.15 Diagram of the three theories of adhesion: mechanical theory of adhesion (mechanical anchoring), diffusion theory of adhesion (diffusion) and chemical theory of adhesion (chemical cross linking) (Butt et al., 2010).
The resins used in adhesion of wood fibres are divided into four different groups, the first group is more brittle, and consists in structural adhesives that have a lower creep, which is described as the lack of flow characteristic under normal conditions, meaning that bonded products will retain their shape. This class includes UF. The second group is more ductile, with good toughness, especially for impact resistance; and includes wood adhesives such as polyurethanes. The third group are plastics used in wood plastic composites; and the fourth group is flexible, excellent for sealant applications (Frihart, 2005).
Thermosets and thermoplastic adhesives are the two types of resins mostly used in the wood industry since they have an improved resistance to moisture and to the effects of microorganisms. Thermoset adhesives cure through chemical reactions activated by means of temperature or catalysts. Amongst the main thermoset adhesives used in wood are UF and polyurethanes. Thermoplastic resins flow with heat, and harden at room temperature, some examples are hot melts (Frihart, 2005) and Ethylene Vinyl Acetate (EVA).
2.3.1.1. Chemistry of urea formaldehyde adhesives
In order to define an appropriate resin for a new more sustainable composite or particleboard, it is necessary to define how adhesives set and perform. This section gives a brief explanation from a chemical perspective of traditionally used UF adhesive. UF resins are thermosetting duromers. Duromers can be defined as heavily cross linked polymers, usually handled as liquids with a hardener that acts as a cross linker, based on linear or branched oligomer and polymeric molecules (Dunky, 1998).
The chemistry of the urea-formaldehyde adhesives begins with the addition of the formaldehyde to the urea under neutral or basic conditions (Pizzi, 2003). Formaldehyde first reacts over the nitrogen atoms N,N,N ́-tris (hydroxymethyl) urea, under slightly acidic conditions. Formaldehyde and urea are then heated to generate oligomers, where the molecules of urea are linked by bismethylene ether or methylene bridges. The pH of UF is normally 7 to 8, which increases when the desired molecular weight is reached. Finally, the temperature is lowered to slow down the reaction (Pizzi, 2003; Frihart, 2005). Setting the adhesive involves both the loss of water, to a solid content of 65%, and polymerization to form the bond (Frihart, 2005). From the different components of the wood, both lignin and hemicellulose soften under high moisture and temperature, being the main places where bonding occurs; while hemicellulose forms hydrogen bonds, allowing the interaction of water and hydrogen to link to the adjoining fibres. Lignin forms chemical bonds, particularly in the lumen walls, because the warty layer of woody materials is high in lignin content (Frihart, 2005; Tsoumis, 1991).
2.3.1.2. Polyurethane adhesives
Polyurethanes are used in more specialty wood-bonding applications, such as laminates, and composites. Their advantage is the wide formulation ability; and the lower sensitivity to wood moisture and process temperatures (Frihart, 2005). Polyurethanes are composed of two monomers, polyols and isocyanates. The most common isocyanates used in polyurethane synthesis are the aromatic isocyanates, particularly polymeric diphenylmethane diisocyanate (MDI) (Langenberg et al., 2010; Latere
Dwan'Isa et al., 2005); and the polyol can be based in petroleum or in plant oils, such as soybean, lesquerella, or castor oil (Conner, 1996; Silva et al., 2006). Isocyanate reacts mainly with the hydroxyl groups of the polyol, and is used to produce polyurethane adhesives by rapidly reacting with liquid diols (Langenberg et al., 2010; Frihart, 2005). Isocyanates used in wood bonding have generally higher molecular weight than other isocyanates, so their volatility and the number of free isocyanate groups are diminished, leaving aside the problem of volatility and risk of handling them during manufacturing (Frihart, 2005). Polyurethanes are highly compatible with vegetable fibres due to reaction of hydroxyl groups of the fibres and the isocyanate groups of the polyurethane, and seem to be more effective in the lignin domains (Langenberg et al., 2010; Latere Dwan'Isa et al., 2005; Marcinko et al., 2000; Conner, 1996; Silva et al., 2006).
2.3.1.3. Plastics in wood composites
Plastics are present in almost everything in our daily lives. These are produced at high amounts every day, due to worldwide increasing consumption rates. The growing rate of plastic production duplicates every four years (Meran et al., 2008), for example, the amount of plastic produced in 1930 was 100,000 tons; and in 2005 it exceeded 500 million tons. Whilst the production of plastics has increased, plastic waste has become of the major components of global municipal solid waste and the need of reusing it is of high importance. Plastic waste can be generated as a post-industrial residue, and as a post-consumer waste (Pospíšil et al., 1995). Usually post-industrial waste is reused in the same plastic industry. Nonetheless, post-consumer waste is more complicated to deal with. Some of the methods of dealing with the plastic waste are source reduction, synthesis of degradable plastics, feedstock generation and energy recovery by incineration or landfills (Pospíšil et al., 1995). An alternative, more sustainable way of dealing with post-consumer plastic is through recycling. Yet if some of the main chemical and physical properties degrades after UV exposure and use, they can be recycled by cleaning and melting them again (Pospíšil et al., 1995), and depending on their application, their life can be extended for many cycles.
The utilization of recycled plastic for the manufacture of WPC has been studied by a number of authors, since the properties of recycled plastics are similar to those with virgin plastics (Najafi et al., 2006; Winandy et al., 2004).
Since the decade of the 80s, wood-plastic composites (WPC) experienced a constant and increasing development, after researchers started to work on the effective addition of high amounts of wood fillers into the plastics matrix. Wood–plastic composites are widely used in the US, mainly in decking (Wolcott and Englund, 1999).
The most common type of such composites is manufactured by mixing wood flour and virgin or recycled plastics to produce a material that can be processed similar to 100% plastic-based products (Klysov, 2007). These composites are transformed by extrusion processes to obtain non-structural building applications including decking (Klysov, 2007), floor parquets, plastic lumbers sheathings, roof tiles, and window trims, with improved thermal and creep performance compared with unfilled plastics. WPC can be also moulded by injection, obtaining products such as flower vases, waste paper baskets, park benches and picnic tables (Najafi et al., 2006). Since WPC have thermoplastic matrix, these can be recycled, similarly to plastics (Winandy et al., 2004). Some of the major advantages of WPC include their resistance against biological deterioration for outdoor applications where untreated timber products are not suitable (Klysov, 2007). Therefore their lifetime is longer than traditional wood decking; besides they do not require maintenance. Some decking manufacturers provide 25 year warranty with theirs products (Cosset, 2013; Timbertech, 2013).
The sustainability of this technology becomes more attractive when the low cost and high availability of fine particles of wood waste is available. The combination of wood and thermoplastics results in materials with unique physical properties. In general, the wood tends to increase the stiffness, thermal stability, UV resistance, and workability of the composite. And the thermoplastics improve moisture resistance, resistance to decay, and impart thermoforming characteristics (Wolcott, 2001).
Wood is hydrophilic, in other words, it absorbs water and plastic is hydrophobic, it repels water. Therefore, wood moisture content during processing should be generally below 1% and wood will absorb moisture when exposed if not properly encapsulated by
the plastic matrix. Depending on the formulation; water absorption can result in an expected uptake greater than 20% (Wolcott and Englund, 1999).
The mechanical properties of WPC are similarly influenced by their composition in different ways. Elastic modulus increases with increasing wood flour fractions. However, it seems to be more difficult to improve strength properties with wood flour. Likewise, impact energy decreases with increased wood content. In order to address these issues, particle geometry is considered, coupling agents additives are added, and tougher plastics are used. Since the high densities of the composites are a drawback in transportation, the new generation of WPC are decreasing the densities through foaming. However, the strength and stiffness decreases in proportion to the density.
Amongst the drawbacks of WPC, manufacturing at high temperatures without appropriate venting can generate the emanation of VOCs and CO2 emissions, causing porosities in the composites that will affect negatively the physical and mechanical performance (Clemons and Caufield, 2005). There are several ways to improve overall properties of WPC panels. Some of them are using right size of wood flour, optimizing and the preparation of the elements in the product, and adding small amounts of additives such as coupling agents, pigments, antimicrobials or light stabilizers during their production (Wolcott, 2001).
Most wood plastic composites have wood fillers, however many researchers have studied the utilization of alternative fillers with a plastic matrix, some of these alternative fillers are pine cones (Dundar et al., 2010; Ayrilmis et al., 2010), Mikania micrantha (Chen et al., 2012), sugar cane bagasse (Fuentes Tavalera et al., 2005) agave bagasse (Ramirez-Calderon et al., 2005), hemp (Soatthiyanon, 2010), coconut fibres (Navarro, 2005), and rice and coffee hulls (Clemons, 2002; Klysov, 2007).
Images of different applications of wood plastic composites are shown in Figure 2.16.

2.3.2. Alternative resins: Natural rubber Guayule (Parthenium argentatum)
Rubbers are elastomeric materials which can be stretched and return to their original shape. Rubbers are used for the fabrication of tires, balls, balloons, gloves, and used as a protective coating in woods. There are two types of rubber, natural rubber or natural latex, and synthetic rubber (Morton, 1987; Allen, 1972).
Artificial rubbers are high molecular weight polymers, derived from petroleum, coal, oil, natural gas, and acetylene. These rubbers are obtained through vulcanization and today synthetic rubber accounts for about 60% of the world's rubber production. Natural rubber consists of isoprene molecules linked into loosely twisted chains. Natural rubber is obtained from the milky secretion (latex) of various plants, and the
most important commercial source of natural rubber is the tree Hevea brasiliensis. The 53

only other plant under cultivation as a commercial rubber source is guayule, a shrub native to the arid regions of Mexico and the South West United States (Morton, 1987; Allen, 1972). The guayule shrub is perennial, requires less water, less planting preparation, less cultivating and harvesting compared to other crops (Yulex, 2009). Australia’s largest seedling producer, Withcott, has recently partnered with Yulex to trial the crop in at least five farms in Queensland. The University of Queensland is also been growing guayule plants and investigating their potential as an industrial crop. Additional global expansion is possible in semi-arid regions of Brazil, Argentina, South Africa, South Asia and the Mediterranean (24-7PressRelease.com, 2008).
Two types of guayule rubber are produced: the dry rubber raw material based and the resin based emulsion. The resin based emulsion is a much stronger adhesive base than the rubber which is used for wound care products and paper and can be used as a matrix or adhesive in rubber natural fibre composites as well. Guayule rubber has high contents of terpenes, which is a natural pesticide. Therefore, this natural rubber can also be used as a source of wood preservatives, or pesticides that can be applicable into termite resistant boards (Nakayama, 2005).
Yulex USA is the company leading the commercialization of guayule latex. The prices of the guayule latex are around US$4.5-11.25/kg (Yulex, 2009). Some of its by- products are resin and bagasse, which is actually used as fuel, but can be used for fibre reinforced panels (Downes et al., 1986; Yulex, 2009). Figure 2.17 shows images of rubber plantations and rubber.
Some studies have been carried out in the manufacture of composites with natural rubber and natural fibres (Bhowmick and Stephens, 2000), such as wood (Abdelmouleh et al., 2007), bamboo (Ismail et al., 2002), sisal, oil palm fibres (Jacob et al., 2004) and coir (Geethamma et al., 1998). Most of the studies report that the addition of fibres to a natural rubber matrix increases the stiffness properties, the hardness, and the tensile modulus of the rubber. Furthermore, the addition of coupling agent to improve the bonding between hydrophilic wood and hydrophobic plastic increases the stiffness of the composites. Yulex has done some work related with guayule rubber-fibre composites (Williams, 2009). However, these are still in a developing stage, and no
published research or commercialization of composite based in guayule rubber has been found in literature.
  a. b.
c.
  Figure 2.17 a. Guayule rubber plantations; b. Solid guayule rubber (bottom left), moulded (upper left) and extruded samples of guayule rubber (right); c. Applications manufactured with guayule rubber (Yulex, 2009).
2.3.3. Plant based man made polymers
Figures 2.18 and 2.19 present the main components of important crops worldwide, and how they can be transformed into polymers or adhesives, which will be further explained in the following section.
The most useful elements to obtain bio-polymers are protein, oil and carbohydrates. Figure 2.18 presents which plants and which parts of the plants are richer in those components.
 Figure 2.18 Diagram of original sources for plant based polymers (Baillie, 2004)
Figure 2.19 shows which types of polymers can be obtained from protein, oil and carbohydrates.
 Figure 2.19 Diagram of potential products in plant based polymers (Baillie, 2004)
2.3.3.1. Carbohydrates based adhesives or polymers
As seen in Figure 2.19, plant based polymers can be obtained from the carbohydrates of the plants. Carbohydrates (polysaccharides) can be found in starch and cellulose (Baillie, 2004). Starch is the main component of corn, wheat, sorghum, and potato; comprising about 70 to 80% starch of their weight. The starch of these crops have a huge possibility of reuse, considering there is 2.3 billion of kilograms produced annually just in US. Starches are composed of glucose monomers polysaccharides
which cross linked amongst them and with other agents make a less water soluble polymer (Wool and Sun, 2005).
Starch: Polylactide biodegradable thermoplastics
Starch based biodegradable thermoplastics are the most common type of biodegradable plastics. The first generation of degradable plastics was based on the modified commodity plastics such as polyethylene or polypropylene, in which an additive was added to the plastic to help speed up the degradation process. The polymer would degrade by photo degradation, thermo degradation, hydro degradation, or biodegrade by the action of micro-organisms. In this last process starch was added between the long polymer chains through grafting or other bonding methods. The problem with this method was when plastics where placed into the soil, microbial action digested the starch, breaking the polymer into small, microscopic pieces, but the polymer was still in the soil (Allen, 2009). The second generation of biodegradable plastics, largely derived from plant based materials are designed to completely break down in a microbial environment. According to ASTM D6400 (2012), ISO 17088 (2012) and CEN EN 13432 (2000), in order to qualify as a biodegradable plastic, complete biodegradation under composting conditions is required. The material must be fully converted into Carbon Dioxide (CO2), water and biomass within 180 days and composted at the same time rate as natural materials, such as leaves, grass and food scraps (Allen, 2009).
Polylactide (PLA) and starch based polymers were some of the first second generation of biodegradable polymers. PLA is a bio-based synthetic polymer, and is one of the most popular sustainable polymers based on lactic acid. PLA is typically derived from fermenting milk, sugar based materials, rice, corn, potatoes, wheat, or sorghum (Mohanty et al., 2002). These mayor resources contain 70-80% starch. PLA plastic has different applications, including packaging, insulation foam, and disposable items such as cutlery, plates or glasses. Processing methods depend on the starch concentrations, these concentrations rages from 50-90%. They degrade quickly in hot water and it is known to have a relatively high melting point, around 160 °C, and a high mechanical performance (Drumright et al., 2000; Wool and Sun, 2005; Allen, 2009; Baillie, 2004).
Some studies and development have been carried out with PLA plastic matrix filled with natural fibres. One example is the project developed by the University of Concepción in Chile; they have developed PLA bio-plastics from grape pomace, residues of the local wine industry. One of the applications targeted was a biodegradable container for forestry and agriculture seedlings (UDT, 2009), exhibited in Figure 2.20.
Figure 2.20 a. Logotype of University of Concepción; b. Extrusion of wood plastic composites with PLA bio-plastic; c. Spoons manufactured by injection moulding of wood plastic composites with PLA bio-plastic; d. Pines planted in biodegradable PLA wood plastic composites containers; e. and f. Grape pomace in barrels after wine production; g. Pellets of wood plastic composites with PLA bio-plastic; h. Seedling container of wood plastic composites with PLA bio-plastic (UDT, 2009).
Other works have considered the manufacture of PLA based composites with less traditional fillers, such as cellulose (Mukherjee et al., 2012; Wu et al., 2013), bamboo, or agricultural by products such as rice hulls. In Australia, the company Ecolife International has amongst its products heat resistant, dishwashing safe, re-usable, biodegradable dishware and cutlery made from corn starch, bamboo and rice husks. The manufacturers claim that these products can be disposed in common composting or leave in the garden and will biodegrade after two years (Benjamin and Benjamin, 2011). The prices of these products are still high; they range from AUD$6 per plate, and the whole picnic set is around AUD$50. The Ecolife picnic set and cups are shown in Figure 2.21.
  Figure 2.21 Ecolife picnic set and cups (Benjamin and Benjamin, 2011).
PLA fibre Composites has not been included further in this research since the cost of acquiring and processing them is still high (Hensler, 2006).
Cellulose
Cellulose is mostly found in by-products from the milling industry, residuals from agricultural crops, or agricultural fibres. By-products from the milling industry and agricultural crop are increasing every day. Only in US the production raises up to 400 million metric tons annually, and straws (rice, corn, flax, and wheat) are generated in quantities close to 150 billion pounds annually. These by-products are composed by cellulose (29-38%), lignin (16-21%), and pentathol (26-32%). Those quantities of cellulose make them an ideal element to manufacture biodegradable thermoplastics (Mohanty et al., 2002; Wool and Sun, 2005). Some studies on the development of cellulosic plastics based in cellulose esters have been carried out by researches. Some of the applications manufactured with cellulosic plastics include small products such as screwdriver and toothbrushes handles (Mohanty et al., 2002; Hokens et al., 2001). No studies were found in regards to the utilization of cellulosic plastics as a matrix in wood or non-wood based composites.
Cellulose based resins were not further considered in this PhD thesis, since these are still in early development stages and little availability in Australia.
Lignin
Lignin is produced as a by-product of paper pulping and is considered a waste product. There is an estimated 10 million tons of lignin produced worldwide every year (Wool and Sun, 2005). It can be used as a copolymer or polymer additive in thermoplastic and thermosetting polymers and rubbers. It was found that lignin can increase the polymer modulus while increasing the melt temperature of various crystallizing thermoplastics (Wool and Sun, 2005). Lignin resins, similarly to cellulose resins were not further considered in this PhD thesis, due to their still early development and lack of availability in Australia.
2.3.3.2. Protein based adhesives
Some proteins are generated as by-products from the processing of oils and starches, they are available in high quantities in oilseeds such as soybeans (51 - 70 % dry weight), and grains like wheat and corn. Adhesions theory between protein polymers and wood substrates is mainly attributed to a combination of three major mechanisms, including physical adsorption, mechanical and chemical bonding. The main reason for the strength of protein adhesives is that protein molecules penetrate into the pores from the wood surface, forming a complex matrix upon curing. The rough surface under pressure can form random micro finger joint effects, enhancing gluing strength (Wool and Sun, 2005).
Two important protein based resins are corn and soy bean adhesives. These are presented in the following sub-sections.
Corn (Zea mays) adhesives
After the oil is extracted from corn germ meal, the corn germ is typically fed to poultry and other livestock animals. However, Hojilla-Evangelista (2012), from United Stated Department of Agriculture (USDA) has reported that corn germ can be used as a protein extender for plywood glues, potentially setting off a new market for the agricultural byproduct . Glue extenders reduce the amount of main binder, or resin, used in such glues and enhance their adhesive action. The conventional extender for most plywood glues is industrial-grade wheat flour; however, Hojilla-Evangelista’s work has expanded the list of agricultural extenders in the event glue manufacturers needed a comparable alternative (Selling et al., 2004). This research is yet not commercially produced, and not available in Australia. Corn germ-based glue was not further considered in this PhD thesis, due to their still early development and poor availability in Australia.
Soy bean (Glycine max) based adhesives
Soy beans plantation is a wide worldwide crop; the main producers in year 2010 were United States (90,605,500 tonnes), Brazil (68,756,300 tonnes), and Argentina (52,675,500 tonnes) (FAO, 2012). Australia, had a much smaller production of 38,000 tons in 2010-2011 (M'Gee, 2011; A.O.F, 2009). Soy beans contain about 20% oil and 40% protein (Mohanty et al., 2002). Moreover, soy protein has potential uses in the production of adhesives with high gluing strength and improved water resistance, while reducing time and waste. These are good resins for binding fibres such as recycled newspaper, wood veneer adhesive, and agricultural residue fibres. Soy protein adhesives can be produced as powder, foam, latex or resin (Wool and Sun, 2005).
Some examples of successful applications of soy bean adhesive are PureBond Composites and Environ BioComposites, produced by Columbia Forest Products (2008) in US since 2005. PureBond is hardwood plywood that uses a resin derived from soy. It can be used in high moisture interior applications such as kitchen and bathroom cabinetry, because of its superior moisture resistance (Columbia Forest Products, 2008). Environ BioComposites is a decorative interior material that combines recycled newsprint paper with soybean resin. No out-gassing solvents are added during the manufacturing process, thus the material emits no VOCs into the atmosphere. EnvironBioComposites are 11⁄2 times harder than oak although it can be cut, drilled, screwed and finished with the same processes than wood. However, it must be sealed to repel stains and moisture. Environ can be used in a variety of commercial and residential interior applications, including furniture, wall panels or cabinetry (Selling et al., 2004; EnvironBiocomposites, 2009), as shown in Figure 2.22.
           Figure 2.22 a. Soy beans; b. Bench manufactured with EnvironBiocomposites; c. Different colours and textures of EnvironBiocomposites (Zoid; EnvironBiocomposites, 2009)
Li et al., in their study demonstrated that soy bean based adhesives seems very promising in terms of having an alternative resin to UF adhesives. Soy bean based adhesives and UF adhesives are have similar physical and mechanical properties, in addition to no formaldehyde emissions of soy based resins; however no physical or mechanical improvement either (Li et al., 2009). Soy bean-based resins were not further considered in this PhD thesis, since their poor availability in Australia, higher prices and no significant physical improvement when compared with UF resins.
2.3.3.3. Plant oil-based polyurethane adhesives
Oils are present naturally as triglycerides in plants such as soy beans, corn, flax, and castor seeds. Adhesives can be derived from those bio-based oils with similar synthetic techniques as used to obtain petroleum polymers. These techniques basically reduce triglycerides into monoglycerides, and polymerize them by free radical polymerization. This process will produce bio-based polymers that have properties and costs comparable to those of petrochemical-based adhesives and resins, and could be recyclable and biodegradable (Latere Dwan'Isa et al., 2005; Kibert, 2008; Wool and Sun, 2005).
One example of a commercial composite material bonded with plant oil-based polyurethane was Woodstalk, manufactured by Dow chemical polymers. Woodstalk was made from harvested wheat straw fibre and vegetable based polyurethane; it was a board like material that could be used as an alternative to medium density fibreboard (MDF) (Kibert, 2008); unfortunately, this composite has been discontinued in 2005 due to the low demand and high operation costs for production (Alter, 2006).
Castor (Ricinus communis) oil-based polyol polyurethane adhesive
The castor plant is a plant of the Euphorbiaceae family from which seeds castor oil is extracted. Castor plant is mainly produced in India with a production in 2010 of 1,350,000 tonnes (FAO, 2012); followed by China with a production in 2010 of 180,000 tonnes; and Brazil with a production in 2010 of 95,183 tonnes (FAO, 2012; Ogunniyi, 2006); but in Australia castor plant is considered a weed common in wastelands.
The castor oil-based polyurethane is a two-component system, which consists on a pre- polymer isocyanate portion (MDI) and a polyol. The polyol is the isocyanate-reactive portion, and it is based on castor oil (Frihart, 2005). The adhesive is obtained by cold polymerization of the polyol (castor oil-based) and the pre-polymer mixture, a reaction leading to the formation of the polyurethane (Azambuja and Dias, 2006).
Castor seeds contain up to 50% oil (Brigham, 1993), comprising mostly of triglycerides. Triglycerides of castor oil are composed of 90% ricinoleic fatty acids, which have hydroxyl groups in its chemical structure. The hydroxyl groups of the castor oil react slowly with the isocyanate to form branched polyurethanes (Latere Dwan'Isa et al., 2005).
The adhesive offers several advantages, such as being non-aggressive to the environment and humans; resistance to water and UV rays, and handling and curing at ambient temperature, even though it can be accelerated with temperatures from 60 °C to 90 °C (Dias and Rocco Lahr, 2004; Silva et al., 2006) . Even though the resin has a
good thermal stability, Claro Neto (1997) defined on his study that the thermal decomposition of this polymer begins with temperatures above 150 °C.
With a density of 1.2 g/cm3, one of the properties of this bio-based polyurethane is resistance to moisture and good low-temperature flexibility (Kehl, 2011a; Latere Dwan'Isa et al., 2005). According to Latere Dwan ́Isa et al. (2005) and to Petrovic and Kajnik (1984), depending on the polyol and on the type and amount of MDI (isocyanate), the flexural properties of the polyurethane will vary. Yet this type of polyurethane is quite flexible. The Young’s modulus can go from 13 MPa with an elongation break of 93%, performing an elastomeric behaviour, to a rigid plastic, with a Young’s modulus of >300 MPa and an elongation at break of 10% (Latere Dwan'Isa et al., 2005). The major disadvantage of using castor oil is its limited production and thus higher price compared to other oils, such as soybean oil or palm oil. As previously presented; these bio-based polyurethanes can be used as a more efficient alternative to UF resins (Latere Dwan'Isa et al., 2005).
Many global chemical companies, including Kehl Brazil (2007), BASF (Mutlu and Meier, 2010), Arkema and Dupont (Treece, 2009), are currently researching on the polymeric properties and applications of castor oil resin as an alternative adhesive for plywood.
Kehl (Brazil) (2007) produces a castor oil-based polyurethane adhesive developed by the researchers of the Polymer Technology and Analytical Chemistry group of the University of São Paulo in São Carlos (Azambuja and Dias, 2006). This adhesive has a price of USD$7/kg FOB, and is proposed to be used in proportions of 10% over the total amount of fibre used. The adhesive is non-biodegradable, because it is produced for long time lasting. This bio-based polyurethane can be used for exterior applications, it has a tack of 60 kg/cm2, and the elongation at break of the castor oil-based polyurethane produced by Kehl is 10% (Kehl, 2011a).
Wood composites with castor oil-based polyol polyurethane
Castor oil-based polyurethane has been widely researched in different types of composites. All the studies have reports that castor oil-based polyurethane is an appropriate resin to be used as a binder in wood based composites (Azambuja, 2006; Azambuja and Dias, 2006; Azambuja et al.; Azambuja, 2002; Jesus, 2000; Silva and Goncalves, 2002; Dias and Rocco Lahr, 2004; Januario, 2006). The most relevant studies are presented in this section.
Jesus (2000) studied the behaviour of the castor oil-based polyurethane in glued laminated timber (GLULAM), evaluating the mechanical properties shear and IB. Jesus concluded that this polyurethane adhesive is a good alternative to be used in wood composites. Jesus reported that the viscosity of the resin increases after 20 minutes of mixing the polyol and the isocyanate components.
Azambuja et al. (2006; 2006; 2002), defined appropriate parameters for curing castor oil-based polyurethane in GLULAM with Eucalyptus grandis and Pinus caribea var. hondurensis, and finger joints. They analysed the resistance of the glue line through static bending test, and compared the results with phenol resorcinol formaldehyde adhesive. The ratio used for the preparation of the polyurethane was one part of polyol and one part of pre-polymer isocyanate. The samples were then pressed for 24 hours at room temperature with a pressure of 0.6 MPa. The results showed that castor oil-based adhesive was appropriate to be used in GLULAM, with no failure by shearing stress found along the layer of the glue. The results also indicated good lamination efficiency of the polyurethane adhesive based on castor oil.
Silva e Goncalves (2002) studied the properties of MDF manufactured with wood from reforesting and castor oil-based polyurethane adhesive. The adhesive used had 30% solids and 70% solvents. The MDFs were manufactured using 5% and 10% of adhesive over the active part. The Author’s concluded that the castor oil-based adhesive is appropriate to be used in MDF manufacture.
Dias and Rocco (2004) used castor oil-based polyurethane adhesive in the production of Eucalyptus grandis plywood, indicating that the castor oil-based polyurethane adhesive is a promising glue for the manufacture of plywood. They pressed the plywood at 60 °C for 10 Minutes at 1.2 MPa.
Januario (2010; 2006) studied particleboards made with Bambusa vulgaris bonded with castor oil-based polyurethane; evaluating the physical and mechanical properties. The particleboards had 3 layers, with particle sizes between 0.42 mm – 2.4 mm, where the particle size of the external layers were <0.45 mm. The particleboards were pressed for 10 minutes at 60 °C, under 4 MPa of pressure. The ratio of resin was one part of isocyanate and two parts of polyol; he studied 3 different amounts of resin in the panel, 5% wt, 10% wt and 15% wt. Januario concluded that the resin content related to bamboo particles affects the physical and mechanical properties of the boards, observing the main difference between the particleboards with 5% polyurethane and the particleboards with 10% resin. Januario did not find any significant difference between the physical and mechanical properties of the particleboards with 10% and 15% adhesive. The main properties identified for the particleboards with 15% resin were density (0.66 g/cm3), MOR (8.71 MPa) and IB (0.32 MPa). Both MOR and IB were below average of what is expected for standard particleboards in Brazil (0.35 MPa) (Associacao Brasilieira de Normas Tecnicas, 2002). One factor that could have affected the mechanical properties is that the inefficient conditions in which the particles were manufactured. Januario concluded that if the amount of polyol increases, the particleboard becomes more elastic, and if the amount of the isocyanate is higher, the material becomes stiffer.
Dias, F. M. (2005), similarly to Januario, manufactured three layer particleboards with three different types of Eucalyptus. Dias used a larger particle size than Januario used in his research; the particle size of the external layer was 4 mm and had a nominal thickness of 15 mm, while the particle size of the internal layer was from 6 - 42 mm with a thickness from 15 - 40 mm. The pressing variables were 10 minutes, at 90°C, under 4 MPa pressing pressure. The ratio of resin was one part of isocyanate for two parts of polyol; and the amount of resin used was 10% wt. The results obtained by Dias were improved than the results obtained by Januario; with a MOR of 16.53 MPa, and an
IB of 0.91 MPa. Figure 2.23 displays images of particleboards manufactured with castor oil-based polyurethane.
      Figure 2.23 a. Addition of castor oil based polyurethane resin to bamboo particles; b. Mixing castor oil based resin with bamboo particles; c. Prepressed mat of castor oil based polyurethane with bamboo particles in hot plates press before pressing; d. Castor oil based polyurethane bamboo particleboards (Pereira and Beraldo, 2008)
2.3.4. Summary of suitable resins for sustainable wood composite materials
The aim of this section was to identify the most appropriate recycled or renewable binders to be used as a substitute to UF in particleboards. This section presented an overview of the main resins used in the production of particleboards and wood based composites. In addition, it gave a review of alternative resins that are suitable to be used as matrix or adhesives for more sustainable particleboards and composites. Table 2.5 displays a summary of the most relevant adhesives used in this research.
The three compared resins, guayule rubber, recycled polypropylene, and castor oil- based adhesive resin have been reported to be suitable to be used as a matrix or adhesive in particleboards and wood based composites. In addition they are available or they are feasible to be produced or obtained locally.
The selected adhesives are more expensive than UF, but their prices could get lower, as new developments take place.

The criteria to select the resins was drawn from industrial ecology framework (Frosch and Gallopoulos, 1989; McDonough and Braungart, 2002; Garner and Keoleian, 1995; Kibert et al., 2002; Graham, 2003; Ayres, 1989; Kneese et al., 1970; Keoleian et al., 1995; Grann, 1997; Nattrass and Brian, 1999; Robèrt, 2002). · Increase the energy efficiency: this criterion is applied towards reducing the energy required for setting the composites, when compared with a UF adhesive. Therefore the new selected resins should be pressed at less than 190 °C.
· Extend useful life of products: Since UF resins have no resistance to moisture; standard particleboards swell and break apart when exposed to high moisture environments. In order to accomplish this criterion, particleboards and composites must be moisture resistant and must have similar mechanical performance to standard particleboards. In other words, this criterion aims to find a moisture resistant resin to give an improved performance in high moisture environments.
· Use recyclable or biodegradable materials: the aim of this criterion is to ameliorate the environmental impacts of disposal of building materials. Therefore, resins should be recyclable and/or biodegradable.
· Eliminate toxic materials and by-products: this criteria state that material should not be toxic or contaminating, and no emissions should be generated during their whole life cycle. UF adhesives releases formaldehyde when exposed to moisture, therefore, it is preferred the utilization of a resin that will not release any toxic chemical during their life cycle.

2.4. CONCLUSION OF THE CHAPTER
The objectives of this chapter were to identify the most appropriate agriculture and forestry by-products in Australia to be used as a substitute to pine wood in particleboards, and to identify the most appropriate recycled or renewable binders to be used as a substitute to UF in particleboards.
The utilization of macadamia shells, pine cones and Eucalyptus capsules as fillers in particleboards, is given as an opportunity. These agricultural and forestry by-products are local or already planted in Australia, and have high lignin, extractives and cellulose content to improve the mechanical properties and the resistance to moisture. In addition, these pods carry out a protective function. Therefore, they are adapted to the local weather. While the utilization of castor oil-based polyurethane, recycled polypropylene and guayule rubber also seem as an opportunity for further research in wood composites, since they are non-toxic, moisture resistant, based in renewable, recycled and recyclable sources.
Having selected alternative fillers and resins, the next chapter describes the analytical framework in which this research is based.
CHAPTER 3: LITERATURE REVIEW PART 2
The aim of this chapter is to outline the most relevant environmental impacts of standard particleboards. According with the criteria drawn from industrial ecology framework, the actual production and performance of particleboards denotes serious problems during their entire life cycle. These environmental burdens are present from the monoculture cultivation of the trees to the carcinogenic UF binding resin, including the emissions released during pressing and drying the wood at high temperatures. This chapter is divided in two sections. The first section presents the main problems of particleboards, and the second section is a review of some life cycle inventories of particleboards.
3.1. STANDARD PARTICLEBOARDS
Particleboards were developed during the period of the Second World War, during the 1940’s when, due to the shortage of wood during that period, it was necessary to develop an alternative to timber (Kollmann and Franz, 1968). Particleboards are mostly manufactured from a mix of virgin and recycled softwood chips and sawdust of sizes ranging between 0.5 mm and 4 mm (Poblete and Burgos, 2010). The wood is mostly sourced from sawmills and other wood industries, consisting mainly in lumber processing residues, such as round wood from forest slash of small diameters, edgings, slabs, trimmings, plywood residues, sawdust, planer shavings or chips (Wilson, 2010b). Although particleboards are mainly based on wood residues, manufacturers prefer to use a homogeneous raw virgin material in order to obtain products with good strength, smooth surfaces, and equal swelling. Around 80% of the material used worldwide is radiata pine (Pinus radiata D. Don), followed by black poplar (Populus nigra) and Eucalyptus (Stark et al., 2010).
The following section introduces to some of the most relevant issues identified with standard particleboards.
3.1.1. Sustainable use of timber and timber plantations in Australia
Forests are one of the richest environments on earth for land based species. Forests are vital to global water, carbon and oxygen cycles, since they play a major role as carbon ‘sinks’, and provide economic and social infrastructure for an immeasurable amount of people (Achard, 2009; UNEP, 2007). Unfortunately, the consumption of natural resources has increased to a higher regenerative capacity than can be carried out by the environment and keeps increasing. This issue only fosters the plantation of more monocultives to cover the need of timber.
The average annual production of sawn Australian-grown timber increased from years 2003 to 2008 by 8%, with an average production of 5,2 million m3 (Pink, 2012). That volume was comprised of 20% of hardwood timber, derived from logs harvested from various native forests, predominantly Eucalypts; and 80% of softwood timber, derived from logs harvested from plantations (Pink, 2012), such as radiata pine Pinus radiata D. Don (radiata pine) is not native to Australia, but is the main species planted in Australia. Occupying 741,598 hectares (Bureau of Rural Sciences, 2011; O’Hehir and Nambiar, 2010), and with an average annual production of 3,8 million m3 between 2003 and 2010, radiata plantations have increased by 22% since 2003 (Pink, 2010). Around 20% of coniferous timber in Australia is turned into particleboards (Pink, 2010).
Although monoculture plantations provide a constant supply of wood for consumer’s needs, help to relieve pressures on natural forests and provide a wide range of social, economic and environmental benefits; in some cases, forest plantations can result in negative environmental and social impacts (Graham, 2003; Achard, 2009; Calkins, 2009).
3.1.1.1. Use of herbicides in monoculture tree plantations
One of the causes of alteration of flora and fauna in the forest plantations is the use of herbicides. Some herbicides such as hexazinone (EPA, 2010), simazine, and atrazine (Cobzac et al., 2012) are used for control of annual and perennial weeds, grasses, broadleaf and woody weeds. These weeds are native vegetation in the plantations of
Pinus radiata, Eucalyptus globulus, Eucalyptus regans and Eucalyptus nitens. These herbicides inhibit photosynthesis and are absorbed by the plant through leaves and roots. Most of them remain in the soil at low concentration for up to three years after application and can contaminate ground waters. Some of them have a low toxicity to fish, birds and insects, however they may affect the habitat of some aquatic animals. In addition, hexazinone, simazine and atrazine can cause eye damage to humans and animals, and reversible irritation to eyes, nose and skin, amongst other symptoms (Cobzac et al., 2012; EPA, 2010).
3.1.1.2. Genetic modification
Another cause of alteration of the environmental ecosystems is the genetic modification of trees. Genetic modification lies on inserting genetic material from another species into a plant or modifying the genes of a plant by manipulating the DNA. Genetic modification differs from traditional breeding techniques, which cross plants of the same or closely related species seeking for maintaining and enhancing the best properties of the plants (Lang, 2004). The real worldwide and Australian amount of genetically modified trees plantations is unclear (Plantations 2020, 2003). Nonetheless, some researchers (Gapare et al., 2010; Woods and Woods, 1990; Henderson and Walter, 2006; O’Hehir and Nambiar, 2010) state that in South Australia, planting of genetically modified trees, particularly radiata pine trees, has been taking place since late 1960s, and increased since the early 1970s after the adoption of a system called the ‘Maximum growth sequence’. This system consisted in high applications of herbicides, fertilizers and genetically enhanced seeds, to improve the tree structure, growth speed, quality and uniformity of trees. To date, three generations of radiata pine trees have been already improved through genetic modifications and silviculture. Rotation and length for sawn products has been reduced from 40–45 to 25–30 years in Australia. Meanwhile, the Commonwealth Scientific and Industrial Research Organisation (CSIRO) in Australia have worked on research projects on genetically modified trees, particularly Eucalyptus, targeting faster growth, improved wood quality, and sterility of trees (Lang, 2004). However this research was discontinued around 2003 (World Rainforest Movement, 2008). The “Plantation 2020” programme developed by the Australian government and private forestry industries, assures that no genetically modified commercial plantations
are in Australia today, and they would only proceed once all risks have been scientifically assessed (Plantations 2020, 2003).
While plantations of radiata pine species already exist, the ideal situation would be to use trees more efficiently. Previous studies state that only 50% of a cut tree is used as timber or recovered (Magin, 2002; Leckoundzou et al., 2010). Further research could include the utilization of additional elements of the tree, therefore it would not be necessary to plant more and more trees to deal with the increasing demand of the building market, and pressure on forest would be reduced.
3.1.1.3. Certification of timber
Conserving forests has become a key weapon in the fight to reduce carbon emissions and slow down the climate change. Therefore, the Forest Stewardship Council (FSC) was formed in 1992 in an effort to validate the claims of timber certifiers and to avoid confusion in the market. The goal of the FSC is to promote environmentally responsible, social beneficial and economically viable management of the world's forests. One strategy is to establish broad based guidelines designed to result in well managed forests. FSC encourages the development of national and /or regional working groups, which help the development of forest management standards and are responsible for recommending these standards to the FSC for approval. It also involves third party auditing of the developed standards (Magin, 2002; Calkins, 2009). FSC is not the only timber existent certification scheme. However, an analysis of the various timber certification schemes reveals that the Forest Stewardship Council (FSC) is the most rigorous, robust, and environmentally desirable certification, due to the strict criteria followed (Clark and Kozar, 2011; Greenpeace, 2009; Ecospecifier global, 2013).
3.1.2. Particleboards and emissions
3.1.2.1. Volatile organic compounds
Volatile Organic Compounds (VOC) includes several hundred of organic chemical gases from certain solids or liquids with boiling point ranges from 50 °C to 260 °C. In
wood based panels, these are released by drying the wood particles and during hot- pressing at high temperatures. In the wood particles, VOCs can be attributed to wood extractives, including terpenes, and aldehydes; and in the resins, they can be mainly found in thinners and adhesives. The most commonly found VOCs in timber products are formaldehyde, methanol, phenol and toluene (Baumann et al., 2000; Wang et al., 2003).
The city of Sydney, in its Medium Density Residential DCP - IEQ-10 Volatile organic compounds (2008), estimates that in Australia alone an excess of 80,000 tons of VOCs are released into the atmosphere annually with the existing technology. VOC emissions have been linked to ozone destruction, photochemical smog and the greenhouse effect (Calkins, 2009; Baumann et al., 2000).
3.1.2.2. Formaldehyde
Formaldehyde (HCHO) is the simplest and most common aldehyde and is found in low concentrations in our environment as a consequence of natural processes. At normal ambient temperatures it is a transparent gas with a strong odour. It boils at 21 0C and melts at 92 0C (Calkins, 2009).
Bonding in most conventional wood based composites is provided by thermosetting (heat curing) adhesive resins, such as UF, with curing temperatures that vary from 130 °C to 195 °C. These resins are the least expensive thermosetting resin of all wood adhesives; they are non-flammable, strong, have a very rapid cure rate, and are light in colour (Azambuja and Dias, 2006; Dias and Rocco Lahr, 2004; Nascimento et al., 2008; Silva et al., 2006; Stark et al., 2010; Frihart, 2005). UF adhesives are the predominant adhesives for interior grade plywood, particleboards and medium density fibreboards (MDF). UF adhesive can only be used in interior applications in furniture, flooring, or walls, because moisture and excessive heat exposure leads to a breakdown of the bond forming reactions, and carcinogenic formaldehyde fumes continues to emerge from the adhesive (Graham, 2003; Pizzi, 2003; Community-COST Concertation Committee, 1990; Ebewele et al., 1993; Frihart, 2005). The formaldehyde emissions are high initially because of unreacted formaldehyde. Unreacted formaldehyde is a problem
during the manufacturing operation of particleboards and in new produced composites, resulting in new and recently renovated buildings with higher amounts of formaldehyde than older buildings (Ebewele et al., 1993; Frihart, 2005). Formaldehyde emissions decrease with time, but do not disappear (Marutzky, 1989).
The health effects of exposure to gaseous formaldehyde are irritation of eyes, nose and throat, lacrimation, sneezing, coughing, nausea and dyspnoea (Community-COST Concertation Committee, 1990). The International Agency for Research on Cancer (IARC) classifies formaldehyde as a Category 1- known human carcinogen (Van Langenberg et al., 2010). After this classification several countries have introduced regulations in MDF, particleboards, and oriented strand board (OSB) in reducing formaldehyde emission levels from products bonded with urea-formaldehyde adhesive resins. For example, Europe led to the E-l standard limiting formaldehyde emissions to be less than 8 mg/100g (milligrams per 100 grams of dry matter) (GECA, 2007; Community-COST Concertation Committee, 1990; Conner, 1996). In China the recommendation is that formaldehyde emissions should be less or equal to 9 mg/100g (Conner, 1996), and Danish regulations stipulate that the maximum level of free formaldehyde should be less than 25 mg/100g dry matter in the panel, determined with the perforator method (EPA, 2011). The emissions requirements for particleboards in Australia are described by the ‘Good Environmental Choice’ organization (GECA). GECA requires for engineered wood products or other natural material panels or products to comply with less than 1% w/w of free formaldehyde in glues, according to the Australian Standard AS/NZS 4266.16-Method 16: Formaldehyde Emission- Desiccator Method (GECA, 2007). Internationally accepted test method EN 120 for particle and MDF boards (CEN, 1994) may be accepted if the product meets an emission limit of < 9 mg/100g (GECA, 2007).
Since health concerns related to formaldehyde exposure have driven formaldehyde emission reductions in countries with strict environmental controls, formaldehyde/urea ratios in current products have been decreased. Lowering resin ratios has respectively been reflected in the decrease of the strength of commercial products. This issue has made industries to consider their replacement with alternative binders that do not contain formaldehyde (Dunky, 2003; Frihart, 2005; Van Langenberg et al., 2010).
3.2. LIFE CYCLE ASSESSMENT
This section of the chapter is a literature review of the environmental impacts of the manufacture process of particleboards.
Life Cycle Assessment (LCA) is a product oriented, integrative, scientific and quantitative tool to evaluate the effects that a product has on the environment over the entire period of its life cycle. An LCA determines the full energy, materials consumption and waste emissions to air, water and land for a product (Kibert et al., 2002). The aim of an LCA is to suggest more sustainable forms of production and consumption, providing objective information for decision making process. Even if an LCA is one of the most complete sustainability tools, it has its constraints. One of the problems of LCA is the shifting of an issue from one product to another. For example, contaminated organic wastes from households that are recycled through agriculture can contaminate food as a result, and that issue is not considered in an LCA. Other problems are using imprecise, old information or wrong assumptions (UNEP, 1996).
The standards for LCA are the International Organization for Standardization (ISO) 14040 and 14044 protocols (ISO, 2006b; ISO, 2006a). In addition, in US the Consortium for Research on Renewable Industrial Materials (CORRIM) has developed guidelines and formats to perform an LCA (Wilson, 2010a).
The Australian Life Cycle Inventory Initiative (AusLCI) has been working towards the manufacture of a Life cycle inventory (LCI) database of a wide range of products and services, and will include wood based products (Tucker et al., 2008). Unfortunately, this database is still under construction and will be available in the short term. At the moment, the only available database in AusLCI is for electricity (AusLCI, 2011). The only result of LCA of a particleboard in Australia was found for Laminex standard particleboard, developed by Ecospecifier global (2013). The LCA obtained a score of 0.73 Ecospecifier EcoPoints, on a scale of 0 to 1 where 0 is neutral and 1 is the worst case business and usual (Ecospecifier global, 2013). This detailed study certifies the standard particleboard as level A-very low formaldehyde emissions (E0). The fibres used in these particleboards are certified from AFS/PEFC certified plantations.
However, the LCA study reports that the product is not able to be exposed to high moisture conditions. Laminex Standard Particleboards are based on <20% UF resin and >80% dry wood particles. While these particleboards have a forest certification, the fibre sources are not certified by the Forestry Stewardship Council (FSC). Therefore their certification is not quite impartial (Ecospecifier global, 2013). Ecospecifier (2013) reported that the wood particles of this product comprises 50% post-industrial recycled content and sourced from Queensland Government plantations and 2% urban waste. Although the recycled content minimises the impacts associated with timber, timber and wood products can be amongst the most environmentally destructive elements from a biodiversity and ecological perspective, as previously explained.
The steps of the LCA are inventory analysis, impact assessment and improvement assessment (Tucker et al., 2008). The inventory analysis consists in collecting the data, identifying and quantifying the environmental loads involved quantified for each process involved in the product life cycle. Some of these loads are energy, raw materials, and emissions to the atmosphere, water, and land. (UNEP, 1996). The results of the inventory analysis are interpreted in terms of the impact they have on the environment.
The impact assessment consists of three steps: classification, characterization and valuation. While the classification step is qualitative, the characterization step quantifies the environmental problems. In addition, the valuation step is an overall comparison of the environmental problems to which each product contributes. The effects of resource use and emissions are generated and quantified in impact categories. The most common impact categories are embedded into three conditions: human health (HH), ecosystem quality (EQ) and resources (R) (UNEP, 1996). Finally, the improvement assessment is the evaluation of the needs and opportunities to reduce the impact of the products to the environment, by processing the data, and adjusting the inputs and outputs to values related with functional unit (UNEP, 1996).
3.2.1. Life cycle inventory of a particleboard
This section presents a review of data from life cycle inventories of particleboard found in literature. One of these studies, developed by Wilson (2010b; 2008b), was based on ISO 14040 (2006a) and ISO 14044 (2006b) standards and on the guidelines from CORRIM (Briggs, 2001). These standards and guideline were used for the methodology, analyses, and for reporting the components, using Simapro LCA software. The data was collected through surveys to 5 particleboard manufacturers in US for the production year 2004 (Wilson, 2008b). While the system boundaries were on-site and cradle-to-gate, the data quality was determined by mass and energy balances. The defined functional unit was 1 m3 of particleboard. The particleboards consisted of 90% wood and 9.2% UF in addition to other additives (Wilson, 2008b). Figure 3.1 shows the life cycle of a particleboard and the system boundaries considered by Wilson.
 Figure 3.1 The life cycle of particleboard and its cradle-to-product gate life-cycle inventory system boundary (Wilson, 2010b).
Other study used as a reference in this chapter was developed by Rivela et al. (2006), using the International Organization for Standardization (ISO) standards; and the Ecoindicator 99 methodology. This study was based in a particleboard manufacturer
plant in Spain. The systems boundaries considered all the activities taking place inside the particleboard manufacturing plant, and the activities related with the production of the chemicals used in the process, energy inputs and transport. The data was obtained by directly on-site measurements.
The main difference between these two studies is that their boundaries systems are substantially different, since Rivela et al. study does not consider the environmental impacts associated with the growing, harvesting, and processing of all wood inputs to the manufacturing process, ignoring the burdens associated with the preparation of the wood, or the original moisture content of wood. The only burden associated with wood is the transportation. Wilson found in his study that wood contributes 32% of the total cradle to gate energy. Besides those issues, all other data for on-site LCI is quite comparable for the two studies.
3.2.2. The process
The process steps considered by Rivela et al. include transport, storage, screening, refining, drying, blending, forming, hot pressing, panel cooling, sawing and sanding. Particulate emissions from stages such as refining, sawing and sanding are collected and reused back in the screening process or as a fuel source (Rivela et al., 2006). The system boundaries and process chain of particleboard production are shown in figure 3.2.
 Figure 3.2 System boundaries and process chain of particleboard production (Rivela et al., 2006)
The manufacturing process of a particleboard is usually conformed by three main subsystems: wood preparation, board shaping and board finishing. The first step consists in the wood preparation. Once the wood arrives to the plant, it is refined, classified and dried. Once the wood is dried, it is blended with binding agents and pressed in a hot plates press. This process is the subsystem of board shaping. After the panel is conformed, the particleboard is cut and sanded into the final product. This process is called subsystem of board finishing. Supplementary subsystems considered by Rivela et al. (2006) are the transport, chemicals used and energy consumption. Rivela et al. (2006) considered three main scenarios for the final disposal of the particleboard: landfill, energetic use and recycling. Figure 3.3 shows the flow sheet of the manufacture of particleboard defined by Wilson (2008b).
 Wood
Figure 3.3 Process flow for the production of particleboard (Wilson, 2010b).
3.2.2.1. Subsystem of wood preparation
The wood is transported to the mill by truck. Once in the plant the wood is arranged by geometry and moisture content (MC). The original MC in wood can range from 10 – 100% of the oven dry weight (wt) of wood. Since the moisture content is obtained from the difference of the original weight of the wood and the dry weight of the wood, if the weight of the water contained inside of the wood is higher than the weight of the wood, the moisture content will be 100% or more. After the wood is sorted, it is stored undercover (Wilson, 2010b).
The wood preparation subsystem involves the following processes:
Debarking: The bark is removed from the wood in debarking drums. The removed bark is then sent to the boiler for energy recovery.
Particle production: Since the quality of the final product is highly dependent on the
shape and the moisture content of the wood particles, this is a crucial process. The wood 83
 
is cut in shaving machines and chippers into chips of a desired particle size, usually from 0.5-4 mm long, and 0.2-0.5 mm thick (Poblete and Burgos, 2010). The oversize particles are then returned to the chippers for further breakdown.
Screening: The wood particles are sieved in a set of screens that separate particles by size. The particles with the desired size are utilized in the board and undersized particles, are either included in the board, or used as fuel for the dryers. The emissions from these particles are collected by baghouses and cyclones (Wilson, 2010b).
Storage and measurement of particles: The sorted particles are stored in silos by size and moisture content. Since the particleboard is manufactured with a three-layer structure, the finer particles are left for the external layers. These external layers also have higher moisture content than the core. The coarser particles are used in the internal layer (Wilson, 2010b; Rivela et al., 2006).
Drying: The particles are dried in rotary dryers down to 3-5% (wt) MC, depending on whether the particles will be used for face or core layers. The drying process is feed with natural gas from the burners, while some dryers also use the wood wastes from other processes as sources of fuel (Rivela et al., 2006). When wood dries at high temperatures in the dryers, VOCs and particulates are released as emissions. These emissions usually go to cyclones and control devices such as regenerative thermal oxidizers (RTOs), regenerative catalytic oxidizers (RCOs), and biofilters (Wilson, 2010b) to reduce particulate and chemical emissions.
Boiler and oil heater: Air emissions of CO2 and CO are generated with the heat produced in the boilers and oil heaters and sent to control devices such as baghouses, cyclones, biofilters, RTO, and RCO (Wilson, 2010b; Rivela et al., 2006).
3.2.2.2. Subsystem of board shaping
The board shaping subsystem involves the following processes:
Urea Formaldehyde resins: Wilson (2010a), in his study of a life cycle inventory (LCI)
of the production of UF resins, considered on-site and cradle-to-gate system boundaries, 84

with a functional unit of 1 kg of resin. He described that the main components to produce formaldehyde in a reactor are methanol (CHOH), and a catalyst. Methanol is the simplest of alcohols, which is produced from natural gas feedstock. After formaldehyde is obtained, formaldehyde with urea (NH2)2CO are combined in a separate reaction mixes. Urea is produced from ammonia, which is based on natural gas feedstock, to obtain the UF resin product.
Resin mixing: UF resin has a solid content of about 65%. This resin is normally modified by adding paraffin wax to improve moisture resistance. Once the resin and the particles are set, the resin is sprayed onto the particles (Rivela et al., 2006). The adhesive is usually applied in a proportion of around 10% of the total composite dry weight.
Mat forming: After the resin is mixed with the fibres, the mat is formed. The glued particles fall onto a conveyor or a tray. Each layer is formed separately, and distributed into a flat mat in three or five layers (Rivela et al., 2006). The mats are pre-pressed before the hot pressing to reduce their thickness (Rivela et al., 2006).
Hot pressing: The mats are then transported to the hot press for pressing and curing. The press consists of a multiopening press which closes simultaneously. A multiopening press is a press with several parallel hot plates which can press many particleboards at the same time. The curing process of the resin commences with temperatures of 190– 220 oC, and pressures between 2 to 3 MPa (Rivela et al., 2006). As a result of the high temperatures and curing process, emissions of particulates, VOCs, hazardous air pollutants (HAPs) and other related emissions are released (Wilson, 2010b).
3.2.2.3. Subsystems of board finishing
The board finishing subsystem involves the following processes:
Cooling: The hot panels are cooled on a cooling wheel at 30 oC, to stabilise, condition, and fully cure the resin. This measure also enables the temperature of the panels to
decrease below a value at which UF resin could start to break down and emit formaldehyde (Wilson, 2010b; Rivela et al., 2006).
Sanding: The next step is to sand the board, in order to reduce it to the desired thickness and smoothness. The particulate emissions are sent to baghouses and cyclones, and can either be recycled back and added to the thinner layers of new particleboards before the forming process or used as fuel for the dryers (Rivela et al., 2006).
Sawing: Panels are sawn to dimensions of panel width and length. The panel trimmings are ground into particles and used as fuel in the boiler. Particulate emissions are addressed by baghouses and cyclones (Wilson, 2010b).
Final Product Storage: Finished particleboards are stored under adequate conditions of temperature and moisture (Rivela et al., 2006), then are stacked and prepared for distribution.
3.2.3. Inputs and outputs
Table 3.1 exhibits a resume of the inputs and outputs for the production of 1 m3 of particleboard. The outputs for the production of particleboard include a small quantity (5.2%) of wood fuel sold to other mills and emissions to air, water and land. The emissions to air include particulates less than 10 mm in size (PM10) from the processes of refining, drying, sawing, and sanding. Other air emissions include VOCs and hazardous air pollutants (HAPs) from the processes of drying, pressing, and panel cooling; the HAPs are acetaldehyde, acrolein, formaldehyde and methanol (Wilson, 2010b). Raw materials and emissions for a cradle-to-gate inventory are greater than those resources and emissions that occur at the particleboard production site (Wilson, 2010b). For example, the onsite energy used to produce UF resin is 0.394 MJ/kg compared with 29.35 MJ/kg resin for cradle-to-gate; emissions to air such as particulates, CO2 and CO represent a 1%; and methanol a 3% for the on-site compared with the cradle-to-gate values.

3.2.3.1. Carbon content
The carbon content of wood is 52.4% w/w on average (Wilson, 2010b). Considering that the amount of wood in 1 m3 of particle board is 672 kg, this means that 1 m3 of particleboard contains 363 kg of carbon. To produce 1 m3 of particleboard, the emissions of carbon dioxide to the air are 1,532 kg. These emissions are due to the wood drying in the different processes of particleboard production, and they can be used to offset CO2 emissions from wood and fossil fuel use, resulting in a difference between the inputs and outputs of about 5% with more carbon flow out than in. Then the carbon store is treated as a negative value when determining the carbon flux (Wilson, 2008a). There is also carbon stored in other particleboard components of UF resin (25.4% w/w), wax (85%), and urea scavenger (20%), but these carbon stores are not counted in the
carbon flux values because they are derived from fossil feedstock of oil or natural gas 87
   
(Wilson, 2010a). Most of the energy in these studies, is supplied by natural gas (47.1%), followed by wood fuel (22.2%), oil (14.9%), and coal (13.1%) (Wilson, 2010b).
3.2.4. Impacts of particleboard production
As previously reported, the impact assessment of an LCA consists of three steps: classification, characterisation and valuation. The results for the characterisation step and damage assessment reported by Rivela et al. are shown in Table 3.2.
Rivela et al., (2006) considered three conditions for the classification step, presenting the environmental problem from a qualitative perspective, with an estimation of an environmental indicator for each category or issue. The three conditions were human health (HH), ecosystem quality (EQ), and resources (R). The human health condition expresses the damages in disability adjusted life years (DALY), while ecosystem quality expresses the damages as potentially disappeared fraction (PDF) and potentially affected fraction of species due to an environmental impact. The results of the impact assessment of this study were obtained with the Ecoindicator 99 methodology.
The characterisation step quantifies the environmental problem, and analyses the contribution of the different subsystems of the particleboard production process to the impact categories, essential to detect the 'hot spots'. The predominant hot spots of the subsystems of particleboard production identified by Rivela et al. are:
Wood preparation subsystem: The emissions of the gas coming from the wood driers were found to be the most significant contributors to the ozone layer category (Rivela et al., 2006). The negative values were obtained since bark and wood wastes were used as a fuel.
UF manufacture: The land use category was the most affected by the natural gas consumption.
Board shaping subsystem: The most significant impact identified was by natural gas consumption for board shaping. The land use category was mostly impacted, becoming a great contributor to the damage to EQ.
Board finishing subsystem: This subsystem was reported to raise a high contribution to the categories of carcinogens, respiratory organics, respiratory inorganics and climate change, which accounts for a large damage to HH. But the greatest impacts were in the categories of ecotoxicity and acidification/eutrophication. This was also reported as the subsystem most dependent in electricity consumption (Rivela et al., 2006).

DALY ·10 PDF·m2yr
            The most relevant environmental burdens of standard particleboards were outlined in this chapter. The identified risks and problems are related with the prime sources, processing emissions and performance. The life cycle environmental burdens related with the prime sources are the fibre source from monoculture plantations and the risks of the carcinogen formaldehyde based resin, in addition to the low resistance to moisture which releases more formaldehyde.
The most relevant impacts of the production of a particleboard are related with the high emissions of CO2 and VOCs released in the processes of wood drying, pressing at high
temperatures and finishing. These emissions affect the ozone layer, land use and human health categories. In addition, the emissions of particulates and PM10 from the processes of refining, drying, sawing, and sanding affect the human health category. Other air emissions include formaldehyde.
The performance parameters drawn from industrial ecology, with the identified issues of standard particleboards, are presented in Table 3.3.
The industrial ecology framework states that
· Local materials should be used: this criterion was approached considering two issues. These are the transportation of the wood and the source of the fillers. Standard particleboard can be local or imported to Australia, and if the production is local, the main filler is pine wood. This tree is non-native to Australia, but it has been already grown in monoculture plantations. This issue can be addressed with the selection of a filler that will not encourage planting more trees.
· Use of wastes as resources and maintain biodiversity: This criteria are related with the fillers and the poor optimization of only 50% of the cut tree from monoculture plantations. This issue can be targeted by using alternative fillers that promotes the optimization of the tree.
· Use of renewable resources and eliminate toxic materials and by-products: this criteria aims to improve the environmental impacts related to the resin and the manufacture process. The UF adhesive used in standard particleboards is based in petroleum; in addition, emissions of formaldehyde, VOC and CO2 are released during manufacture and use. This criterion states the need of decreasing emissions by processing at low temperatures, and using materials without formaldehyde.
· Increase the energy efficiency: As previously presented, this criterion concerns to the issue related with the processing energy of the standard particleboards. One significant factor is that wood requires intensive drying to achieve the targeted moisture content of 6% (wt). Another factor is the high temperature required for
setting the UF resin in standard particleboards (190 0C). These high temperatures 90

require high energy. In addition, lignin and extractives in wood are degraded, releasing VOC and CO2 emissions. Pressing the particleboard at high temperature also causes formaldehyde emissions.
· Extend useful life of products: This criterion targets the issue of the low performance of standard particleboards in high moisture environments. This issue shortens the life cycle of these materials. Particleboards have a poor resistance to moisture since the pine wood has a higher amount of cellulose and hemicellulose which causes high water absorption and thickness swelling. In addition UF adhesive breaks down and releases formaldehyde.
· Use recyclable or biodegradable materials: This criteria aims to target the end of life of particleboards. The current standard particleboards are non-recyclable, since their components cannot be separated or reused.
