To this point, the performance criteria for more sustainable particleboards or composites have been stated in chapter two and the most important environmental burdens of the current practice have been reported in this chapter.
The next step is to manufacture the new proposed panels, and evaluate experimentally if they can be used to manufacture panel materials. The next chapter presents the experimental research methods to achieve that objective.
CHAPTER 4: MATERIALS AND METHODS
This chapter presents the experimental research methods used in the experimental section of this research. The first part of this chapter is to establish the methods used to manufacture the composites and particleboards with the new proposed alternative constituents. The second objective is to define the evaluation method for the physical and mechanical performance of the new proposed composites, in order to compare their physical and mechanical performance with the current practice.
4.1. PREPARATION AND ANALYSIS OF THE MACADAMIA SHELLS, PINE CONES AND EUCALYPTUS CAPSULES
The experimental part of this research commenced with the preparation of the fibres, in addition to the chemical, particle size and microscopy analysis of the fibres.
The term ‘fibre’ is a generic name used for all cellulosic materials explored in this research; however, the technical name in regards to the size of the fibres is particles. Both names are used as synonyms.
The cracked macadamia shells were shipped from the Macadamia Processing Co. Limited, located in Lismore, New South Wales, Australia. The species of pines cones and Eucalyptus capsules selected were Pinus radiata D. Don (radiata pine) and Eucalyptus globulus, respectively; these were collected from plantations in Concepción, Chile. The wood fibre used was Pinus radiata D. don which was obtained in the form of sawdust and was provided by the Laboratory of Composites Materials (LMC), University of Bío-Bío, in Concepción, Chile. This sawdust was as a by-product from a panel door manufacturing industry in Concepción, Chile.
It is relevant to indicate that this research originally considered the utilization of agricultural and forestry by-products from Australia and Latin America, and was reframed later to Australia. Therefore, some of the sources used in this research were obtained in Chile. This fact does not affect the results since these same fibres and resins are also available in Australia
4.1.1. Preparation of the fibres (grinding, drying, sieving)
Prior to manufacture the particleboards, the macadamia shells and wood fibres were ground in LMC, Chile, using a Ming Lee Strong Crusher; model ML-SC, with a sieve opening of 3 mm. No further particle refining was done after the grinding. The size of the screen opening was chosen after previous research confirming that 40 mesh is an effective particle size for extruded wood-plastic composites (Klysov, 2007; Migneault et al., 2009; Wechsler and Hiziroglu, 2007; Ballerini et al., 2005a; Ballerini et al., 2008; Ballerini et al., 2005b). Considering the recycled PP selected for this research is recommended for extrusion, and commercial applications are mostly extruded (Sanchez, 2005), this size of particle seemed appropriate for the research. Regarding the particleboards with castor oil-based polyurethane, Januario and Beraldo (2006; 2010) had previously used similar particle size in particleboards manufactured with castor oil adhesive and bamboo particles with acceptable results. Similar particle size has also been used in pine cones bonded with UF particleboards with positive results (Büyüksarı et al., 2010). Since no studies were found in literature in regards of mixing guayule rubber with wood fibres; the same particle size was used for all mixes in order to have some uniformity in the methods.
The ground fibres were then dried in LMC, in a muffle furnace overnight at 100 oC to reduce the moisture content to one percent (dry weight) for the samples with a polypropylene matrix (Dundar et al., 2010; Ayrilmis et al., 2010; Ballerini et al., 2005b; Ballerini et al., 2005a; Wolcott, 2001); and at least a moisture content of four percent (based on dry weight) for the panels based in castor oil (Stark et al., 2010a). The cracked and ground macadamia shells are shown in Figure 4.1; the radiata pine cones and Eucalyptus capsules, before and after grinding are shown in Figures 4.2 and 4.3.
     Figure 4.1 a. Cracked macadamia shells; b. Ground macadamia shells. Author.
       Figure 4.2 a. Radiata pine cone in tree; b. Cut radiata pine cones; c. Ground pine cones. Author.
     Figure 4.3 a. Eucalyptus globulus capsule in tree; b. Ground capsules. Author. 4.1.2. Chemical content and density of the fibres
The chemical composition of the macadamia nut shells, pine cones and Eucalyptus capsules was determined using the standards from the Technical Association of the Pulp and Paper Industry (TAPPI), after reviewing in literature previous studies on plant fibres used in plant based composites materials (Çöpür et al., 2007; Gonultas, 2008;
Eroglu; Alma et al., 2005; Fengel and Wegener, 1983; Güler et al., 2008; Iñiguez- Covarrubias et al., 2001). The test method T264 om-88-Preparation of wood for chemical analysis was used to determine the moisture content (TAPPI, 1988). The test methods T9 wd-75-Holocellulose in wood (TAPPI, 1975); T203 os-74-Alpha-Beta, and Gamma-Cellulose in Pulp (TAPPI, 1974) were used to define the cellulose content. The test method T222 om-02-Insoluble Lignin in wood and pulp (TAPPI, 2002c) was used to define the amount of lignin of the fibres. The test method T12 om-02-One Percent Sodium Hydroxide Solubility of Wood and Pulp to measure the ethanol-toluene soluble including extractives (TAPPI, 2002b) was used to evaluate the amount of extractives in the fibres. The test methods T207 cm-99-Water solubility of wood and pulp (TAPPI, 1999), and T211 om-02-Ash in wood, pulp, paper and paperboard (TAPPI, 2002a) were used to determine the water solubility and ash content of the fibres. Pentosan was measured by house method. The fibres were sent ground and dried. All the tests were carried out in duplicate by the Forest Research Institute, in Malaysia (FRIM).
The densities of the ground macadamia shells, pine cones, and Eucalyptus capsules were measured in a gas pycnometer. The gas pycnometer uses the Archimedes displacement method as a principle to measure the density of ground fibres, soil or grains (Chang, 1988; Bielders et al., 1990). It operates by detecting the pressure change resulting from displacement of a gas by a solid object (Webb, 2001). The equipment used was a Micromeritics, model AccuPyc II1340, serial number 2317, with a 100 cc sample holder, and three chambers. The cycles used were 10-10.
The aim of these analyses was to define the influence of the chemical composition and density of the fibres over the physical and mechanical properties of the composites.
4.1.3. Analysis of particle size distribution
An analysis of the particle size of the fibres resulting from the grinding process was carried out. Although the proposed production process was to grind the shells, cones and capsules, and use them immediately after passing through the 3 mm sieve of the grinder without further sieving, an accurate definition of the main size ratio of the resulting particles after grinding was required. The particle size analysis was carried out 96

based in the ASTM standard D6913-04 Standard test methods for particle-size distribution (gradation) of soils using sieve analysis (2009), with a few modifications. The analysis consisted in weighting an oven dry mass of 250 g of each fibre after grinding, and then sieving them through sieves of 10 mesh (2 mm or 2,000 microns), 40 mesh (0.42 mm or 420 microns), 60 mesh (0.25 mm or 250microns), and 100 mesh (0.149 mm or 149 microns), during 15 min. The material retained in the thinner mesh sieve of each division was weighted and the percentage in relation of the original amount of material was calculated.
4.2. PREPARATION OF THE PROPOSED COMPOSITES AND PARTICLEBOARDS
The polypropylene (PP) used as a matrix material was PP homopolymer obtained from recycled sacks, in the form of pellets of 3 mm diameter. It was also provided by LMC. This plastic was supplied to LMC by an industry partner of LMC, called Reprocesa (2012), located in Coronel, Chile. The type of plastic was a recycled polypropylene homopolymer obtained from maxi sacks. The code number of the virgin plastic is PH0320, and it is used for raffia extrusion (Sanchez, 2005). The main physical and mechanical properties of the virgin polypropylene produced by Petroquím Chile, the manufacturer of the virgin resin, PH0320 were reported in chapter 2.
The castor oil-based resin was supplied by the chemical industry Kehl Industria e Comercio, located in Saõ Carlos, Brazil (2007). The resin consisted in two components: a polyol based in castor oil and an isocyanate (MDI). The UF adhesive was provided by LMC. This resin was supplied to LMC by an industry partner, manufacturer of industrial particleboards and medium density fibreboards, in the Bío-Bío region, Chile. Yulex Latex was obtained from Yulex Corporation, located in Arizona, United States. The rubber consisted in two components, a compounded Guayule latex emulsion and a gelling agent called sodium silicofluoride (SSF) normally used in the production of open cell latex foam (Williams, 2009).
Due to the different nature of each of the matrix used, each set of composites preparation required a different process. A schematic diagram with the experimental 97

procedure from the preparation of the fibres to the testing is displayed in Figures 4.4 for the samples prepared with guayule rubber, 4.5 for the plastic based samples, and 4.6 for the polyurethane based samples. These procedures are explained in detail in the following sections.

4.2.1. Manufacture of composites with guayule rubber matrix
As previously mentioned in chapter 2, guayule rubber is mostly used in applications like gloves or thin sheets; and the utilization with natural fibre or particle fillers is still at a very basic, laboratory testing scale. This type of experimental procedure is still under development by the suppliers of the guayule rubber Yulex (Yulex, 2009). And no academic publications or further literature has been found. The process design was defined following the recommendations from the manufacturer and adapting them to the available equipment. The set variables were 60% fibre content, and 40% Yulex latex. The latex was in a ratio of 70% guayule and 30% gelling agent. To prepare the mix, the fibres were first soaked in water until saturated; then the excess of water was decanted and squeezed until obtaining wet slurry. The guayule and the gelling agent were then carefully mixed, avoiding high shear mixing to prevent coagulation. Finally, the wet fibres were slowly added (Williams, 2009).

Normally, guayule latex is processed by calender; however, since having no access to a calender machine, instead of calender the samples the recommendation of the supplier was to press the mix for 20 min at 105 °C in a hot plates press with cooling system, opening it occasionally to release the moisture. The material was moulded inside of a 190 mm x 170 mm x 3 mm metallic frame. After the 20 min pressing, they were left in a wood drying oven between two wooden boards, to continue drying at a low temperature (60 °C) for 24 h (Williams, 2009).                                         4.2.2. Manufacture of composites with recycled polypropylene matrix
4.2.2.1. Mixing
The dried ground fibres and the PP were mixed at 165 °C at 75 rpm in a Haacke Polydrive internal rotating mixer Rheomix 600 for 5 min. First, the polypropylene was put into the mixer at 75 rpm, at a temperature of 165 °C for two mins. This was followed by adding the fibres to the molten PP and mixing them for another 3 min. Due to the limited net volume capacity of the chamber of 69 cm3, and the recommendation of the supplier of no filling it up with more than 70% of its capacity, each batch was calculated at 50 grams of fibre plastic composite, considering an average target density of the composites at 1 g/cm3. It was then required to prepare 2 batches of each composite before pressing to fill the pressing frame of 96.9 cm3 (Thermo Haacke, 2001), raised in the next section. Figure 4.7a below shows the mixer used for material manufacture.
4.2.2.2. Pressing
The mixed samples were then pressed in a hot plate press with a water cooling system are shown in Figure 4.7b. The size of the plates was 200 mm by 200 mm. The mixes were pressed inside of a metallic frame with interior dimensions of 170 mm x 190 mm x 3 mm between two aluminium sheets to avoid the mixes to stick in the plates of the press. Each batch was pressed using a temperature of 170 °C for 15 min, and cooled down in the same hot plates press with water cooling system while the samples were still under compression (Wechsler and Hiziroglu, 2007; Ballerini et al., 2008). Both procedures were defined after reviewing previous researches that had homogenous mixes and positive results with radiata pine wood and virgin polypropylene (Ballerini et al., 2008; Wechsler and Hiziroglu, 2007).
Figure 4.7 a. Haacke Polydrive Rheomix 600; b. Hot plates press with cooling system. Author.
4.2.2.3. Formulations of recycled polypropylene based samples
The 60% filler ratio chosen in the current study is based on the work of Wolcott (2001) and on previous studies from this author (2004; 2005a; 2005b; 2007; 2008), who successfully used filler ratios of 60% in their wood-plastic composites (WPC). Three different panel material combinations were made: recycled PP matrix with macadamia shells (PP-MS), pine cones (PC-PP) and Eucalyptus capsules (EC-PP) fibre fillers. The properties of each of these composites were compared with a panel manufactured from recycled PP with radiata pine wood fibre filler (RPW-PP) and a panel of 100% recycled polypropylene (PP). Table 4.2 shows the make-up of each of the five panels prepared.

4.2.3. Manufacture of castor oil-based polyurethane particleboards
After reviewing previous studies in which castor oil-based polyurethane was used as a matrix in fibre based composites with positive results (Januario, 2006; Januario and Beraldo, 2010; Azambuja et al.; Jesus, 2000; Silva and Goncalves, 2002; Dias and Rocco Lahr, 2004; Azambuja and Dias, 2006; Silva et al., 2006; Rebolo Lapo and Beraldo, 2008; Azambuja, 2006), and recommendations from the manufacturer (Kehl, 2011b; Kehl, 2011a), the polyurethane was prepared by weighting the castor oil-based polyol and the MDI isocyanate in separate containers, and hand mixing both components in the ratio 2:1 w/w until the colour was homogeneous. Figure 4.8 presents both components of the castor oil-based polyurethane.
40
 Figure 4.8 Polyol and isocyanate of castor oil-based polyurethane used in this research. Author.
4.2.3.1. Mixing
Januario and Beraldo (2010; 2006) reported in their studies that there was not a significant statistical difference in the physical and mechanical performance of a particleboard with 10 or 15% resin when compared with 5%. The original amount of resin considered to be used in this research was 10%, but after the first approach of manufacturing a particleboard with radiata pine wood and castor oil-based adhesive with 10% resin, it was decided to increase the amount of resin. The cause of this decision was the low compacting and looseness of the fibres in order to improve the performance. The final amount of resin used was 20% polyurethane (weight based).
The mixed resin was then added to the ground fibres and combined by hand in a mixing pot to form slurry in a 20% weight ratio of resin. Figure 4.9 shows particles of Eucalyptus capsules being weighted before mixing.
 Figure 4.9 Particles of Eucalyptus capsules being weighted before mixing. Author. 4.2.3.2. Prepressing
The composite slurry was pre-pressed before final hot pressing. The prepressing is done to attain homogeneity in the distribution of the particles and to reduce the thickness of the mat. The slurry was laid an equally distributed on an aluminium sheet with a protective Teflon sheet in between the mix and the aluminium sheet, to avoid the panel getting stuck on the pressing tray. The mat was pressed inside a 200 mm tall wooden frame of dimensions 300 mm x 350 mm. This is shown in Figure 4.10a and 4.10b. The mat was manually pressed by applying body weight to the slurry with a wooden cover for around 10 min, as seen in Figure 4.10c. Figure 4.10d shows the mat after prepressing. This process has been defined in accordance with previous literature (Stark et al., 2010a; Januario and Beraldo, 2010; Moura Dias, 2005).
   a. b.
  c. d.
Figure 4.10 a. Slurry inside wooden frame over aluminium and Teflon sheets; b. Distribution of the slurry to attain an equal surface height; c. Mat after cold prepressing; d. Prepressed mat before hot plates pressing. Author.
4.2.3.3. Pressing
The pressing variables were suggested by the polyurethane adhesive manufacturer, and were fine-tuned using a Differential Scanning Calorimetry (DSC) test; on a Mettler Toledo model 22/200 which measures the optimal curing temperature through the temperature and heat flows associated with transitions of materials as a function of time and temperature (Gill et al., 1993). Figure 4.11 shows the results of the DSC test, in which the minimum value of 48 °C represents the lowest viscosity and the best flow behaviour of the resin to penetrate the fibres. While the temperature rises, curing begins, represented by the slope rising. The slope correlates with the reactivity of the resin. After 12 minutes and at 83 °C, the slope reached the peak of reactivity of the resin and begun to drop, meaning that the resin has cured. Since having the particleboard inside the press for longer time at lower temperatures would not affect the final product, but will assure better setting of the resin inside of the press, it was decided to leave the
panel inside of the press until the resin stopped reacting; this was set at 18 minutes. The optimal curing properties were defined as pressing 18 min at 84 oC.
 Figure
4.11 Differential Scanning Calorimetry results of castor oil-based polyurethane adhesive. Author.
The mixed samples were then pressed in a Dumont hot plate press model 250T with a 40 cm x 40 cm platen capacity, with a pressure of 3.5 MPa. Two stop metallic bars with dimensions of 10 mm x 10 mm x 400 mm were set on both sides of the mat to fix the final height of the panel (Kelly, 1977) (Figures 4.12d, 4.12a, and 4.12b).
a. b.
  Figure 4.12 a. Press panel before removing it from the hot plates press; b. Pressed panel after removing from hot plates press. Author.
The conventional reference wood/formaldehyde particleboard was prepared following the recommendation from the urea formaldehyde resin manufacturer. The resin had 65% solids w/w, and it was prepared by adding 9% water to 91% resin w/w. The processes of mixing the resin with the ground fibres and prepressing the slurry was the same as the process previously described for the castor oil-based resin samples. The target density was 0.64 g/cm3. The slurry was then cured for 2 min at 193o C in the top plate and 183o C in the lower plate in a Dumont hot platen press, with a pressure of 3.5 MPa, to produce panels 300 mm x 350 mm x 10 mm (Dunky, 1998; Conner, 1996).
After hot pressing, all panels were removed from the hot plates press, cooled at room temperature laying on a flat surface. Once the panels had reached room temperature, 5 cm were cut from each side and then cut to the dimensions of the test samples using a circular saw with carbide tipped blade. This method was defined from literature (Januario, 2006; Moura Dias, 2005). The final size of the particleboards was 200 mm x 250 mm x 1 mm.
4.2.3.4. Formulations of castor oil-based polyurethane samples
Three different particleboard combinations were considered in this study: macadamia shells with castor oil-based polyurethane adhesive (MS-CO), pine cones with castor oil- based adhesive (PC-CO), and Eucalyptus capsules with castor based adhesive (EC-CO). The three previous combinations were manufactured with 20% resin w/w. Three reference samples were manufactured to compare the results, radiata pine wood with castor adhesive (RPW-CO) with 20% resin w/w, radiata pine wood with 10% castor oil- based polyurethane w/w (RPW-CO10), and radiata pine wood with urea formaldehyde (RPW-UF) adhesive. RPW-CO was included as a reference while RPW-UF was a panel used as comparison with a commercially available resin. The compositions of the six material combinations are detailed in Table 4.3. Figure 4.13 shows the finished cut panels before bending strength testing.

4.3. TECHNIQUES USED IN ANALYSIS
The main physical (density, moisture content, water absorption and thickness swelling) and mechanical (MOR, MOE, tensile strength and impact for plastics) properties were studied for the fibre plastic composites and for the castor oil-based polyurethane composites; in addition to the formaldehyde emissions for the adhesive based samples and a microscopy analysis. The results of the physical and mechanical properties were analysed statistically. The methods used in these testing are introduced in this section. After a first visual inspection of the resulting panels, it was decided not to continue with the analysis of the composites based in Guayule rubber, therefore no experimental is presented in the following section for those samples.
4.3.1. Physical properties
4.3.1.1. Density
The density in wood based composite materials is highly relevant. It is known that if the density of a composite is higher the composite should have a better physical and mechanical performance. Factors such as the density of the fibres, and the pressing strength applied to the mat have high influence over the final density and performance of the composites. Kelly (1977) states that the final density of the panels should always be higher than the original densities of the fibres to ensure sufficient inter-particle contact and appropriate mechanical properties. In this research, density was evaluated in order to study the density-performance relationship, and fibres-final density relationship. Density was measured in the samples with recycled PP and castor oil- based polyurethane matrix. The equipment used was a densitometer equipped with a with a Ludlum 44-17 low energy gamma scintillator (Ludlum, 2011), shown in Figure 4.14, using just a single replicate of each sample. This equipment measures the density using the principle of the attenuation of gamma radiation penetrating the test parts. The density of materials affects the capability of compact elements to absorb gamma radiation, therefore the attenuation of gamma radiation functions as a measurement of the density (Gammetec, 2012). This densitometer measures the vertical density gradient of the sample scanning every 0.1 mm on its thickness, and is mainly used to confirm the
different densities in the different layers of particleboards; particularly in three layer particleboards in which the middle layer should have a lower density than the external layers for improved mechanical properties (Kelly, 1977). In this research, as it was an exploratory research, the composite panel materials were manufactured with only one layer, therefore, not much density variation was expected between the core and the faces.
Figure 4.14 Density profile of samples in densitometer equipped with a with a Ludlum 44-17 low energy gamma scintillator. Author.
Klysov (2007) stated in his research that wood-plastic composites should have the highest density as possible, in order to avoid porosity between plastic matrix and hydrophilic fibres. This porosity allows the penetration of moisture inside of the fibres producing water absorption, thickness swelling and decrease in the mechanical properties. Therefore it is highly relevant to define the porosity of the samples. The porosity of the samples was calculated from the difference between the absolute densities and the densities of the composites obtained from the densitometer, and are given as percentages. The absolute density of the samples was obtained by dividing the total mass of the composite by the addition of the absolute volume of each component. And the absolute volume of each component was obtained by dividing the mass (g) per batch by their absolute density (Klysov, 2007).
4.3.1.2. Moisture content
ASTM standard D1037-99 (1999) states that the physical and mechanical properties of wood based composite materials depend on the moisture content at the time of the test. Therefore it is important that all samples are in the same condition before commencing the evaluation. In this research all testing were done at room temperature (20 ± 3 °C) under the same condition for all the samples, and moisture content (MC) was only measured at the beginning of all the testing. MC was determined according to ASTM D1037-99-Standard test method for evaluating properties of wood-base fibre and particle panel materials (1999; Stark et al., 2010a; Ballerini et al., 2008) using 50 mm x 50 mm test samples. The moisture content was determined from the measured weight loss after drying the samples for 24 h at 100°C according to equation (1):
Wd is the weight of the specimen dried for 24 h at 100 °C.
Four replicates were used for the samples containing recycled polypropylene; and three
replicates were used for the samples manufactured with castor oil-based polyurethane. These are shown in Figure 4.15a and 4.15b.

4.3.1.3. Water absorption and thickness swelling
Particleboard, similarly to wood is hygroscopic and dimensionally unstable when exposed to vapour or water, increasing in volume, affecting it irreversibly. Particleboard swells similarly to wood when exposed to high moisture conditions, but the changes are much more evident in the thickness than in the length of the material (Kelly, 1977) .
Water absorption (WA) and moisture induced thickness swelling (TS) were determined according to ASTM 1037-99-Standard test method for evaluating properties of wood- base fibre and particle panel materials (Stark et al., 2010a; 1999; Ballerini et al., 2008) using 50 mm x 50 mm test samples.
One replicate test was carried out for WA and TS, in which four measurements were made, one on each corner of the sample.
WA was determined from the measured weight gain of the panels during the 24 hour immersion period (ASTM, 1999), using equation (2):
WA(%)Wn Wd 100
Wd (2)
where WA is the water absorption (% w/w); Wn is the weight of the wet specimen after soaking and Wd is the initial weight of the specimen prior to soaking.
TS was determined from the average of the measured change in thickness at four locations on each sample after immersion in water for 24 h. TS was calculated according to equation (3):

4.3.2. Mechanical properties
4.3.2.1. Recycled polypropylene panels
Flexural strength test
Flexural strength test was conducted using the three point bending test according to ASTM D790-03-Standard test for flexural properties of unreinforced and reinforced plastics (2003b), to determine the MOR and MOE of the samples. The MOR is the ultimate strength of a board when the material is under bending stress; and the MOE is the measure of the stiffness, or the resistance to bending, when a material is stressed, and it is calculated by dividing the board stiffness by the moment of inertia (Kelly, 1977). The flexural strength test was conducted using an Instron 4468 universal testing machine, which has the software Instron VIII series. According to the standards, the size of the samples was 127 mm x 12.7 mm x 3.2 mm, the span was 16 times the thickness of the sample, and the speed used was 1.35 mm/min, using a 5 kN load cell. Five specimens of each formulation were tested. The test procedures appear in Figures 4.16a; 4.16b; and 4.16c.
After testing, the MOR and MOE of the samples was obtained with equations (4) and (5) respectively (2003b; 1999):
(4) (5)
where R is the MOR, P is the maximum load, L is the length of span (mm), B is the width of specimen (mm), D is the thickness (depth) of specimen (mm), E is the MOE, P1 is the load at proportional limit (N), and y1 is the centre deflection at proportional limit load (mm)
This standard was selected because it is specific for reinforced plastics, and it has been
used by other researchers in previous studies related with composite materials 113
 
reinforced with non-traditional natural fibres (Klysov, 2007; Dong and Davies, 2012; Soatthiyanon, 2010; Stark et al., 2010b; Sui et al., 2009; Migneault et al., 2009; Yemele et al., 2010; Adhikary et al., 2008; Najafi et al., 2006).
Figure 4.16 a. Samples before flexural strength test; b. Recycled polypropylene sample during flexural test; c. Samples after flexural test. Author.
Tensile strength test
Tensile test was carried out according to ASTM D638-03 (2003a) using a dog-bone shaped sample. The size of the samples was modified in their total length, since the smaller size of the samples obtained after pressing. The samples had 150 mm length; with gage dimensions of 14 mm thick by 50 mm length, and 3 mm thicknesses. The samples were manually cut in a small carbide tip band saw, as shown in Figure 4.17a and 4.17b. The testing was conducted using an Instron 4468 Universal Testing Machine, which had the software Instron VIII series, with a 5 kN load cell. The speed used for this test was 5 mm/min and five repetitions of each formulation were tested. The tensile strength of the samples was calculated using the method given in ASTM D635-03 (2003a), dividing the maximum load in newton by the average original cross sectional-area in the gage length segment of the specimen in square meters. The results are expressed in Pascals. This standard test was selected after previous research using polypropylene and other thermoplastics filled with natural fibres. These studies confirmed ASTM D638-03 (2003a) as the appropriate standard for tensile strength test (Klysov, 2007; Najafi et al., 2006; Adhikary et al., 2008). Figure 4.17c shows one sample after testing.
      Figure 4.17a. Cutting samples in a small manual carbide tip band saw; b. Cut samples with dog-bone shape; c. Sample after testing. Author.
Impact strength test
Impact test was conducted according to the standard ASTM 256-05-Standard test methods for determining the Izod pendulum impact resistance of plastics (2005). The equipment used was a Qualitest advanced digital pendulum impact, shown in Figure 4.18a. The dimensions of the samples were 63.5mm (±2 mm) x 12.7 (±0.2 mm) x 3 mm with a centred notch of 2.45 mm (±0.25 mm) depth and a radius of 0.25 (±0.05 mm). The impact of the hammer was 2.75 J. Five replicate samples of each composite were used; these are presented in Figure 4.18b. The impact strength was determined using the method given in ASTM D256-05 (2005) by dividing the impact strength by the area of the notch. This method allowed obtaining the impact strength by area of impact (J/m2). This standard test was selected after reviewing previous research using polypropylene and other thermoplastic plastics filled with wood fibres with good results (Klysov, 2007; Najafi et al., 2006).
Figure 4.18a. Izod impact testing machine; b. Cut and notched samples before testing. Author.
4.3.2.2. Castor oil-based polyurethane panels
Flexural testing and tensile strength perpendicular to surface were carried out using an Instron 4468 universal testing machine with the software Instron VIII series fitted with a 50 kN load cell.
Flexural strength test
As previously mentioned in the research methods of the plastic based composites, the MOR is the ultimate strength of a board when the material is under bending stress; and the MOE is the measure of the resistance to bending when a material is stressed. Generally, boards tend to be stiffer when the value of MOE is high and tend to be more ductile or flexible when the value is low (Yang et al., 2003; Kelly, 1977). Flexural strength test was conducted using the three point bending test according to ASTM D1037-99 (1999). Since the size of the particleboards obtained after pressing in laboratory scale was limited, the standard had to be slightly modified. Therefore, the dimensions of the test samples were 240 mm x 50 mm x 10 mm instead of the 290 mm x 76 mm in the case of samples of 10 mm thickness required by the standard. The tests were conducted with a span length of 20 cm and a cross head speed of 3.3 mm/min. The test procedure is shown in Figure 4.19a. Five replicate samples of each composite were used for each test; these are shown in Figures 4.13 and 4.19b.
Figure 4.19 a. EC-CO panel during three point bending test; b. RPW-UF, RPW- CO, MS-CO, PC-CO, RPW-CO10 samples after three point bending test. Author.
MOR and MOE of the samples were then obtained using the method given in ASTM D1037-99 (1999) with equations (4) and (5) respectively.
This standard has been widely used by other researchers and wood composites manufacturers to test their wood-base fibre and particle materials (ASTM, 1999; Stark et al., 2010a).
Internal bond strength
The tensile strength perpendicular to surface or internal bond strength (IB) provides a quantification of the bonding amongst the particles in the direction perpendicular to the plane of the board. It is expected that a particleboard will normally fail when stressed in tension perpendicular to the board surface in the middle of the thickness, and that higher density particleboards will have higher IBs (Kelly, 1977). In this research the IB was measured according to ASTM D1037 (1999) standard, using samples of 50 mm x 50 mm x 10 mm glued to steel loading blocks with hot melt adhesive. The temperature in which the hot melt adhesive was applied to the loading blocks and samples was around 170 °C (±10 °C). The crosshead speed of the test was 0.8 mm/min. Five replicate of each composite were used (ASTM, 1999; Stark et al., 2010a). The IB strength was obtained in MPa, by dividing the maximum load (Newtons), by the surface (mm2) of the samples. Images of the preparation of the samples and the IB test are presented in Figures 4.20a, and 4.20b.
Figure 4.20a. Cooling down of samples after bonding to the loading blocks; b. RPW-UF sample after testing. Author.
Formaldehyde emissions
The formaldehyde emissions were only evaluated in the samples containing adhesive to confirm that the new proposed castor oil-based polyurethane adhesive had lower or no content of formaldehyde when compared with the traditional UF adhesive based particleboards. The standard used for formaldehyde emissions was the European standard EN-120: Determination of formaldehyde content, extraction method called the perforator method. This method measures the amount of formaldehyde in the panels, shown in milligrams of formaldehyde per 100 grams of dry panel (mg/100g) (Risholm- Sundman et al., 2007; CEN, 1994; Stark et al., 2010a). The formaldehyde is extracted from the samples using boiling toluene, transferring it afterwards to distilled or mineralized water. The retained formaldehyde in the water solution is then determined photometrically. The samples of each different composite were obtained from a single panel, each of them with dimensions of 25 mm x 25 mm x 10 mm (CEN, 1994). Figure 4.21 shows the samples when tested.
Figure 4.21 Formaldehyde emissions test. Author.
4.3.3. Microscopy analysis
The samples manufactured with recycled PP as a matrix and with castor oil-based polyurethane adhesive, including the reference panels, were examined under optical microscopy and scanning electron microscope (SEM) to study the adhesive fibre interaction and fibre alignment (Kelly, 1977).
4.3.3.1. Optical microscopy
One sample measuring 10 mm x 10 mm by the thickness of each of the composites based in recycled PP and adhesive were cut with carbide tipped band saw. The samples were then mounted in epoxy resin with the face perpendicular to surface facing down, and then were metallographically ground and polished to a 6 μm diamond finish (Soatthiyanon, 2010). The mounting process in epoxy resin and the samples before polishing are displayed in Figure 4.22a and 4.22b. The model of the microscope used was a Nikon Epiphot 200 optical microscope. Digital images were recorded using Nikon ACT-1 software.
Figure 4.22a MS-PP samples mounted in epoxy resin; 4.22b Mounted samples in epoxy resin before polishing. Author.
4.3.3.2. Scanning electron microscopy
SEM was used to examine the adhesive fibre interaction of the composites. The fibres of macadamia shells, pine cones, and Eucalyptus capsules were also observed on the SEM.SEM examination was performed using a Hitachi S3400I scanning electron microscope operated in high vacuum mode at an operating voltage of 15 kV, shown in Figure 4.140b. The samples were sputtered coated with a thin layer of gold/palladium prior to examination to avoid charging during examination, this is exhibited in Figure 4.23a.
Figure 4.23 a. PC-CO sample sputtered coated with gold/palladium layer; b. Hitachi S3400I scanning electron microscope with sample. Author.
4.3.4. Statistical analysis
The MC, TS, MOR, MOE, tensile strength, impact strength and IB of the samples were statistically evaluated following the one way fixed effects analysis of variance (ANOVA), using the software GraphPad Prism 5.
4.4 CONCLUSIONS OF THE CHAPTER
This chapter has presented the research methods used to evaluate the fibres, and manufacture the composites and particleboards with the proposed alternative constituents. In addition, the evaluation method for the physical and mechanical properties, the emissions of formaldehyde and the microstructural analysis of the new proposed composites was given, in order to compare their performance with the current practice.
The results of the methods presented in this chapter are presented next.
CHAPTER 5: RESULTS
Chapter five gives the results of the preparation and analysis of the composites with the new proposed fibres (macadamia shells, pine cones, and Eucalyptus capsules) with the three different selected resins (castor oil-based polyurethane, guayule rubber and recycled polypropylene). The aims of this chapter are to manufacture the composites and particleboards with the new proposed alternative constituents, and to evaluate if the physical and mechanical performance of the new proposed composites and particleboards is comparable with the current practice.
This chapter is divided in four sections. The first section reports the chemical properties, morphology evaluation and particle size distribution of the fibres. The second section reflects the manufacturing process of the composites and the results of a first visual evaluation of the composites and particleboards. As a result of this first visual evaluation it was decided to drop further analysis on composites based on Guayule rubber. This decision was taken based on their elastomeric behaviour instead of the desired stiffness for panels. The third section reports the results of the physical and mechanical properties of the composites based in a recycled polypropylene matrix, in addition to an optical and SEM analysis. And the fourth section gives the results of the physical and mechanical properties, the optical analysis and the formaldehyde emissions of the particleboards based in castor oil-based polyurethane.
5.1 CHEMICAL ANALYSIS, DENSITY, SCANNING ELECTRON MICROSCOPY AND PARTICLE SIZE DISTRIBUTION OF THE FIBRES
5.1.1 Chemical analysis and density
The results of the chemical analysis and density of the fibres are displayed in Table 5.1 and compared with data obtained from literature corresponding to radiata pine wood.

The density of the fibres has a high incidence in the performance of particleboards. Softwoods, such as radiata pine wood is known for having low bulk densities, between 0.4-0.75 g/cm3, and an absolute powder density of 1.3 to 1.4 g/cm3 (Klysov, 2007). This density excludes the voids and spaces between the particles. The density evaluation of the new alternative fibre fillers was in average with the absolute powder density of softwood. Macadamia shells and pine cones had an absolute powder density of 1.44 g/cm3, and Eucalyptus had an absolute powder density of 1.43 g/cm3. The density obtained by the macadamia shells, is still higher than what has been reported by Jennings and Macmillan (1986) in their study, in which the total density of macadamia shells is 1.27 g/m3.No reports of the total density of ground pine cones or Eucalyptus capsules have been found in literature.
5.1.1.2 Chemical analysis of the fibres
The chemical analysis begun with the moisture content of the fibres since it is relevant that all samples have similar moisture content at the beginning of the study. The fibres that had highest moisture content were Eucalyptus capsules with 12% per weight; followed by pine cones, with 10% per weight; and macadamia shells had the lowest, with 8% per weight.

                  5.1.1.1 Density of the fibres
Wood is mainly composed of holocellulose, lignin and extractives (Fengel and Wegener, 1983). The holocellulose is the part of the plant that contains the hemicellulose and the cellulose, and gives the structure to the plant and trees. Since holocellulose is hydrophilic, it swells and changes dimensionally with water (Horvath, 2006). The expected holocellulose content in radiata pine wood is 68% (Pettersen, 1984; Fengel and Wegener, 1983; Diaz-Vaz et al., 2009), as seen in Table 5.1. The three new proposed fibres had lower holocellulose content (TAPPI, 1975) than radiata pine wood, with around 60% in all of them.
The cellulose is composed of molecules organized in bundles and held together by hydrogen bonds (Horvath, 2006). The cellulose content for radiata pine wood is 45% (Pettersen, 1984; Fengel and Wegener, 1983; Diaz-Vaz et al., 2009). The results from the chemical analysis for the cellulose content (TAPPI, 1974) in the new proposed fibres were all lower than what is expected for radiata pine wood, with the highest for pine cones and Eucalyptus capsules (35%), and the lowest for the macadamia shells (30%). Toles et al., reported in their study the same amount of cellulose in macadamia shells (Toles et al., 1998).
Lignin fills the cell walls between the cellulose and hemicellulose, and is bonded to the hemicellulose by hydroxyl links, providing mechanical strength to the cell walls. Lignin is hydrophobic; therefore if the amount of lignin is higher, the resistance to moisture will be improved (Horvath, 2006). Lignin covers the cells walls and allows the water to circulate through them. Lignin also decomposes slowly, so it is expected that plants with high lignin content might decompose slower (Horvath, 2006). Lignin content in radiata pine wood is 26.8% (Horvath, 2006; Pettersen, 1984; Fengel and Wegener, 1983; Diaz-Vaz et al., 2009). In the chemical analysis of the new proposed fibres (TAPPI, 2002c) the highest content of lignin was macadamia shells (40.1%). This result is 50% higher than lignin in radiata pine wood, and higher than what has been reported in previous studies by Toles et al (55.8% lignin in macadamia shells) (Toles et al., 1998). Pine cones had 33% content of lignin; and Eucalyptus capsules had the lowest lignin content, with only 22%. Variability in chemical analyses is however common for lignocellulosic materials (Han and Rowell, 1996).
Extractives are the substances existent in wood as non-structural constituents, contributing with colour, odour, taste and smell. Several researches relate extractives with adverse effects on the setting of adhesives, decreasing the particle-particle bond strength; therefore affecting negatively the mechanical properties of the boards (Moslemi, 1974; Nemli and Colakoglu, 2005; Nemli and Aydin, 2007). In this study, extractives were obtained with the test ethanol-toluene soluble (TAPPI, 2002b) and with hot water soluble (TAPPI, 1999; Pettersen, 1984). In radiata pine wood, ethanol-toluene extractives conform around 1.5% (Diaz-Vaz et al., 2009; Horvath, 2006; Fengel and Wegener, 1983). The results from the ethanol-toluene soluble test showed that the Eucalyptus capsules had the highest amount of extractives, with around 8%; followed by pine cones, with 5%; and macadamia shells, with 4%; these two last ones were closer to radiata pine wood. The hot water soluble results expected for radiata pine wood is from 3% - 6%. These results were quite higher in the pine cones (18%), followed by the Eucalyptus capsules (13%); and finally by the macadamia shells (4%), which was much closer to radiata pine wood.
Hot alkali soluble in l% NaOH extracts low molecular weight carbohydrates from wood, consisting mainly of hemicellulose and degraded cellulose in wood. This solubility of wood is an indication of the degree of fungal decay, or degradation due to external factors. The more decay or degradation, the percent of alkali-soluble material is higher (TAPPI, 2002b; Pettersen, 1984). Alkali soluble has been reported to be 16% in radiata pine wood (Fengel and Wegener, 1983). In the new proposed fibres, the highest level was obtained by Eucalyptus capsules with 33%, followed by macadamia shells, with 15%, and the lowest value was obtained by pine cones with 13%; these two last ones closer to the values expected from radiata pine wood.
Hemicellulose in soft woods is composed mainly of two elements, pentosans and hexosans. The pentosan content in radiata pine wood is about 9% (Diaz-Vaz et al., 2009; Pettersen, 1984; Fengel and Wegener, 1983). Hardwood hemicellulose consists mainly of pentosans, which is about 19 to 25% (TAPPI, 2001). In this research, the highest amount of pentosan was obtained by the macadamia shells, with 21.2%, which is in the same ratio of hardwoods; followed by Eucalyptus capsules, with 17%, and pine cones, with 11%. All of them are higher than radiata pine wood.
The ash content is the amount of minerals or inorganic matter in a material (TAPPI, 2002a). In radiata pine wood, the ash content is 0.3% (Diaz-Vaz et al., 2009; Pettersen, 1984; Fengel and Wegener, 1983). In this research, macadamia shells and pine cones were relatively close, with 0.31% and 0.36% respectively. Eucalyptus capsules had quite larger amount of ash content with 2.08% when compared with the other fibres. The macadamia shells consisted of 0.3% ash, which agrees with what had been reported by Toles et al. (1998) in their study of 0.2% ash in macadamia shells.
5.1.2 Scanning electron microscopy of the fibres
SEM micrographs of ground macadamia shells, pine cones and Eucalyptus capsules are shown in Figure 5.1a, 5.1b and 5.1c. The three different fibres, when observed under the microscope seemed quite porous and organized in bundles. The damaging effects of the crashing in the hammer mill are evident in the extremes of the fibres.
Figure 5.1 a. SEM micrograph of Macadamia integrifolia shells; b. SEM micrograph of Pinus radiata D. don cones; c. SEM micrograph of Eucalyptus Globulus capsules. Author.
   a. b.
 c.
As previously reported (Kaupp and Kaupp, 2007), the macadamia shells are characterized by a fullerene-like structure composed of polygons with additional small columnar vertical stiffeners. The polygons exhibit edge walls and consist of flat lying fibres which make up the hard shell (Kaupp and Kaupp, 2007). The cells are grouped into bundles, which are randomly distributed. This is the reason for the high toughness and good mechanical properties of the macadamia shells (Wang and Mai, 1995).
The microfibers composing the ground pine cones are arranged in a tubular shape, which is composed of a rough fibre-composed flat multilayer surface. This surface has pores indicating that this material exhibits good characteristics to fibre adhesive interaction (Arrakhiz et al., 2012). Eucalyptus capsules seem to have a rougher and more porous surface than macadamia shells and pine cones. The SEM shows the fibre from a transversal view.

The distribution of the particle sizes after ground in a Ming Lee Strong Crusher, model ML-SC with a sieve opening of 3 mm, shows that 65% of the fibres are in the size ratio of 10 - 40 mesh (2 mm to 0.42 mm). 11% of the particles were in the range size of less
than 100 mesh (0.149 mm). And 10% of the particles were in the range of 40 - 60 mesh (0.42 mm to 0.25 mm). The lower ratio was obtained by the particles with sizes larger than 10 mesh (2 mm), and from 60 - 100 mesh (0.25 mm to 0.149 mm) with an average of 7% and 6% respectively. No more than 2% of the particles were lost in the process.
5.2 MIXES OF COMPOSITES
After the preparation of the composites and particleboards with the new proposed fillers and with recycled PP, guayule rubber, and castor oil-based polyurethane; they were visually examined and assessed if they were suitable for further analysis. Samples with guayule rubber as a matrix were excluded from this research after this stage.
5.2.1 Fibre plastic composites
The mixes with the recycled PP matrix did not report any problems during the manufacturing or cutting process. The composites presented smooth surfaces and expected sizes. The samples containing pine cones and Eucalyptus capsules seemed to turn a bit darker than expected, which may be attributed to an excess of pressing time. Further studies on the thermal properties of pine cones and Eucalyptus capsules might help defining the right variables for these two fibres. Figure 5.2a and 5.2b exhibits composites manufactured with recycled PP.
Figure 5.2a Composite panel manufactured with macadamia shells and recycled PP after pressing; b. Cut composite panels: MS-PP, PC-PP, EC-PP, RPW-PP, and recycled PP. Author.
  a.
b.
5.2.2 Castor oil-based polyurethane composites
The panels containing castor oil-based adhesives and the three new proposed fibres were manufactured as explained in the research methods. Besides increasing the adhesive–fibre ratio from 10% adhesive to 20%, no further changes or problems occurred during mixing, prepressing, pressing, cooling or cutting. The samples had a rougher surface than the panels manufactured with PP, and with a commercial particleboard. The new proposed particleboards were expected to have a rougher surface than plastic composites, since the lower amount of resin (20%), compared with the 40% plastic. These new proposed particleboards were also expected to be rougher than commercial particleboards since the particle size of the surface were larger, and these were not sanded. Figure 5.3a shows the particleboards cooled down before cutting 5 cm per side, and Figure 5.3b shows the particleboards cut in samples of 5 cm x 5 cm. The particleboards containing macadamia shells had a rougher grainy texture, while the panels containing pine cones and Eucalyptus capsules appeared to have a smoother texture, due to the higher flexibility of the fibres and bending with the pressure of the press. It is remarkable the fact that all samples kept the original aroma of the fibres; as macadamia shells have little smell, it was hardly noticeable, but in the case of the pine cones, the odour of the cones was noticeable, and it was quite strong in the case of the panels manufactured with Eucalyptus globulus capsules, these particleboards kept the aroma without fading, freshening the air of the rooms where they were stored or used.
Figure 5.3a Composite panels manufactured with castor oil-based polyurethane after pressing, before cutting 5 cm per side; b. 5 cm by 5 cm cut composite panel samples. Author.
   a. b.
5.2.3 Guayule rubber composites
The samples obtained in this exploratory research kept their rubber appearance and smell, the matrix covered and blended with the fibres with no problems when mixing or pressing. Macadamia shells showed some tendency to disaggregate from the latex; probably caused by the short fibre-ratio, the texture, and less porosity of the macadamia shells when compared with the pine cones and the Eucalyptus capsules. Pine cones and pinewood particles did not raise any problems of fibre resin adhesion.
Processing the samples was somehow different as expected, because they were hot pressed instead of calendar, and after pressing, even if they were bonded and moulded, they still had high contents of moisture, and they had to be left inside an oven at 60 °C overnight to let them dry under some pressure. If not dried properly, this issue could lead into fungus formation of the fibres.
Figure 5.4a, shows the reference sample containing 100% guayule rubber; 5.4b, the sample containing radiata pine wood fibre and guayule; 5.4c the sample with macadamia shells and guayule, and 5.4d the sample containing radiata pine cones with guayule rubber.
The samples bonded with guayule latex were manufactured in an exploratory stage of the research. These were visually compared with the particleboards manufactured with castor oil adhesive and composites based in recycled polypropylene, filled with the same fibres. After this first visual inspection it was decided not to continue with the study of the composites based in Guayule rubber. The final applications proposed for this research are panels for interior applications, such as furniture or non-structural wall dividers. However, after preparing the composites with guayule rubber, it was decided to down select the resins, and only continue with castor oil-based polyurethane adhesive and recycled polypropylene matrixes. The boards obtained with the plastic and the polyurethane resins were significantly stiffer than the samples based in Guayule rubber; therefore, more suitable for the final applications. The guayule latex samples opened a new research opportunity for further developments in external layers of panels or
furniture, flooring, or other applications. These developments must consider defining particle size, fibre-ratio, particle-rubber ratio, and process of manufacture.

5.3 PHYSICAL AND MECHANICAL PROPERTIES OF COMPOSITES WITH RECYCLED POLYPROPYLENE MATRIX
5.3.1 Physical Properties
The results of the vertical density profile, the moisture content, the water absorption and thickness swelling of the panels manufactured with recycled PP are reported in this section, and briefly compared with existing literature.
5.3.1.1 Density and porosity
The results for the vertical density profile are shown in Table 5.3 and Figure 5.5. The average density corresponds to the column Ex-sander. The results for the porosity calculation of each recycled PP composite are presented in Table 5.4. The densities of the composites with the four different fillers are 10-20% higher than the density of the polymer matrix and are typical of commercial wood-plastic composites (Clemons and Caufield, 2005; Klysov, 2007). Only minimal differences are evident amongst the four composites and the density of the recycled PP fits with the density of the original virgin PP (Sanchez, 2005).

                                                    The porosity of each composite, shown in Table 5.4 clearly presents the high difference amongst all samples, meaning that 14% of PC-PP, 12% of MS-PP, 9% of EC-PP and 4% of RPW-PP composites are composed of pores. These porosities can affect negatively the physical and mechanical performance of the composites due to the
possibility of moisture filtration to the fibres. The porosity is mostly caused by moisture
in the fibres, and VOCs and CO2 released by the decomposition of lignin and extractives in the fibres while hot pressing. Since the hot plates press do not have venting systems, steam and gases stay inside of the samples forming voids and porosity. This can be avoided by switching the manufacturing process to a vented extruder (Klysov, 2007). These results could be explained after the chemical analysis of the fibres. Macadamia shells had the highest amount of lignin when compared with the rest of the fibres, and Eucalyptus capsules and pine cones had the highest amount of extractives when compared with macadamia shells and radiata pine wood. The amount of moisture was not an issue since the fibre were dried down to 1% moisture before manufacturing the composite.

12 14 9 4
                    Source: own results.
     The results from the vertical density profile (Figure 5.5) showed that the fibre based composites had a uniform density distribution through all their thickness, with the exception of PC-PP, which displayed a couple of drops. These drops show the voids in the transversal section of the sample. Wood-plastic composites are usually homogenous in their density profile after extrusion, and these results do not differ from what has been cited in literature (Jeong, 2005; Chen et al., 2012). Wood-plastic composites, are expected to have a higher density at the core of the panel than at the surfaces, particularly in extruded and pressed composites, due to a possible formation of a transcrystalline layer formed around the fibres and to a migration of the PP to the faces (Wolcott and Adcock, 2000). The results from the density profile shows that the densities of the core of the fibre filled composites are up to 3% higher than the densities of the surfaces. When reviewing the core to ex-sander ratio (average of the densities), they are from 1.1 to 1; meaning that all the densities are equal or slightly higher in the core than the average of the sample. Therefore, all studied recycled polypropylene based composites meet the density requirements for wood plastic composites as mentioned above.
 Figure 5.5 Vertical density profile recycled PP based composites. Author.
5.3.1.2 Moisture content
Previous to prepare the composites for this research, the fibres were dried down to 1% moisture before mixing with the plastic. After manufacturing the composites, these were stored at room temperature for one day before testing the moisture content. This test showed the original moisture content of the samples before analysing the water absorption, thickness swelling, and mechanical properties. The results of the moisture content are given in Table 5.5 and Figure 5.6.
The moisture content of the composites ranged from 2.9% to 3.6% w/w compared with 0.2% for the unfilled PP. The ANOVA statistical analysis showed no significant statistically difference amongst the means of none of the fibre filled composites, but it did present a significantly statistical difference between the fibres filled composites and the recycled polypropylene sample. These results were expected due to the hydrophobic nature of the plastic.

5.3.1.3 Water absorption and thickness swelling
Water absorption and thickness swelling are two highly important properties of natural fibres based composites, because they can affect the dimensional stability and the mechanical properties of the composites. Since cellulosic fibres have a hydrophilic nature and plastic is hydrophobic, the absorption of water occurs in the fibres, resulting in an increase of the volume of the fillers. Volume changes in the fibres can lead to interfacial damage, cracking of the plastic matrix and voids between matrix and fillers after the fibres have dried and shrunk inside of the plastic matrix, resulting in irreversible mechanical property reductions (Klysov, 2007; Peyer and Wolcott, 2000; Stark, 2001; Balatinecz and Park, 1997; Clemons and Caufield, 2005).
Table 5.6 shows the results of water absorption after 2 and 24 hours and the results of the thickness swelling of four points of the samples after 2 and 24 hours water immersion, with the standard deviation of the 24 hours thickness swelling; Figure 5.8a shows the results of water absorption after 24 hours immersion of the recycled PP based composites, and Table 5.8b gives the results of the thickness swelling of the recycled PP based composites with standard deviation after 24 hours immersion.

As shown in Table 5.6 and Figure 5.7a the water absorption of the composites was 4% - 12% compared with 0.5% for the unfilled matrix material. Amongst the composites, MS-PP had the lowest water absorption of 4.4%, which was approximately only half the value obtained for RPW-PP of 8.4%. The water absorption for EC-PP of 6.6% was also substantially lower than for the reference panel RPW-PP. However, the value for PC-PP of 11.9% was much higher than for the standard radiata pine wood composite. The high water absorption of PC-PP, EC-PP and RPW-PP can be attributed to a low encapsulation of the fibres by the plastic matrix, due to the porosity of the panels. Generally an absorption rate below 9% is considered to be acceptable for wood-plastic composites with 40% wood (Stark, 2001) and only MS-PP, EC-PP and RPW-PP were below this limit.
The results of the thickness swelling test of the composites, as seen in Table 5.6 and Figure 5.7b, are also very different to that for the unfilled recycled PP, with values ranging from 1% for the unfilled matrix material compared with 5 - 8% for the composites. Amongst the composites, MS-PP had the lowest swelling of 5.3%. The remainder gave values of 7% - 8% but PC-PP and EC-PP showed slightly lower swelling than the reference panel RPW-PP. The ANOVA statistical analysis showed that there was a very high significant statistical difference of the means of the thickness swelling amongst all the fibre filled composites when compared with the recycled PP. When the composites are compared amongst each other, MS-PP had a significant statistical mean difference with PC-PP and PW-PP; however the two latter ones did not present statistical difference amongst each other, nor with EC-PP. MS-PP did not raise any statistically difference in the mean with EC-PP either.
Wolcott (2003) reported in his work regarding hot pressed composite materials, that if the density of the panels is higher, the physical properties at the short term should be better. Wolcott (2003) stated that an optimal composite density should be 0.95 g/cm3, and thickness swelling should follow the performance of water absorption. In this research, all samples had a density above 0.95 g/cm3 and presented expected thickness swelling but quite different water absorption. It is important to note that even if PC-PP had an original higher moisture content than the rest of the samples, and had higher
water absorption than all of the other samples; it did not had higher thickness swelling when compared with the rest of the samples, opposite to RPW-PP.

5.3.2.1 Flexural strength test
     The flexural strength test results are given in detail in Table 5.8 and Figures 5.8a and 5.8b. Out the results, the MOR and the MOE were obtained.
The MOR of the composites ranged from 19 MPa to 40 MPa, and was substantially lower than the values obtained for the unfilled polypropylene of 61 MPa. All fibre filled composites, including RPW-PP (40 MPa) had very high significant statistical difference when compared with recycled PP. The values for the composites MS-PP, PC-PP, and EC-PP were also lower than those for the reference panel RPW-PP, with values of 19 MPa, 21 MPa, and 27 MPa respectively. The results of the MOR showed no statistical significant mean difference when comparing MS-PP, PC-PP and EC-PP amongst each other; but when comparing these three with RPW-PP, all of them showed high significant statistical mean difference. Other studies agree with the MOR of the reference panel RPW-PP for wood-plastic filled composites (Stark, 2001; Soatthiyanon, 2010). The results obtained in this research agree with other studies in which the
addition of pine cones to the plastic matrix decreased the flexural properties of the composites (Ayrilmis et al., 2010).
The MOE was generally higher for the composites than for the unfilled polypropylene with a value of 1.6 GPa. The MOE of the PP is in the range of the MOE reported by the manufacturer of the virgin PP of 1.5 GPa (Sanchez, 2005). It is expected that the MOE should increase after adding fibres to the plastic matrix (Najafi et al., 2006; Klysov, 2007; Stark, 2001; Wolcott, 2001; Adhikary et al., 2008). The standard RPW-PP composite had the highest MOE (2.8 GPa). Similar values for MOE have been found in other researches using wood as filler in PP based composites (Wechsler and Hiziroglu, 2007; Soatthiyanon, 2010). The values for EC-PP of 2 GPa and MS-PP of 1.9 GPa were approximately 30% lower than RPW-PP while the modulus for PC-PP composite was the same as that of the unfilled polypropylene of 1.6 GPa.
No significant statistical difference was found when analysing the means of the MOE of the samples with ANOVA. The only exceptions were PC-PP compared with RPW-PP, which presented a high significant statistical difference, while RPW-PP compared with recycled PP showed a significant statistical difference between their means.
The low values obtained for MS-PP, PC-PP and EC-PP could be caused by the high porosity generated in the composites while pressing. Dong and Davies concluded in their research when increasing the amount of macadamia shells filler to the plastic matrix, the MOE of the panel increased, but if the composite exhibited voids, the flexural modulus decreased dramatically (Dong and Davies, 2012).

5.3.2.2 Tensile strength test
The results for the tensile strength test are presented in Table 5.9 and Figure 5.9.
The tensile strength of the composites was from 6 MPa - 9 MPa, and was substantially lower than the values obtained for the unfilled recycled PP of 26 MPa. The values obtained for the recycled PP are 35% lower than the value of 35 MPa given by the supplier of virgin PP (Sanchez, 2005). These results can be due to the reprocessing and use of the plastic could have degraded the plastic, affecting the tensile properties This result agrees with reports from literature reporting that the use of recycled plastic in wood fibre based composites lowered the tensile properties, obtaining similar results (Najafi et al., 2006). RPW-PP had the highest tensile strength of the composites (9.3 MPa), in average of what has been reported in previous researches using recycled PP and sawdust (Najafi et al., 2006). From the forestry and food industry by-products based composites; MS-PP had the highest tensile strength of 7.5 MPa, while EC-PP had the lowest tensile strength of 6.4 MPa.
The ANOVA statistical analysis showed that all the composites had very high significant statistical difference amongst their means when compared with the recycled PP sample. No significant difference was found amongst the studied composites, with the exception of PC-PP and RPW-PP, which had a low statistically difference between their means.

5.3.2.3 Impact strength test
The results for the impact strength test are exhibited in Table 5.10 and Figure 5.10. The test method used was Izod pendulum impact resistance for plastic, with notched samples. The impact strength values for MS-PP (1.5 kJ/m2), PC-PP (1.5 kJ/m2) and EC- PP (1.8 kJ/m2) composites were approximately 40 to 50% lower than the values obtained by the unreinforced recycled PP (2.87 kJ/m2). ANOVA analysis showed no statistically difference in the means of all the samples. The only exceptions were MS-PP and PC-PP when compared with unfilled recycled PP. However, no reduction in impact strength was observed for the standard RPW-PP (2.92 kJ/m2) compared with the recycled PP samples. It has been reported in literature that impact strength should slightly increase when adding fibres to the matrix (Caufield et al., 2010; Stark, 2001). In this study, the impact strength of the composites only increased with the RPW-PP when compared with the PP. All other samples, MS-PP, PC-PP, and EC-PP decreased their impact strength in relation with the unfilled recycled PP. This performance is assigned to the higher porosity of MS-PP, PC-PP and EC-PP when compared with RPW-PP.

5.3.3 Morphological Characteristics
The morphological characteristics of the recycled PP matrix composites were studied through optical microscopy and scanning electron microscopy. The objective of this study was to define and confirm the reasons of the physical and mechanical performance of the new proposed fibre based composites. The matrix fibre interaction was the main focus of this study.
5.3.3.1 Optical microscopy
The images from the optical microscopy are shown from Figure 5.11 to 5.14 for MS-PP, PC-PP, EC-PP, and reference sample RPW-PP at four different magnifications. The transversal view is shown for the five different polished samples. MS-PP panel is shown in images 5.11a, b, c and d.
The particles in MS-PP composite seem to be well dispersed, with no particular
alignment or direction, and well encapsulated by the recycled PP matrix, as seen in 148

Figures 5.11a and 5.11b. MS-PP had the lowest MOR, which can be attributed, furthermore the high porosity of the sample, to the short fibre-ratio of the shell. The lack of alignment of the fibres in the matrix, as seen in Figure 5.11c is opposed to the nature of the fibres of the other fillers. This could affect the interconnections between the fibre and the matrix.
     Recycled polypropylene matrix
Recycled polypropylene matrix
  Macadamia shells particles
Macadamia shells particles
    Macadamia shells particles
Macadamia shells particle
Figure 5.11 Optical microscopy images of polished samples MS-PP transversal to face at different magnifications. Author.
PC-PP panel is displayed in images 5.11a, b, c and d. The transversal view of the polished samples of PC-PP composite shows a good dispersion of the particles, with no particular direction or alignment. Big voids are clearly seen in the composites, particularly in Figures 5.12a and 5.12b. These voids were suggested in the vertical density gradient profile, and in the results of the porosity of the samples. The voids in the matrix affected negatively the moisture content, water absorption, thickness swelling, MOE, tensile strength, and impact strength. Figures 5.12c and 5.12d shows
the composite at higher magnification, in which is possible to observe the fibres bundles, and the transversal structure of the fibres. In addition, the walls of these fibres in Figure 5.12d seem to be rougher than the macadamia shell fibres.
Figure 5.12 Optical microscopy images of polished samples PC-PP transversal to face at different magnifications. Author.

Similarly to MS-PP and PC-PP, the transversal view of the polished EC-PP composites seem to have well encapsulated particles, as shown in Figure 5.13a and 5.13b. In Figure 5.13c is possible to see the bundle of fibres and 5.13d shows a rougher surface of the walls of the fibres when compared with macadamia shells and with pine cones.

5.3.3.2 Scanning electron microscopy
The fibre-plastic interaction was examined on fractured surface of the composites using a Hitachi S3400I scanning electron microscope (SEM). The aim of this study was to analyse if there were any pull outs or matrix-fibre gaps. Representative SEM micrographs are shown in Figures 5.15a for the MS-PP; 5.15b for the PC-PP; 5.15c for the EC-PP; and 5.15d for the reference panel RPW-PP.
The SEM micrograph of MS-PP, opposite from the optical images, clearly presents the two phases of the plastic matrix and the particles. The macadamia particles are agglomerated in bundles and separate from the plastic, while the interphase behaviour seems irregular and heterogeneous. It is possible to identify some cracked bundles of fibres adhered to the matrix, some voids due to the cracking and bare fibre pulled out. It is appreciated that a few good matrix-fibres bonding have occurred in some spots, but 152

also high separation can be seen in others. The voids have affected negatively the mechanical properties MOR, MOE and impact strength, in which MS-PP had one of the poorest performances of all the samples. This separation of the fibre-plastic matrix has not affected the water absorption and thickness swelling of the MS-PP composites, leading to the conclusion that macadamia shells do have a good moisture resistance by itself, without the interaction with the PP.
PC-PP composite, shown in Figure 5.15b, also offers a heterogeneous cracked surface, in which is possible to see some pull out fibres from the matrix when cracked, and some fibres inserted in the matrix. The pulled out fibres shown non-adhering matrix, and have been imprinted in the matrix, demonstrating poor fibre-matrix bonding. Some fibre matrix adhesion can be seen though, and the performance has been mainly damaged by the voids in the mix instead of the interphase interaction.
EC-PP composite is shown in Figure 5.15c. Similar to MS-PP, it presents voids between fibres and matrix, in which fibres seem loose and matrix does not seem ductile. Some imprints of the pulled out fibres are observed. This interaction could be improved by the utilization of coupling agents.
The reference panel RPW-PP, shown in Figure 5.15d, seems more compact and homogeneous than MS-PP, PC-PP, and EC-PP composites, some fibres and some voids can be observed, but in general, the matrix seems to cover the fibres, and no fibre pull- outs occurred. The more homogenous surface can be due to the better encapsulation of the particles. This has been reflected in the better mechanical performance of RPW-PP.

5.4. PHYSICAL AND MECHANICAL PROPERTIES OF CASTOR OIL- BASED POLYURETHANE PARTICLEBOARDS.
5.4.1. Physical properties
The results of the density, the vertical density gradient, the moisture content, the water absorption and thickness swelling of the composites manufactured with castor oil-based polyurethane are reported in this section, and compared with existing literature. Porosity has not been considered for the castor oil-based polyurethane particleboards, since porosity is a property inherent to plastic based composites.
5.4.1.1. Density
Stark and the Engineered Wood Products Association of Australasia (EWPAA) (2010; 2008 ) reported the densities of particleboards from 0.6 - 0.8 g/cm3. The results for the vertical density profile of this study are shown in Table 5.11 and Figure 5.16. The average density of the samples is the column named Ex-sander of Table 5.11.
The lowest density amongst the samples was that of the reference panel RPW-CO10 (0.5 g/cm3), followed by RPW-CO (0.69 g/cm3), and by RPW-UF (0.82 g/cm3) which are slightly above the average. The density of MS-CO, PC-CO and EC-CO were the highest amongst all samples, ranging from 0.9 g/cm3 - 0.99 g/cm3. The differences of densities are directly related with the higher amount of fibres and resin added to the mat. Since this was an exploratory study, the first sample manufactured was RPW-CO10, with a target density of 0.5 g/cm3; this sample included 630 g of radiata pine wood fibres and 10% resin per weight for a volume of 1,400 cm3. Since the fibres of the resultant panel were quite loose, it was decided to increase the amount of material in the mat in order to increase the density and compaction of the composites. The next sample manufactured was RPW-CO with 20% resin and a target density of 0.65 g/cm3. The mat included 800 g of wood, with the same mould and pressing variables than the previous panel. The result was acceptable, and the same amount of fibres was considered for all the following samples. However, when preparing the mix for MS-CO, it was also found that the mat was quite low, probably due to the rounded and more compact shape of the
ground macadamia shells. Therefore, the amount of fibres and adhesive were increased in the same 80% adhesive 20% resin ratio to fill the prepressing mould. The mass of fibres added to the mat was 867 g. When preparing the mixes of RPW-CO and EC-CO, a similar issue occurred; therefore the amount of fibre was increased to 1,000 g to ensure an adequate compaction. In the case of the reference sample RPW-UF, the mass of fibres added to the mat were up to 900 g and UF resin was added in a 10% ratio. All samples were prepressed and pressed in the same mould and press.
It has been stated in literature that most particleboards are composed of three layers, two high density layers with smaller grain size on both surfaces, and a lower density core, composed of thicker particles. In the three layer particleboards it is expected that the vertical density gradient should be higher at the ends and lower in the middle in order to achieve better mechanical properties (Kelly, 1977). In this research, composite materials have been manufactured in only one layer; therefore a flatter vertical density gradient was expected in all particleboard composites, including the reference panels. The results from the density profile showed a homogeneous density gradient in most of the samples with the exception of the reference panel RPW-UF which showed 13% lower density in the core than the peak density of the surfaces, and a core to ex-sander ratio slightly lower than one (0.96). This result means that the density of the core is lower than the average density of the sample. Kelly explained that a vertical density gradient is caused by the speed, pressure, and temperature of closing the hot plates press. If the press is too hot and closes too fast, the particles with adhesive closer to the plates compresses and begins to cure faster, while reaching the desired height due to the metallic stops, before compression failure occurs in the core. The RPW-UF was pressed for 2 minutes at 193 °C, while the castor oil-based panels were pressed for a much longer time of 18 minutes at only 85 °C, allowing the polyurethane to cure at all thickness of the samples (Kelly, 1977)

5.4.1.2. Moisture content
Table 5.12 and Figure 5.17 show the moisture content of the composites. The results of the moisture content shows that this is similar in all the samples, with values form 4.2% to 6.3%. The lowest moisture content was obtained by EC-CO (4.2%), followed by MS- CO (4.9%) and PC-CO (5.7%). The reference panel RPW-CO10 (6.3%) had the highest moisture content of all samples; and RPW-CO and RPW-UF were closest to PC-CO with 5.6% and 5.8% respectively. When comparing these results with commercial particleboards, which are between 10% and 12% (EWPAA, 2008 ), the new proposed 157

fillers all had lower moisture content. This result is not unexpected as all of the fibres were thoroughly dried prior to mixing.
ANOVA showed not significant statistical mean difference between any of the six samples for the moisture content test, with the exception of a slightly statistical difference between the means of EC-CO and RPW-CO10. Each composite system demonstrated a very stable average original physical behaviour.
