5.4.1.3. Water absorption and thickness swelling
The results for the water absorption and thickness swelling of the samples after 2 and 24 hours soaking are shown in Table 5.13. Figure 5.18a shows the water absorption after 24 hours immersion, and Figure 5.18b shows the results of the thickness swelling of the particleboards after 24 hours immersion with their respective standard deviation.
The three reference panels manufactured with radiata pine wood: RPW-CO10 (46%), RPW-CO (43%), and RPW-UF (49%) had quite high water absorption values. When comparing the pine wood filled composites with the new proposed panels, MS-CO (11%), PC-CO (15%) and EC-CO (15%), the latter results are up to 3.5 times lower. The lower water absorption of the new proposed composites reflects the high hygroscopic nature of radiata pine wood. One factor that could have influenced the improved performance of water absorption is that macadamia shells, pine cones and Eucalyptus capsules have lower content of hydrophilic holocellulose than softwood; and macadamia shells have a higher content of hydrophobic lignin. The good moisture resistance of macadamia shells has been previously noted in literature (Kaupp and
Kaupp, 2007). Differing from Ayrilmis et al. (2010) and likewise the results obtained in this PhD research, Buyuksari et al. also reported a substantial decrease in the water absorption and thickness swelling while adding pine cones to the particleboards. Buyuksari attributed the results to the higher amount of extractives than in pine wood (Büyüksarı et al., 2010). Both Pine cones and Eucalyptus capsules have higher amount of extractives than radiata pine wood, as seen in section one of the results chapter. The relationship between improved physical properties and higher amount of extractives has been previously mentioned in other studies (Nemli and Aydin, 2007). The rate of water absorption has also been reported to decrease while increasing particleboard density (Thoemen et al., 2010). Therefore, the higher density of MS-CO, PC-CO and EC-CO particleboards could be a contributing factor in their water absorption when compared with RPW-CO10, RPW-CO and RPW-UF. Amongst the reference panels, this statement applies to PW-CO; but not to PW-UF which had a higher density than PW- CO and still had higher water absorption. Aside from the hygroscopic nature of the wood filler, the castor oil-based polyurethane also enhances the resistance to water absorption. The improved performance of water absorption of castor oil-based polyurethane is manifest in the water absorption of the reference samples manufactured with radiata 90% pine wood. Both samples RPW-UF and RPW-CO10 had 10% resin; however, the water absorption in RPW-CO10 was 3% lower than RPW-UF.
All new proposed fibres have shown to be very dimensionally stable when compared with radiata pine wood. The swelling resulting from water uptake was substantially less for MS-CO, PC-CO and EC-CO than for the radiata pine wood particleboards, obtaining values from 3% to 8% thickness swelling, compared with 12 to 32% for the pinewood particleboards. When analysing the results with ANOVA, all samples showed to have a very high significant statistical difference amongst their means, with the exception of PC-CO and EC-CO, and RPW-CO with RPW-CO10.
Thickness swelling for conventional commercial particleboards is reported to be 15% and for MDF is up to 30% (EWPAA, 2008 ). The swelling observed for the particleboards bonded with castor oil-based polyurethane are well below this range, but not RPW-UF. It has been found in this research that the thickness swelling first depends on the adhesive and second in the type of fibres and not so directly on the density or the
                                                         amount of fibres. RPW-UF had a higher density than RPW-CO10 and RPW-CO, and still had an extremely high swelling of 32% compared with 13% and 12% of the latter two. The higher wood content in RPW-CO10 and RPW-CO might have induced a higher thickness swelling, but results of both composites with 90% of wood clearly confirm the difference performance between both resins.

5.4.2. Mechanical properties
A resume with the MOR, the MOE and the tensile strength perpendicular to surface or IB results for the three particleboards is given in Table 5.14.

5.4.2.1. Flexural strength test
MOE (GPa)
0.38 0.73 0.69 0.36 0.93 1.2
IB (MPa)
1.5 2.3 1.7 0.4 1 0.5
                                The detailed results for MOR and MOE with standard deviation for the three particleboards are given in Table 5.15 and shown in Figure 5.19a, for the MOR, and 5.19b for the MOE.
The MOR and MOE with their respective standard deviations were obtained for all samples. The highest values for the MOR were obtained by the reference panels PW- CO (8.7 MPa); and PW-UF (8.4 MPa). The reference panel with 10% castor oil-based polyurethane, RPW-CO10, had the lowest results, with only 2.8 MPa. Amongst the new proposed fillers, MS-CO, PC-CO, and EC-CO, the higher value was obtained by EC- CO (7.3 MPa), followed by PC-CO (5.7 MPa); and the lowest MOR was for MS-CO (4.3 MPa). The results of the means of the MOR of the samples were statistically compared with ANOVA. None of the three latter samples presented significant statistical mean differences. MS-CO showed a small significant difference when compared with RPW-CO; and EC-CO had a small significant statistical mean difference with the reference panel RPW-CO10. This sample also had a small statistical mean difference with RPW-CO and RPW-UF
Similarly to the MOR, two of the reference composites, RPW-CO (0.93 GPa) and RPW-UF (1.21 GPa) had the highest MOE followed by PC-CO (0.73 GPa) and EC-CO
(0.69 GPa). The reference sample RPW-CO10 had the lowest MOE of all composites with 0.36 GPa, followed closely by MS-CO, with 0.38 GPa. The means of the results were statistically analysed via ANOVA test. The new proposed fillers, MS-CO, PC-CO and EC-CO did not present a significant difference amongst their means, as neither did the reference samples RPW-CO and RPW-UF between their means, but all three new proposed fillers showed a significant statistical mean difference with RPW-UF. RPW- CO10 had a high significant statistical mean difference with the two other pine wood reference panels, RPW-CO and RPW-UF.
The results obtained in this study by RPW-CO agree with the results from Januario and Beraldo (Januario, 2006; 2009; Pereira and Beraldo, 2008), in which they manufactured 1 layer particleboards with bamboo particles and the same castor oil-based polyurethane used in this research. They concluded that the flexural properties were negatively affected with the new proposed adhesive when compared with commercial panels.
Dias (2005) developed in his PhD research particleboards based in Eucalyptus and castor oil-based polyurethane with improved results. The MOR of his samples was 17 MPa, and the MOE was 2.2 GPa. The main difference between Dias study and this study was the larger particle size he used, from 4 to 42 mm, distributed in three layers. This research considered instead of 3 layers, the distribution of the particles in 1 layer with particle sizes from 0.42 mm to 2 mm.
All the particleboards manufactured for this study showed lower MOE than commercial composite materials (2.8 GPa) (EWPAA, 2008 ). The lower flexural modulus of the samples in this study can be caused since the particleboards of this research considered one layer panel distribution instead of three layers. One layer particleboards have a non- existent V shaped vertical density gradient. Another factor is the smaller particle size than what is traditionally used in commercial particleboards. Several researches have stated that smaller and smoother wood fibres result in low fibre aspect ratios, leading to poor physical fibre to fibre contact. Short fibre aspect ratios result in poorer flexural properties when compared with longer fibres (Ayrilmis et al., 2009; Shi and Gardner, 2006; Maloney, 1977; Nemli et al., 2003; Büyüksarı et al., 2010). The type of resin also
plays a critical role; while UF adhesives are more brittle, with a lower creep; polyurethanes are more ductile and tough (Frihart, 2005).
Regarding the new proposed fillers, no studies have been found using macadamia shells or Eucalyptus capsules in adhesive based composites, However, the use of pine cones has been previously studied, reporting that adding pine cones to panels negatively affect the mechanical properties due to the higher amount of extractives of the fibres (Ayrilmis et al., 2009; Büyüksarı et al., 2010; Nemli et al., 2003; Maloney, 1977). In this research extractives were found to be even higher in Eucalyptus capsules than in pine cones. This composition could have affected the flexural performance of EC-CO when compared with RPW-CO and PC-CO. The poorer mechanical performance of MS-CO compared to the rest of the samples, could be caused by the short fibre aspect ratio and by the lower cellulose content than the rest of the filler, since the cellulose is mostly responsible for the strength and stiffness of plant fibres (Wool and Sun, 2005). Another cause for the low performance of MS-CO can be the MOE of the base fibres. The MOE of radiata pine wood alone is from 8 to 9 GPa (Kretschmann, 2010; Kamke, 2006) and the MOE of macadamia shells is only 5.7 GPa (Wang et al., 1995). This result could lead to the observation that the flexural properties of the fibres affect directly the flexural properties of the composites.
The results obtained in this research are not surprising and they agree with previous studies, in which lower flexural properties have been reported for particleboards manufactured with agricultural by-products (Bektas et al., 2005).

5.4.2.2. Internal bond strength
The detailed results of the IB strength of the panels and the standard deviation are displayed in Table 5.16 and Figure 5.20. It is important to note that many composites failed in the hot melt glue line instead of the middle of the thickness of the samples as expected. These are marked with an asterisk, and were not considered in the calculation of the IB strength nor the standard deviation, with the exception of MS-CO, in which all samples failed in the hot melt glue line (Figure 5.21).
The higher results were obtained by the three new proposed fillers, PC-CO leads the IB strength results with 2.3 MPa, followed by EC-CO, with 1.7 MPa, and MS-CO with 1.5 MPa. The reference panels filled with radiata pine wood performed significantly lower results. RPW-CO had IB strength of 1 MPa, and the two reference panels with 10% resin had the poorest results. RPW-UF had IB strength of 0.5 MPa; while RPW-CO10 had the lowest of all with 0.4 MPa. ANOVA analysis showed that all new proposed fillers had no significant statistical mean difference amongst each other, and none of the three reference panels filled with radiata pine wood had any statistical difference amongst their means either. PC-CO had a significant statistical mean difference with RPW-CO and a very high significant statistical mean difference with both reference samples with 10% resin, RPW-CO10 and RPW-UF. While EC-CO also had a significant statistical difference with the latter two reference samples.
The vertical density gradient could be the cause of the lower performance of RPW-UF, since the density in the core is lower, and the failure unavoidably occur in the weakest point of the thickness (Kelly, 1977). The lower density of RPW-CO10 could also have affected its performance. Another factor affecting the IB strength of the composites is the higher tensile properties of the new proposed fibres. This factor could have affected positively in the IB strength of the composites. While macadamia shells have a high tensile strength of 55 MPa (Wang and Mai, 1995), radiata pine wood has a tensile strength parallel to the fibre of 10.6 MPa (Harris et al., 1991), significantly lower than macadamia shells. No data has been found in regards of the tensile strength of pine cones and Eucalyptus capsules.
The results obtained in this study contradict those of other researchers, where the IB strength of panels manufactured with pine cones mixed with pine wood and UF adhesive were lower than those filled only with pine wood (Ayrilmis et al., 2009; Büyüksarı et al., 2010). Moura Dias also reported higher IB results for the samples manufactured with castor oil-based polyurethane resin when compared with the standard and with formaldehyde based reference samples (Moura Dias, 2005).
The IB strength for standard particleboards less than 12 mm thick has been reported to be 0.6 MPa (EWPAA, 2008 ). All samples bonded with 20% castor oil-based polyurethane had higher IB strength values than standard particleboards, while both reference samples bonded with 10% resin, were slightly lower than standard particleboards reported in literature, therefore the castor oil-based polyurethane used has shown to have better results when used at 20% ratios in all fibres.
It is relevant to state that not all samples broke in the centre of the thickness (Figure 5.21). Therefore, some results have not been considered, as in the case of all MS-CO samples, EC-CO3 and EC-CO4, shown in Table 5.16. The fact that the failure of the sample occurred in the hot melt adhesive and not in the interior could be due to a higher IB strength in the sample than the hot melt glue line, failing in the weakest point, therefore the results are not reliable, or might be higher for MS-CO and EC-CO. Dias and Januario (2005; 2006) reported the same situation in their researches.

5.4.3. Formaldehyde emissions
The formaldehyde emissions are given in Table 5.17 for the four particleboards manufactured with 20% castor oil-based polyurethane, compared with the reference panel RPW-UF, pine wood, and commercial standard UF bonded particleboard and MDF. Figure 5.22 shows a comparative graph of the samples manufactured for this research.
The results for the four particleboards manufactured with castor oil resin present minimal levels of formaldehyde, as expected. These are 0.1 - 0.12 mg/100g compared with 8 mg/100g for the urea formaldehyde particleboard. Commercial particleboards are reported to have UF emissions of 2 - 3 mg/100g, MDF to have emissions of 7.5 mg/100g, while formaldehyde emissions from pine wood are 0.23 mg/100g (Risholm- Sundman et al., 2007). The values of the castor oil polyurethane based samples are actually, half of that of pine wood by itself. Also as expected, PW-UF panels have UF emissions in the range of MDF panels.

5.4.4. Morphological characterization
The adhesive fibre interaction of the composites manufactured with 20% castor oil- based polyurethane and the reference sample RPW-UF was investigated in terms of adhesive penetration and surface coating through visual inspection of the micrographs of the composite panels.
5.4.4.1. Optical microscopy
Transversal view images of the optical microscopy analysis are shown in Figures 5.23 for MS-CO, 5.24 for PC-CO, 5.25 for EC-CO, 5.26 RPW-CO and 5.27 RPW-UF at four different magnifications.
The MS-CO images show a composite with well dispersed particles in the resin, with no particular alignment or direction, and no visible voids. It is possible to observe a short fibre aspect ratio (Figure 5.23a), and the different directions of the fibres (Figure 5.23c) MS-CO had the highest physical performance. After hot pressing, the macadamia cells exhibited extensive penetration of the adhesive, as shown in Figure 5.23b. The hollow hexagonal cell structure, clearly seen in Figure 5.23d, allowed an easy penetration of the aromatic adhesive, forming a strong bond resistant to the effect of moisture. MS-CO had the poorest flexural performance of all samples bonded with 20% castor oil-based polyurethane. The rounded shape of the fibres, when compared with Eucalyptus capsules, radiata pine cone and radiata pine wood, could have affected negatively the flexural properties when compared with the other composites. It has been previously reported than shorter fibres aspect ratios affects negatively the bending properties of wood based composites (Ayrilmis et al., 2009).

The transversal views of the polished samples of PC-CO seem to have a heterogeneous distribution of the particles in the matrix, with no particular alignment or direction, as seen in Figures 5.24a and 5.24b. Similar to MS-CO, the fibres seem to have a low fibre aspect ratio, even though they all have similar length. Therefore, the length-diameter ratio is shorter, and might have affected negatively the flexural properties. It is possible to see some voids between the fibres and the resin in Figure 5.24b that could have affected the flexural properties.
Similarly to what was observed in the optical microscopy images of PC-PP, the walls of the pine cones fibres seemed to be rougher and more unidirectional than the macadamia shell fibres (5.24c). PC-CO had a slightly higher water absorption and thickness than MS-CO. This could be attributed to the more porous surface of the pine cones fibres when compared with the macadamia shells.

The transversal views of the polished EC-CO composite (5.25a, 5.25b), seem to have well encapsulated particles with a slightly tendency to horizontal alignment. The shape of the fibres of Eucalyptus capsules have higher fibre aspect ratios than the fibres of macadamia shells and pine cones, considering that fibres have the same particle size distribution in all samples. In Figure 5.25c and 5.25d is possible to see the bundles of fibres, with a clearly more aligned orientation than the previous samples.
EC-CO presented poorer mechanical properties than the reference samples RPW-CO and RPW-UF. However, the MOR was better than MS-CO and PC-CO. This could be attributed to a higher fibre aspect ratio, allowing a better fibre to fibre interaction.

The transversal view of the polished samples of RPW-CO shown in Figures 5.26a, 5.26b, and 5.26c presented well dispersed, smooth fibres with an aligned orientation. All fibres seem completely covered and embedded by the castor oil-based polyurethane.
It is possible to note the higher amount of resin surrounding the fibres when compared with RPW-UF (Figure 5.27), and a better resin-fibres interphase. A better interphase would result in an improved mechanical performance than MS-CO, PC-CO and EC-CO. RPW-CO had a better physical performance than RPW-UF and RPW-CO10, probably caused by the encapsulation of the wood fibres by the water resistant polyurethane resin.

The transversal view of the polished samples of RPW-UF (Figures 5.27a and 5.27b) shows well horizontally aligned, highly compacted fibres. The MOE values confirm Kelly’s observation in which he noted that the effective MOE values are substantially higher for boards of parallel alignment (Kelly, 1977). The composites manufactured with pine wood had better mechanical performance than MS-CO, according with the higher flexural properties of RPW-CO and RPW-UF. The better flexural performance result can be associated with the higher fibre aspect ratio of pine wood compared with macadamia shells, pine cones and Eucalyptus capsules.

5.4.4.2. Scanning electron microscopy
The fibre-adhesive interaction was examined on the fractured surface of the composites using a Hitachi S3400I scanning electron microscope (SEM). Representative SEM micrographs are shown in Figures 5.28a for MS-CO; 5.28.b for PC-CO; 5.28.c for EC- CO; 5.28.d for the reference panel RPW-CO; and 5.28e for the panel RPW-UF.
The SEM of MS-CO (Figure 5.28a), presents a bundle of broken fibres of macadamia shells surrounded by resin, with no particular alignment. It is possible to identify a crack in the resin with very few pull-outs.
Figure 5.28b shows bundles of fibres of pine cones with parallel alignment amongst some of these bundles, and different direction in others. Some resin can be seen attached to the fibres. The occurrence of voids between the fibres and resin confirmed that the 178

fracture of this sample occurred in the matrix instead of the fibres, although some cracks can be observed in the fibres.
Figure 5.28c shows EC-CO with a more aligned direction. It is possible to appreciate a few cracks and voids parallel to the fibres. Similarly to MS-CO and PC-CO, the fracture occurred in the matrix, as no fibres cracks are observed. Some residues of matrix are appreciated around the fibres as well.
Figure 5.28d shows the sample RPW-CO. The fibres are aligned in one direction. Broken fibres, a few cracks and voids between fibres are seen. Fractures on wood fibres are appreciated, which could lead to the conclusion of a good bonding and penetration of the resin into the fibres.
Figure 5.28e presents the samples RPW-UF. Similarly to RPW-CO, the sample shows unidirectional alignment of the fibres, according to the optical microscopy analysis (image 5.26). A few voids are visible, but it is evident that the fracture occurred in the fibres instead of the matrix as they seem broken, and no pull outs are observed.
The difference in the type of fibres was a defining factor while comparing the flexural strength of the composites. As shown by the results of the MOE and MOR, the fibres contained in MS-CO, PC-CO and EC-CO showed rounded shapes and smoother surfaces, while being visibly porous. When comparing the pine wood based samples with the new proposed fibres, the first ones appeared to be rougher in texture, but with less porosity, while the structures of the macadamia shells, pine cone and Eucalyptus capsule fibres seem to be more compact and less fibrous than that of the pine wood.

5.5. CONCLUSIONS OF THE CHAPTER
Chapter five reported the results from the experimental section of this PhD research. The objectives targeted were to manufacture the composites and particleboards with the new proposed alternative constituents, and to evaluate if the physical and mechanical performance of the new proposed composites and particleboards is comparable with the current practice.
Macadamia shells, pine cones and Eucalyptus capsules based composites were manufactured with three different resins: guayule rubber recycled polypropylene, and castor oil-based polyurethane. However, after a first visual inspection, the two latter ones were selected for further analysis.
Amongst the composites manufactured with the plastic matrix, the best physical performance was achieved by the samples with macadamia shells, followed by the samples containing eucalyptus capsules, and finally the samples with pine cones. Nevertheless, all of them had a better performance that the reference sample manufactured with wood filler.
All the new proposed recycled plastic based composites had a lower mechanical performance than the reference panel filled with radiata pine wood. And amongst these, the poorest mechanical performance was obtained by the pine cones filled composites, followed by the macadamia shells composite and the best mechanical performance of the three new fillers was achieved by composites with eucalyptus capsules. The low mechanical properties of the new proposed composites could be caused by the voids in the mixes, producing poor encapsulation of the fibres and poor interphase bonding. This was shown in the morphological evaluation. These results are further discussed in chapter 6.
The densities of MS-CO, PC-CO, and EC-CO were significantly higher than that of the wood fibre particleboards, due to the higher mass of material added to the mixes. The vertical density gradient of the samples showed clearly the effects of the pressing variables over the fibres and the resins. The UF based sample was the only one with lower density in the core caused by the higher temperature and shorter time of pressing.
The particleboards manufactured with castor oil-based polyurethane had improved physical properties when compared with the reference sample based in UF. Therefore, the water absorption and thickness swelling of these particleboards was strongly related to the structure of the new fibres and to the adhesive type. The only test in which the new proposed particleboards performed poorly than the reference sample with pine wood and UF was the flexural strength test. The MOR and MOE were substantially lower for the samples with macadamia shells, pine cones and Eucalyptus capsules than for the wood fibre particleboards. This is considered to be due, in first place, to the lower cellulose content of the macadamia shells, pine cones and Eucalyptus capsules compared with radiata pine wood. In second place, to the non-oriented low fibre aspect ratio of the new fibres when compared with the slimmer and parallel aligned fibres of radiata pine wood. And in third place, to the type of resin, which seems to have affected the flexural strength. All these results are discussed in detail in chapter 6. IB strength was significantly better for the particleboards with the new proposed fillers when compared with radiata pine wood based samples, and samples bonded with 20% castor oil-based polyurethane had improved IB strength when compared with 10% resin. The formaldehyde emissions from the castor oil-based polyurethane particleboards were extremely low compared to those for conventional particleboards manufactured with urea formaldehyde resin.
The microscopy analysis was quite useful to evaluate the shape, size, distribution and interphase bond of the fibres with both resins. The rounded shape, less alignment, and fractures in the resin of the new proposed fibres gave evidence on the lower flexural performance when compared with the reference samples filled with radiata pine wood. The results show that the new fibres and resins considered in this study have acceptable properties for the manufacture of particleboards and composite materials for the built environment, particularly in high moisture environments.
The discussions of these results are presented in chapter 6.
CHAPTER 6: DISCUSSION OF RESULTS
This chapter is the discussion of the results presented in the previous chapter. This chapter aims to determine if the physical and mechanical performance of the new proposed composites and particleboards is comparable with current practice.
The fillers studied as an alternative to pine wood had important differences and some similarities in their chemical composition and in the shape of the fibres that are highly relevant to understanding the physical and mechanical performance of the new composites. The resins used to bind these fillers also had important unique characteristics that were highly incidental in the final properties of the manufactured panels. The effects of these differences, similarities and characteristics over the new proposed particleboards and composite materials are presented in this chapter in three sections: influence of the fibres over the properties of the panels, influence of the resins over the properties of the panels, and effects of the density over the properties of the panels.
6.1 FIBRES: CHEMICAL COMPOSITION, MORPHOLOGY AND DISTRIBUTION.
As presented in the previous chapter, the content of holocellulose was 12% lower in macadamia shells, pine cones and in Eucalyptus capsules than in softwood (Fengel and Wegener, 1983). The cellulose content was similar (30% - 35% by weight) in all fibres, including softwood (Fengel and Wegener, 1983).
Macadamia shells had the highest content of lignin of all samples. The lignin content of radiata pine cones (33%) was higher than pine wood (27%) (Diaz-Vaz et al., 2009; Fengel and Wegener, 1983) and Eucalyptus capsules were the lowest of all (22%).
The highest amount of ethanol and hot water soluble extractives were obtained by the pine cones and Eucalyptus capsules, which were up to 491% and 371% higher than softwood, respectively. The lowest proportion of extractives of the 3 new proposed fibres was obtained from the macadamia shells, which was still up to 57% higher than the reference fibre softwood (Fengel and Wegener, 1983).
6.1.1 Effects of holocellulose and cellulose on the physical properties of castor oil- based polyurethane particleboards and recycled polypropylene composites.
Castor oil-based polyurethane and recycled PP matrices filled with macadamia shells, pine cones and Eucalyptus capsules showed a significant improvement in their physical properties compared with their counterparts filled with radiata pine wood. Amongst the samples manufactured with recycled PP, MS-PP had 36% - 50% lower water absorption and thickness swelling than RPW-PP, followed by EC-PP, which was 22% - 10% lower than RPW-PP. PC-PP had an unexpected 50% higher water absorption than RPW-PP, but a thickness swelling 4% lower than the reference panel, reflecting the dimensional stability of the pine cones.
The water absorption and thickness swelling of the castor oil-based polyurethane particleboards were substantially higher than in the recycled PP-based composites. Similar to the results obtained by the composites with the plastic matrix, MS-CO also had the lowest water absorption and thickness swelling, 75% lower than RPW-CO. Both PC-CO and EC-CO had 65% less water absorption and 33% - 42% lower thickness swelling compared with RPW-CO.
It has been reported that most of the water absorption occurs in the holocellulose of the fibres, particularly in the hemicellulose component and also in the cellulose (Horvath, 2006; Fengel and Wegener, 1983; Pettersen, 1984). Both hemicellulose and cellulose contain high quantities of the hydroxyl groups and have hydrophilic characteristics (Nemli and Colakoglu, 2005; Büyüksarı et al., 2010). All three new proposed fillers had 12% lower holocellulose content than radiata pine wood (Fengel and Wegener, 1983). The cellulose content of macadamia shells was 33% lower than the cellulose content of radiata pine wood and both pine cones and Eucalyptus capsules had 22% less cellulose content than the reference fibre. The lower water absorption ability and thickness swelling of the proposed fibres compared with softwood was expected since the cellulose and holocellulose are lower.
6.1.2 Effects of lignin on the physical properties of castor oil-based polyurethane particleboards and recycled polypropylene composites.
Lignin protects the fibres against moisture due to its hydrophobic characteristics (Pirayesh and Khazaeian, 2012). While it has been reported that macadamia shells have a hydrophobic external hardwood layer to protect the nut inside (Kaupp and Kaupp, 2007), the chemical analysis of the fibres in this research showed a 50% higher level of lignin in macadamia shells compared with softwood (Fengel and Wegener, 1983). Pine cones had a 23% higher lignin content than radiata pine wood, and Eucalyptus capsules presented 18% less lignin than radiata pine wood. The higher quantity of lignin in macadamia shells and pine cones could explain the reduced thickness swelling in both matrices compared with the reference fibres in this research.
6.1.3 Effects of extractives over the physical properties of castor oil-based polyurethane particleboards, and recycled polypropylene composite
Several researchers have observed that extractives protect the fibres against moisture, improving resistance to the water in composites (Pirayesh and Khazaeian, 2012; Nemli and Colakoglu, 2005; Büyüksarı et al., 2010; Kim et al., 2009).
The chemical analysis of the fibres in this study showed that both pine cones and Eucalyptus capsules had an impressive 183% - 491% higher level of extractives than softwood (Pettersen, 1984; Fengel and Wegener, 1983), while macadamia shells had up to 57% more extractives than softwood. Although Eucalyptus capsules had a lower amount of hydrophobic lignin than softwood, it has a very high a compensatory amount of well-known water resistant extractives. The difference in the chemical composition explains the fact that the proposed fillers showed improved physical properties in both matrices, particularly in the thickness swelling criterion.
6.1.4 Effects of extractives on the colour and odour of composites
It has been previously reported that extractives in wood contribute to the colour and odour of the fibres (Horvath, 2006; Fengel and Wegener, 1983). Two unexpected results were observed after preparing the composites filled with pine cones and Eucalyptus capsules.
First, the mixes prepared with recycled PP exhibited a darker colour and a smell of burnt wood. This colour and odour was not observed in the samples containing macadamia shells or pine wood, even though these were manufactured with the same processing variables. As extractives were found to be substantially higher in pine cones and Eucalyptus capsules than in macadamia shells or in softwood, the difference in colour and smell could be attributed to a degradation of the extractives as a result of the high pressing temperature (170 °C).
Second, it is remarkable that all samples manufactured with the castor oil-based polyurethane matrix kept the original aroma of the fibres, particularly the pine cones and the Eucalyptus capsules. The odour of the cones and capsules was quite strong and long lasting. This evidence corresponds with the higher quantity of odour contribution from extractives of pine cones and Eucalyptus capsules.
6.1.5 Effects of chemical content of fibres on the mechanical properties of composites
The effects of the chemicals of the three proposed fillers on the composites with both matrices were compared with the performance of the reference samples. The results of these analyses are given next.
6.1.5.1 Effects of chemical content of fibres on the mechanical properties of recycled PP based composites
Other authors have previously reported that extractives of wood flour have a detrimental effect on the flexural properties of wood-plastic composites (Ayrilmis et al., 2010; Ayrilmis et al., 2009; Büyüksarı et al., 2010). This negative effect is caused during the pressing of the fibres in a plastic matrix over 170°C. Lignin and extractives in the fibres degrade and release CO2 and VOC respectively (Klysov, 2007). If the production process does not consider a venting system, the gases stay inside the mix, forming porosities and voids (Klysov, 2007). Since the samples manufactured with recycled PP were hot plates pressed without a gas extraction system, the high amount of lignin
contained in macadamia shells, and the high amount of extractives from pine cones and eucalyptus capsules degraded with the heat. Therefore MS-PP, PC-PP and EC-PP presented 9% - 14% porosity when compared to the reference sample RPW-PP, which only had 4% porosity. PC-PP had the highest porosity, and also showed higher numbers of voids when compared with the rest of the samples. These voids were shown in the vertical density gradient and in the optical microscopy analysis. The porosity of PC-PP caused a poor encapsulation of the fibres by the plastic and they were exposed to moisture, allowing water absorption and a poor plastic-fibre interface.
Stark (2001), in her study, stated that higher water absorption causes a decrease of tensile strength and flexural properties in wood-plastic composites. This is due to a degradation of the interface between the two materials and a decrease of the elastic modulus of the wood upon moisture absorption. If the MOE of the constituent fibres decreases, the MOE of the composites should be affected (Wolcott, 2003). In this research the highest water absorption of all fibres was attained by PC-PP, with 12%. This higher water absorption of PC-PP coincidently gave the lowest MOE and lowest tensile strength of all the recycled PP based composites. The modulus of elasticity (MOE) obtained by PC-PP was up to 45% lower than the reference sample RPW-PP. Therefore, the higher water absorption levels might have affected the mechanical performance of PC-PP to some extent.
The modulus of rupture (MOR) is sensitive to the ability to transfer stress through the interface between the material components. If that interface is poor the strength will be poor (Wolcott, 2003). In this research, both MS-PP and PC-PP had the highest amount of porosity of all composites, and the lowest MOR which was 47% - 52% lower than the reference samples of RPW-PP. When observing both samples on SEM a poor interface was observed, with a clear differentiation between the plastic and the fibres for MS-PP and clean pull-outs of fibres from the plastic matrix, showing a damaged interface.
Impact strength test results have also been affected by the porosity of the samples, with results 48% lower for MS-PP and PC-PP, and 38% lower for EC-PP than RPW-PP. These results matched with the reported 14%, 12% and 9% of respective porosity of the
composites, confirming the statement that higher porosities and voids reflect in reduced mechanical properties (Klysov, 2007; Dong and Davies, 2012).
6.1.5.2 Effects of the chemical content of fibres on the mechanical properties of castor oil-based polyurethane matrix
Most adhesive-fibre bonding occurs through hydrogen linkage in the hydroxyl groups of the three main components of wood - cellulose, hemicellulose and lignin (Pirayesh and Khazaeian, 2012; Frihart, 2005; Tsoumis, 1991; Güler et al., 2008; Nemli and Colakoglu, 2005; Büyüksarı et al., 2010). Hemicellulose softens under high moisture and temperature, turning out to be the main place where bonding occurs and allowing the interaction of water and hydrogen to link to the adjoining fibres (Frihart, 2005). As hemicellulose is a component of holocellulose, lower holocellulose content means that less bonding will occur with the adhesives, resulting in poor bonding and therefore, impoverished mechanical properties. The preceding statements are confirmed in previous studies in which lower mechanical properties have been reported for particleboards manufactured with the by-products of agricultural crops which have lower holocellulose content than wood (Bektas et al., 2005; Kozlowski and Piotrowski, 1987; Güler et al., 2008; Güler and Ozen, 2004; Çöpür et al., 2007; Nemli and Colakoglu, 2005; Pirayesh and Khazaeian, 2012). In this research, since all new proposed samples had 12% less holocellulose and 22% to 33% lower cellulose content than radiata pine wood; it was reflected in lower flexural properties, particularly in the MOR. Since MOR is sensitive to the ability to transfer stress through the interface, a good interface and improved hydrogen bonding would reflect in a higher MOR (Wolcott, 2003).
It has been reported that polyurethane adhesives are highly compatible with vegetable fibres due to reaction of the hydroxyl groups of the fibres with the isocyanate groups of the polyurethane, and seem to be more effective in the lignin domains (Langenberg et al., 2010; Latere Dwan'Isa et al., 2005; Marcinko et al., 2000; Conner, 1996; Silva et al., 2006; Frihart, 2005). After evaluating the lignin content of the proposed fibres and their mechanical performance, the lignin content did not seem to have influenced the mechanical properties, but it did seem to have affected the internal bond strength to a
degree, at least in the case of macadamia shells and pine cones. Lignin content in radiata pine wood was 27% (Diaz-Vaz et al., 2009), while macadamia shells and pine cones had 50% and 23% higher lignin content, respectively. Eucalyptus capsules presented 18% less lignin content than the reference fibre. The last result contradicts the statement that hydroxyl links produced in lignin improve the bonding of fibres and adhesive, but the two first results confirm it. The results for the internal bond (IB) strength of MS-CO, PC-CO, and EC-CO were up to 170% higher than RPW-CO in the three proposed samples. Therefore, the lignin content might have affected positively the results for the macadamia shells and pine cones, but there are other contributory factors, raised in the following sections.
Effects of extractives on the mechanical properties of particleboards
Several researchers have discussed the adverse effects of extractives on the setting characteristics of adhesives, decreasing the particle-particle bond strength and affecting negatively the mechanical properties of the boards (Moslemi, 1974; Nemli and Colakoglu, 2005; Nemli and Aydin, 2007; Güler et al., 2008; Büyüksarı et al., 2010). The three new proposed fibres contained up to 419% more ethanol-toluene extractives and 347% more hot water soluble extractives than radiata pine wood. The higher amount of extractives in the new proposed fibres coincides with the lower MOR of MS- CO, PC-CO and EC-CO.
6.1.6 Influence of the size, shape, alignment and layers of the fibres on the mechanical properties of the composites
Many researchers have demonstrated that increasing the fibre ratio, arranging a parallel alignment of the particles and placing a three layers particleboard will result in an improved MOE (Kelly, 1977; Heebink and Hann, 1959; Lehman, 1974). Although all fibres used in this study were ground under the same conditions and the final particle size distribution was the same (60% - 69% particles from 2 mm – 0.42 mm), all four fibres exhibited quite different shapes, as seen in the optical microscopy analysis. While the ground macadamia shells presented a low fibre aspect ratio, grainy texture and rounder shapes, and it was possible to see the two phases of particles and resin in the
composites, pine cones seemed to have some slimmer particles. Eucalyptus capsules looked less rounded with a softer texture than macadamia shells, appearing more similar to wood fibres and more homogeneous in both recycled PP and castor oil-based polyurethane matrices. Radiata pine wood fibres had a higher fibre aspect ratio, were narrower than all the previous fibres and seemed much softer when mixed in the PP matrix. The same characteristics were found in the finished panels bonded with castor oil-based adhesive, where MS-CO had a grainy texture, while EC-CO and PC-CO had a smoother texture.
Reference samples RPW-CO and RPW-UF had smoother textures due to the higher flexibility of the fibres bent under the pressure of the press. The difference in shape was noted initially after grinding the materials and later, when mixing the composites. Macadamia shells seemed to be smaller and more compact, bearing a greater resemblance to large sand granules than fibres. It was even necessary to add more fibres and adhesive to the mix, increasing the densities of the MS-CO particleboards. The texture, shape, size, distribution of the fibres and interphase bond of the fibres with the matrix was confirmed after optical microscopy.
Furthermore, the shorter and thicker shape and the multidirectional orientation of the proposed fibres when compared with the parallel aligned and slimmer fibres of radiata pine wood, affected negatively the interconnection of the fibres amongst each other and the adhesive. This reduced fibre-fibre and fibre-adhesive interconnection decreased particularly the bending stress results. These observations agree with the findings of Januario in his research. He used the same particle size of bamboo (0.42 mm to 2.4 mm), bonded with the same castor oil-based polyurethane adhesive used in this research and obtained a similar MOR to RPW-CO of 8.71 MPa. He concluded that particle size could have affected the mechanical performance (Januario, 2006).
6.1.7 Effects of the mechanical properties of fibres on the mechanical properties of the composites
Wolcott (2003) states that the MOE is highly influenced by the properties of the constituent materials. Therefore, it is important that the fibres have the required mechanical properties for the final composites. The only data found in literature on the mechanical properties of the fibres used in this research was on macadamia shells and the reference fibre, radiata pine wood.
The shell of the macadamia is known to be hard and brittle, similar to ceramics (Kaupp and Kaupp, 2007). The MOE of a macadamia shell is within the range 4.2 to 5.2 GPa (Wang et al., 1995), which is significantly lower than the MOE of radiata pine wood of 8.1 GPa (Kretschmann, 2010). The results of the MOE for the samples MS-PP and MS- CO were 1.9 GPa and 0.38 GPa respectively, quite lower than their counterparts RPW- PP and RPW-CO with 2.9 GPa and 0.93 GPa, respectively. No data have been found connected with the MOE of pine cones or Eucalyptus capsules. However, the difference in the MOE of the composites containing macadamia shells and radiata pine wood reflects the better flexural properties of radiata pine wood compared with macadamia shells, pine cones and Eucalyptus capsules.
The tensile strength of macadamia shells is 55 MPa (Wang et al., 1995; Wang and Mai, 1995; Jennings and Macmillan, 1986) and the tensile strength parallel to the fibre of radiata pine wood is of 10.6 MPa (Harris et al., 1991). The IB of MS-CO was 1.5 MPa, when compared to RPW-CO of 1 MPa. It is possible that the higher tensile strength of the filler might have affected the tensile strength of the composite in the case of the samples bonded with castor oil-based polyurethane. The opposite situation occurred with the samples containing recycled PP matrix. The tensile strength of MS-PP (7.5 MPa) was slightly lower than the tensile strength of RPW-PP (9.3 MPa). This lower performance is attributed to the high porosity of the samples, instead of the mechanical properties of the components of the composites.
6.2 RESINS: EFFECTS OVER PHYSICAL AND MECHANICAL PROPERTIES OF COMPOSITES
6.2.1 Effects of the recycled polypropylene matrix on the physical properties of the composites
It was explained above that one of the strengths of wood-plastic composites is their higher resistance to moisture when compared with adhesive based composites (Caufield et al., 2010). In this PhD research the physical properties of the composites manufactured with a recycled PP matrix showed the same characteristics as traditional wood-plastic composites. One exception, however, was the lower performance in water absorption for PC-PP, caused by the porosity in the samples.
6.2.2 Effects of the castor oil-based polyurethane matrix on the physical properties of particleboards
It has been stated by other researchers and commented previously in this thesis that composites bonded with castor oil-based polyurethanes have improved water resistance (Dias and Rocco Lahr, 2004; Silva et al., 2006; Kehl, 2011; Latere Dwan'Isa et al., 2005). This improved water resistance is mostly demonstrated in the thickness swelling of the composites (Januario, 2006; Moura Dias, 2005). Water absorption levels of the proposed fibres bonded with 20% castor oil-based polyurethane were from 69% - 76% lower than with RPW-UF. In contrast, the differences amongst the three reference particleboards manufactured with radiata pine wood were reduced. RPW-CO10 and RPW-CO had 7% and 11% lower water absorption than RPW-UF, respectively. The differences of the thickness swelling for the proposed fillers bonded with castor oil- based polyurethane were from 74% - 92% lower than the sample with RPW-UF. Tests on both samples based on radiata pine wood, RPW-CO10 and RPW-CO, resulted in a 58% and 64% lower thickness swelling than RPW-UF respectively. The results show that independently of the amount of resin, castor oil-based polyurethane offered an improved performance against moisture.
The results of the thickness swelling test on the particleboards were much better for MS-CO than the results obtained by all the samples based in recycled PP. This was an unexpected result since MS-CO was bonded with 20% polyurethane resin, and all recycled polypropylene composites were manufactured with 40% plastic resin. This result clearly shows that it is not so much the amount of fibre that affects thickness swelling as the effect on the type of matrix. The different manufacturing processes of both types of composites might have affected the fibre-resin adhesion, and therefore, their performance. The high pressing temperatures for the recycled polypropylene based composites caused VOC and CO2 emissions due to the degradation of lignin in macadamia shells, and extractives in pine cones and in Eucalyptus capsules (Klysov, 2007; Ayrilmis et al., 2010). Since the thermoplastic matrix was pressed with no venting systems, these emissions stayed inside of the panels producing porosities. These porosities have allowed the moisture to penetrate the fibres and allow water absorption and in consequence, some thickness swelling inside the plastic matrix. Meanwhile, the improved thickness swelling results of the castor oil based polyurethane samples could have a correlation with the better dispersion and expansion of the adhesive in the curing process (Fiorelli et al., 2012), which was set at a much lower temperature than the polypropylene matrix of 85 °C.
6.2.2.1 Effects of the urea formaldehyde matrix on the physical properties of the composites
The lower performance of the UF resin compared with the castor oil-based polyurethane matrix could be explained from a chemical perspective. The linkage of wood and resin is called bond based in celluloid hydrogen bond formation (Patel et al., 2009). These bonds occur by sharing an atom between the abundant common polar compounds of wood and UF adhesive; since water also has hydrogen, affects the hydrogen linkages, weakening the bond (Frihart, 2005).
6.2.3 Effects of type of adhesive on the flexural properties of particleboards
As previously mentioned in the literature review (Frihart, 2005; Frihart and Hunt, 2010; Gilbert Gedeon; Patel et al., 2009; Stoeckel et al., 2013), UF adhesive is characterized
for having low creep and high brittleness, while polyurethanes are more elastic, flexible and ductile than UF. Therefore their utilisation is limited in the structural market. In consequence, particleboards bonded with UF adhesive are expected to have a higher MOE than polyurethane based particleboards. In addition, depending on the formulation of polyurethanes, they can be more or less flexible. As stated by Januario (2006), the polyol gives the elasticity to the polyurethane. Therefore, if the polyurethane has more polyol than MDI isocyanate, the polyurethane becomes more flexible and once the amount of isocyanate is higher the material will be stiffer. In this research, since the amount of polyol used in the polyurethane was twice the amount of isocyanate, the results for the MOE in castor oil based polyurethane particleboards were expected to present an elastic behaviour when compared with the current practice.
All MOE results for the composites bonded with castor oil-based polyurethane adhesive were from 22% - 70% lower than RPW-UF. When comparing only the samples with 10% resin, RPW-CO10 and RPF-UF, the first one had the most significant difference of 70% lower MOE.
Another important factor to consider is the amount of resin used to bond the particleboards. When comparing all samples bonded with castor oil-based polyurethane the higher amount of adhesive seemed to have affected positively the flexural strength. The MOE of RPW-CO10, with 10% resin, was 61% lower than RPW-CO, which had 20% resin. These results agree with Wolcott’s (2003) statement, in which he explains that the MOE is highly influenced by the volume fraction of the composites.
6.2.4 Effects of type of adhesive on the internal bond strength of particleboards
It has been previously presented in this thesis that it is expected for a particleboard to fail in the middle of the thickness when pulled apart from both faces perpendicular to the board surface (Kelly, 1977). In this research, regardless of the fact that MS-CO and EC-CO broke in the hot melt glue line instead of the centre of the thickness, the three of them had a higher IB strength than the reference samples based on radiata pine wood. The reason for this unexpected failure could have been a poorer bond between the steel loading block and the sample, rather than the internal cohesion of the panel (ASTM,
1999). Januario (2006), whom had a similar issue on his research, suggested the failure in the hot melt glue instead of the middle of the thickness could have been caused by the high temperature of application of the hot melt adhesives (180 °C). This high temperature could have degraded the castor oil-based adhesive of the external faces of the particleboards.
All composites bonded with 20% polyurethane resin had a higher IB strength than the reference samples bonded with 10% adhesive. Furthermore, when comparing both reference samples bonded with castor oil-based polyurethane at 10% and 20% ratio, the first one had a 60% lower IB strength than the latter one. Therefore, the castor oil-based polyurethane adhesive demonstrates a higher IB strength when used with 20% ratios in all types of fibres.
The observation that IB strength is improved by castor oil-based polyurethane can be debated after comparing both reference samples bonded with 10% resin RPW-CO10 and RPW-UF. The first one had 20% lower IB strength than the latter one. Nonetheless, this issue is inconclusive due to the considerably lower density of RPW-CO10. Therefore, both results are not comparable. The effects of the density of the composites on the main properties of the composites are further explored in the following section.
6.3 EFFECTS OF DENSITY ON THE PHYSICAL AND MECHANICAL PROPERTIES OF COMPOSITES
It has been reported that higher densities in particleboards increase moisture resistance in the short term (Kelly, 1977). This statement was reflected in the results of the studied composites. The sample that recorded the lowest density amongst the recycled PP based composites was PC-PP with a density of 0.99 g/cm3. This density is 6% lower than the highest density recorded by the recycled PP based composites, EC-PP and RPW-PP, both with densities of 1.05 g/cm3. Reflecting with the lowest density, the poorest performance for water absorption was for PC-PP (7.5 wt. %). The highest thickness swelling performance was for RPW-PP (7.8 wt %), which differs with the previous statement. However, as previously stated, all three proposed fibres, macadamia shells, pine cones and Eucalyptus capsules had a lower thickness swelling when compared
with radiata pine in both matrices. Further research would be able to define the long term moisture resistance of these composites. Composites manufactured with castor oil- based polyurethane displayed similar characteristics to the samples manufactured with the plastic matrix. The sample with the lowest density was the reference sample RPW- CO10 with 0.5 g/cm3, which was 49% lower than the particleboards with the highest density. Both samples MS-CO and EC-CO had a density of 0.99 g/cm3. The particleboard that presented the highest thickness swelling and water absorption of all the samples was RPW-UF, with 49% and 32% respectively. This result does not accord with the lowest density and the result is attributed to the non-water resistance features of the UF adhesive.
Amongst the composites bonded with castor oil-based polyurethane, the sample with the poorest physical performance was RPW-CO10, with 46% water absorption and 13% thickness swelling, according with the lowest density. The sample with the following higher water absorption (43%) and thickness swelling (12%) was RPW-CO, which also had the following lowest density (0.68 g/cm3). MS-CO and PC-CO had the highest density of all samples (0.99 g/cm3), and coincidently MS-CO performed better for water absorption and thickness swelling with 11% and 3% respectively.
The positive influence of increasing board density and the amount of adhesive on mechanical properties has been previously mentioned by other researchers (Maloney, 1977; Lehman, 1974; Kelly, 1977). PC-PP recorded the lowest density (0.99 g/cm3) of all samples based on recycled PP, and also had the poorest MOE, tensile strength, and impact strength of all plastic composites. PC-PP was followed by MS-PP, which consistently had the second lowest density of 1.02 g/cm3. The highest density of the plastic composites was achieved by RPW-PP and EC-PP (1.05 g/cm3), and RPW-PP also gained higher mechanical performance for MOR, MOE, tensile strength, and impact strength.
Amongst all the particleboards bonded with adhesive, the sample with the lowest density was RPW-CO10, with a density of 0.5 kg/cm3. This sample presented the lowest mechanical properties of all the particleboard samples. In contrast with the previous results, the highest MOE and MOR were achieved by RPW-UF which only recorded the
third highest density of all the samples, at 0.82 g/cm3. However, due to the stiffness of the UF resin compared with the polyurethane resin used in this study, the results are not comparable. Amongst the samples bonded with 20% castor oil-based polyurethane, both samples with the highest density were MS-CO and EC-CO with 0.99 g/cm3, followed by PC-CO with 0.9 g/cm3. The lowest density was for RPW-CO with 0.69%.
The results of testing for flexural properties contradicted the theory of higher density affecting positively all mechanical properties, since RPW-CO had the best MOE and MOR and the lowest density. These results are clearly influenced by other factors different form the density, such as fibre ratio and fibre alignment in the matrix. The effects of the morphology and distribution of the fibres over the recycled polypropylene based composites and the castor oil polyurethane based particleboards has been extensively explained in the first section of this chapter (See section 6.1.6 Influence of the size, shape, alignment and layers of the fibres on the mechanical properties of the composites, on page 189)
It has been stated by other researchers (Kelly, 1977) and previously explained in this research, that a vertical gradient improves the flexural properties of particleboards, and also decreases the IB strength due to a lower density in the core. The core of a particleboard is the most difficult part of the particleboard to cure. Therefore, particleboards with higher core densities present higher IB strengths. These statements are reflected in the better results of the IB strength of the new particleboards bonded with castor oil-based polyurethane, MS-CO, PC-CO and EC-CO, in which the effects of the higher and regular densities of the composites throughout their thickness are evident. All samples filled with radiata pine wood presented 43% - 83% lower IB strength than the new particleboards manufactured with macadamia shells, pine cones and eucalyptus capsules. The reference sample RPW-UF had better flexural properties and poorer IB strength than the new proposed samples and coincidently was the only panel that had a vertical gradient when compared with all other particleboards. Neither samples RPW-CO and RPW-CO10 had a vertical gradient. However, since these samples had lower densities than MS-CO, PC-CO and EC-CO, they were expected to present a lower IB strength.
CHAPTER 7: CONCLUSIONS AND RECOMMENDATIONS
This research aimed to assess the feasibility of developing more sustainable options to the standard particleboards used in the built environment from the perspective of holistic industrial ecology. The research was conducted through a combination of literature review and practical laboratory experimentation in order to explore the research questions presented in chapter 1.
7.1 RESEARCH QUESTION
The main research question raised was:
Is it possible to develop more sustainable particleboards, targeting the weakest points of current practice in terms of performance and sustainability?
A clear conclusion is that it is possible to develop more sustainable particleboards using a life cycle perspective than the current practice of material production, applying the principles of industrial ecology. The development of these more sustainable particleboards and composites also enables the following research sub-questions to be answered:
· What are the life-cycle environmental impacts of particleboards that undermine their sustainability?
The life-cycle environmental impacts of standard particleboards were identified through a literature review of life cycle inventories (LCIs) of particleboards. The identified impacts spring from the utilisation of monoculture practices; the carcinogenic petroleum-based urea formaldehyde adhesive used to bind the fibres; the CO2 emissions released to the atmosphere during the wood drying process and resin production; and the high temperatures required for pressing particleboards with UF adhesive. Other features identified were related with the poor performance in high moisture environments of the particleboards. The poor performance in high moisture environments has two causes: first there is the hydrophilic nature of the wood, caused
by the high holocellulose content. And second, UF adhesive breaks down in humid and hot environments.
· Given that the identified life-cycle environmental impacts are related to the origin and the processing of the fibres, and with the origin and toxic properties of the adhesives, are there alternative constituents or processes in Australia that have the potential to reduce or eliminate these impacts and contribute positively to sustainability?
Alternative agricultural and forestry by-products in Australia were identified through a literature review to be used as possible substitutes to wood in the manufacture of wood composites. These were macadamia shells, pine cones and Eucalyptus capsules.
· What would be the criteria for selecting these alternative constituents?
The main criteria for selecting the local forestry and agricultural industry by-products were their availability in Australia, the dryness and cleanliness of the by-products and also their good performance in high moisture environments. Appropriate binders were also identified as useful substitutes, these being recycled polypropylene, guayule rubber and castor oil-based polyurethane adhesive. The criteria for selecting the guayule rubber, recycled polypropylene, and castor oil-based polyurethane resins were to have no formaldehyde, to be processed at less than 190 °C, to be water resistant, and to be based in renewable, recycled or recyclable resources. These criteria were introduced from industrial ecology, which states the importance of using local materials, of turning wastes into resources, of preserving biodiversity, not using toxic materials, of increasing the efficiency of energy used, and achieving a longer life span in high moisture environments than standard particleboards.
· Can these by-products fibres from macadamia nut, pine and Eucalyptus seed production be used to manufacture panel materials?
The new alternative constituents were combined into composites, as seen in the research methods and results of this thesis. After a first visual inspection, the composites based in guayule rubber were not considered further for this research.
The results of the analysis of the samples based on recycled polypropylene and castor oil polyurethane showed that the new fibres and adhesive considered in this study have acceptable properties for use as fillers and present a new opportunity for composite panel applications for the built environment, particularly in high moisture environments. All new samples demonstrated exceptionally good physical properties. Some of these results were up to 92% lower thickness swelling for the new particleboards compared with standard particleboards. Composite materials based in castor oil polyurethane recorded up to 360% greater internal bond strength than traditional particleboards, and formaldehyde emissions were 99% lower than those of standard particleboards. The flexural properties of the new proposed composites were, however, 36% - 68% lower than the current practice but still acceptable for use in non-structural applications. The tensile strength in the recycled polypropylene based composites was down to 37% lower than in the reference sample manufactured with radiata pine wood. A similar behaviour was observed in the impact strength test, in which the new proposed composites presented results 48% lower than the composites based in radiata pine wood and recycled polypropylene.
The overall performance of the composites was affected by both the type of fibres and the type of resin. The chemicals of the fibre fillers were found to be to be completely incidental to the physical in the physical and mechanical performance of the composites. The higher content of lignin and extractives, and lower content of holocellulose in the fibres studied affected positively the physical properties of the particleboards and composites, while extractives affected negatively the mechanical properties in both matrices. The lower amount of holocellulose in the macadamia shells, pine cones and Eucalyptus capsules affected the flexural properties of the particleboards with the castor oil-based adhesive.
The type of resin used has also affected the performance of the adhesive-based composites. Castor oil-based polyurethane improved thickness swelling and internal bond strength when compared with standard UF particleboards, while their flexural properties were found to be poorer due to the higher flexural modulus of the matrix.
The low aspect ratio and non-orientation of the fibres in the matrix as compared with radiata pine wood fibres were found to negatively affect the mechanical performance of the proposed composites when compared with the reference samples based in radiata pine. Similarly, smaller particle size and one layer distribution both negatively affected the flexural properties of all samples when compared with commercial particleboards.
· If the by-product fibres from macadamia nut, pine and Eucalyptus seed production can be used to create panel products, would these impacts be ameliorated and the products be, therefore, more sustainable?
The fibres of the macadamia shells, pine cones and Eucalyptus capsules were found to be suitable for use as fillers in the manufacture of particleboards and composites and were able to ameliorate the previously identified environmental impacts of standard particleboards. The criteria were drawn from industrial ecology framework and are shown in Table 7.1. This table compares the main environmental issues of standard particleboards with the performance of the new proposed panels. The performance criteria used in this PhD research states that in order to consider a material sustainable, it should:
· Use local materials: Standard particleboards use mostly radiata pine wood chips, which can be locally grown or imported. The new proposed composites and particleboards were manufactured with local agricultural or forestry by-products. These by-products were native to Australia, or already grown in Australia.
· Use of wastes as resources and maintain biodiversity: The wood fibre used in standard particleboards is grown in monoculture plantations, and once a tree is cut, only half of each tree is used. All new proposed fillers are by-products from plantations that already exist. Therefore, no more trees are required to manufacture
these composites. The utilisation of these fibres discourages planting more monocultives and promotes the optimization of trees.
· Use of renewable resources and eliminate toxic materials and by-products: Standard particleboards use a non-renewable petroleum based adhesive that consists in carcinogenic formaldehyde. Both alternative resins can ameliorate that burden. Castor oil-based polyurethane adhesive is constituted by 75% renewable resource (castor plant), while recycled PP is recycled. Neither castor oil base polyurethane nor recycled PP is based in formaldehyde; therefore, their formaldehyde emissions are almost inexistent. Aside from the emissions of formaldehyde, standard particleboards also generate VOCs and CO2 emissions during pressing and drying at high temperatures. Therefore the proposed alternative resins should require lower temperatures for pressing, and minimal drying of fibres, in order to avoid CO2 and VOCs emissions. This requirement was fulfilled by castor oil-based polyurethane, since the pressing temperature required was only 80 °C. However, the polypropylene based samples released CO2 and VOCs gases due to the degradation of lignin and extractives from macadamia shells, pine cones and Eucalyptus capsules when pressed at 170 °C.
· Increase the energy efficiency: The wood used in standard particleboards is obtained at moistures contents above 25 wt%, and has to be dried down to 6 wt% for panel manufacturing. This is a highly energy consuming step of the particleboard manufacturing process. While the macadamia shells, pine cones and eucalyptus capsules are dried down to 1.5 wt% moisture content during the previous processing of seed extraction. Therefore no further intensive drying is required to manufacture the panels.
Another issue is that UF adhesive must be pressed at temperatures of 195 °C to cure. Both proposed resins require lower pressing temperatures than the 195 °C. Therefore, less energy is required for pressing.
· Extend useful life of products: Standard particleboards have no resistance to moisture. This issue is caused by two factors: the UF resin and the pine wood fibres.
UF breaks down when exposed to water, releasing formaldehyde, and pine wood has a high amount of cellulose and hemicellulose, which causes the material to absorb water and swell. Therefore, standard particleboards have a short span life in humid environments. All new proposed panels showed improved physical performance when compared with standard particleboards, since both studied matrixes, castor oil-based polyurethane, and recycled PP, are well known hydrophobic resins. The other factor is the high amount of extractives and lignin in fillers, and lower holocellulose than wood. These chemical components of the fibres provided a high resistance to humid environments and offered the potential for extending the useful life of these new composites and particleboards. In addition to comparable mechanical performances to standards particleboards.
· Use recyclable or biodegradable materials: Standard particleboards are non- recyclable since their prime sources cannot be separated. The new proposed panels present the opportunity to be recycled or biodegradable. The composites based in recycled PP can be recycled since the resin is thermoplastic. While castor oil-based polyurethane adhesives could biodegrade with the proper formulation of the polyurethane. Further study might be required to define this issue.
All these issues are presented in detail in Table 7.1.

All the points discussed above confirm that all new proposed composites and particleboards ameliorate the environmental impacts identified for standard particleboards. The only environmental drawback identified in this research was for the composites with the recycled polypropylene matrix. Recommendations for further research are presented next.
7.2. RECOMENDATIONS FOR FURTHER RESEARCH
This research has resulted in several findings. However, there are some recommendations for further testing which could make the study more comprehensive. The following points are some suggestions for further research:
· After the initial selection of composites to undertake this research, guayule rubber was discarded. Nevertheless, the experience of preparing composites with fibres and guayule rubber opened new research possibilities, for instance in the development of
flooring applications. Improved results might be obtained by using smaller particle size and a calender process instead of pressing and drying inside a stove.
· All new proposed composites showed improved physical performance when soaked for 24 hours. Further research could be appropriate to define the long term moisture resistance of both types of composites studied.
· Additional studies of the performance of the new proposed composites would be helpful in order to define their applicability in exterior environments. Some of the properties to analyse could be resistance to UV rays, fungus, insects and biodegradability.
· The composites manufactured with recycled polypropylene filled with pine cones and Eucalyptus capsules presented a darker colour and a burnt odour after pressing when compared with the samples filled with radiata pine wood and macadamia shells. Since the levels of extractives were found to be substantially higher in pine cones and Eucalyptus capsules compared with macadamia shells or in softwood, this result was attributed to a degradation of the extractives. As previously mentioned, extractives degrade at high temperatures, generating porosities in the samples. Similar problems occurred with the macadamia shells due to the high content of lignin. Further studies on thermal properties, temperature of degradation of extractives and lignin and their respective emissions should be undertaken and defined before setting processing parameters. The flexural properties of the composites could be improved by manufacturing particleboards with three layers instead of one.
· A study of the most appropriate particle size for each type of composites and layer is suggested. The manufacture of particleboards with the castor oil-based polyurethane matrix and the new proposed fillers is suggested with higher fibre aspect ratios. Likewise, a study of alternative grinding processes is suggested in order to obtain a higher fibre ratio in the new proposed fibres.
· A study of the surface roughness of the composites is so that the compressibility of the type of fibres can be defined and the final flatness of the different samples pressed under the same processing variables.
· A study to determine the slip resistance of the adhesive based composites is suggested in order to evaluate the possibility of using them in flooring materials.
· The testing of the internal bond strength of the composites bonded with castor oil- based polyurethane was not reliable since the failure occurred in the hot melt glue line instead of the centre of the thickness of the samples. New stronger alternatives of bonding the sample to the die should be explored.
· Other mechanical properties worth exploring are screw extraction, hardness, impact test strength, and machinability, in order to evaluate their possible application in flooring, interior decorative walls and in flat panel furniture.
· If the results of the studies previously suggested are positive, the new proposed composites can be applied in flat panel furniture for high moisture environments such as kitchen and bathroom countertops, drawers, and wall coverings. These composites could also be used in decking, around swimming pools, staircases, outdoor furniture and more structural applications. Composites based in Eucalyptus Globulus capsules and pine cones might be also applicable in environments which will make the most of the aroma: some could be saunas, health centres, beauty shops, nature shops, outdoor equipment shops, or other places in which it is important to bring nature to mind.
· The results of this research have been obtained at small laboratory scale. Since the results were favourable, once the processing variables are defined for the recommended further research, the manufacturing process could be scaled up with production and commercialization by a large manufacturer.
· The low technology required for curing the castor oil-based polyurethane resin, in addition to the local availability of the semi-processed by-products used in the
production of these particleboards, present promising alternatives for the manufacture of these composites in communities nearby the macadamia, pine and Eucalyptus seed industries.
Since urbanization is increasing, demand for wood based panel products and more sustainable alternatives to the current standard particleboard are urgently required. This research has shown that viable alternatives can be produced by applying industrial ecological criteria of using wastes as resources for another product. Therefore, the linear non-surviving nature of the industrial system would be converted to a cyclical, survival design system.
